THE RED PAGE.
Love & Letters.
THE business of Life is to secure the
most intense sensations at the least
physical cost ; and the beauty of Love
is that it can provide the most intense
sensations at a physical profit. For the
normal man, in the fitting season —that is.
Dumas JUs advised the use of love from
twenty years till forty; and abstinence
before and after that epoch. This dogma
allows a quite sufficient margin for
the pursuit of Wisdom: it does not
pay to be wise before forty : much
better to fall in love. “Fall,” of
course, is a misnomer: the Fall of Love,
like the “ Fall of Man,” is really a rise—a
welcome offered to more complex, intenser
Life. Between twenty and forty every man
should have three grand passions at least.
It is well to begin with a brune, climax on
a red-headed girl, and taper off with a
lymphatic blonde. Women need not exercise
such a deliberate choice of Rossetti’s House
of Love : for them any house is a haven,
provided it be well-furnished.
Love-letters, despite the common employ-
ment of them which the quoted examples
attest, have really no place in the House of
Love : they are mere makeshifts and subter-
fuges. They divide themselves, like the
patent medicine illustrations, into “Before”
and “ After”—the man’s coming before and
the woman’s after. In both cases, their
office is to relieve a plethora—much to the
disgust of personified Nature. Personifica-
tions of Nature are to be deprecated ; but,
behind every, pair of fond lovers, whoso will
may see with an imaginative eye the eager,
intent figure of the universal Sairey Gamp,
waiting to plant the baby. And the poor,
deluded fools think that they exist for one
another ! Behind the figure of Nature, if
you are an orthodox religionist, you may see
the figure of the Angel who carries the Soul,
but (as we may imagine) wearily enough, a
slave of the routinier theologian. And if we
prattle on at this rate there will soon be no
space for Love Letters. To the work !
Begin with the Song of Innocence, adoring
in the vestibule of the Temple. Innocence
proses rather prettily (as we remember) —
i Selene,
I do nofknow where you are on the wide bosom
of earth to-night. Why should I wish to know ?
In flesh you may be worlds away; yet in spirit you
are always near. You never have spoken to me;
Ido not know you. Yet to see you was to love
you, and having seen you I love you. I love you
through bitterness and stress, driven onward
before my emotions helplessly as a bird before a
storm. I love you in the stillness of afternoon
when for hours I lie and dream, and I can do
nothing for thinking of you ; I would not move
lest the colored dome of my fancies might shiver
into fragments. I pass along the streets at night,
the dark blue of the sky above, and the coolness
of the night air around ; I think of you, and with
a sudden start the spring of sadness wells up
within me. It is love that brings the sweetness of
S3/dIIOSS.
Yet at all these times I can only think of you ;
but there is One short space when I see you and
my vision stays awhile. It is the hour of sunset
when I watch the blazoned West. . . . You come
to me, you float in at my window in the deepening
twilight, with the dusk in your hair and the moon
in your eyes and the passion of night in your
bosom, you, the moori-goddess, Selene. And as
the night pours out its liquid stillness, with de-
parting twilight I lose 'you, and am left in the
darkness with the memory of a dream. Still there
are times when the trees sway to the rushing air,
and in the tumult of the wind I feel the deepness
of my passion. Its tenderness is of the grace of
twilight, but the grand music of the night alone
can fathom its deepness. It fills me, it quivers in
me and stirs me to the core. And thus it is that
you hold my destiny, my future, between your soft
white hands. ... Do as you will, Selene.
G.C.W.
Here is the amusing male in Platonics—
My Life, my Sunshine, —I am going to try to
make this a “bright letter, because you say it
makes you sad to receive despondent ones. I
have just spent a sweet half-hour reading over all
your letters to me. And such a strain of love runs
through them, that I see your dear, piquant face
in them, and I feel your dear hand in mine.
... Oh ! my dear one, do you realise what
a poor creature you would link yourself to ? You
are so bright, too much the offspring of Nature’s
I merriest mood to ever be satisfied linked to a poor
miserable writer’s wretched life-way.
Oh ! , how well I know how passionately
i love you. I devour every letter you send to me
to see if there is a word less fond, and, if I fancy
it is so, my heart aches. You called me in your
last letter “ My dear Boy ” ; how sweet it looked.
lam not living now. When I know that nothing
I can take you from me, a new life will commence.
I love you so, my darling, and it is such a wonder
how you could ever love me (when all men love
you). You are so above me. An ancient Greek
said, “Know thyself.” A modern Frenchman
said “ Know that thou lovest.” When I first met
you your mind fascinated me. I had never met a
woman who spoke as you did : when we chatted
together, thoughts flew from your eyes like sparks
from an anvil. In one week, from adoration of the
mind it turned to adoration of the heart. May I
speak plainly. I have never felt towards you that
purely physical emotion, which so many mistake
for love, which is so transient and which dies so
easily. I worship you mentally, and from my
heart, and therefore I know that will be with me
all my life. I know you are the only one the
world holds for me, who will feel my thoughts,
Iderstand and participate in my ambitions,
id when I think of you, and of having you with
3 through all the years of my life—blessed years !
to come, my heart thrills (not my body) and my
ain pulsates, and I know that is “Love.” And
loving you, my own Chia—Good night. . . .
AIUXTON.
Then, of course, she left him : wliat true
oman was ever content with Platonics ?
pace for some cries of Passion ! —sliding
om psychical to unprintable physical (and
sver forget that psychical is physical trans-
uted) —
o my dead lover.
My beloved, —It is a year since you left me, and
y heart aches with loneliness. When I knew
m were ill I could not think that death was
sar. You, who were so strong, who had fought
) rough so many dangers, surely must conquer,
our sister took me to your bedside. You did
it know I was there, for delirium held you fast;
ut when I laid my hand on your brow—oh, how
hot it was!—your wild raving ceased and you
looked up with a world of love in your eyes. I
longed tp kiss you, and had I known you were to
die, I would have kissed the fever from your lips
and died with you.
That was our farewell. I heard with a dull un-
belief that you had died suddenly. I saw your
coffin carried out, then fell back into your weeping
sister’s arms. Was your spirit near me then?
Do you know how I have mourned you all through
this long, dark year? You come to me in my
sleep and give me the kiss I long for, and I wish
that I may never wake, so that you may kiss me
always.
WIDOWED BRIDE.
lam not going to marry him. I see now that I
can’t—there is you.
He loved me, and took me in his arms, and
kissed me, but' it was nothing, it passed me by.
llt is as if it never had been, because there is you.
I never felt until he came last night, then my
heart seemed to wake and scream out, “Why,
but there is my love, how can I ever belong to
you Y” I had been letting my life slip by as it
would, not seeing that it was an insult to you, but
now the whole dead world else swooned away in
the darkness of that night, and left you and me
alone. Again your dear dark eyes enfolded me,
devoured me. I heard your voice—the voice with
which a man makes a woman his own—calling me
as you called me that night of our Renunciation,
when we accepted the wisdom of the fool which
has ruled through all time.
But I’m really yours, yours, yours, and every-
thing else that we have said or lived has been a
lie. It is too horrible to be true. Is it possible
that all the ages of man could have evolved us for
that? And all the time we should have to live
and never come together !
We are so young, and we only have this life.
God and man have conspired to cheat you. The
one allowed you to exist glorious as you are, and
made you me, only that the other might hold you
from me with chains of poverty and false morality.
But my love, my love, if you want me I will
come. See, I stretch out my hands to you, my
lips are trembling for your kisses.
I am not brave, your spirit has compelled me.
But now I feel I could faint with fear. You are
cruel as well as tender. Ido not complain, dear,
I know that love is different for a man. But I
I have thrown myself on your mercy. andys.
From Clara to Jack, who is in Melbourne.
My dear love,—l received your letters on Satur-
day. ... I am going to Sydney this week. I
cannot stay here any longer. lam tired of it. I
thought I could work better alone, but I find I
cannot. It seems so hard to have no one to love.
You speak about some woman you saw in Collins-
street. I think, if you loved a woman, Jack, I
would kill her, or kill myself—perhaps it would be
best. You know how jealous I am. I cannot
help it, and when you speak of women it nearly
drives me mad. I think when we meet it will
surely kill me, if not before. Oh, Jack, Ido want
you so badly; worse than you want me. lam
simply dying for you. They are always talking
about you up here. They tell me lam not fit for
anything the day I receive a letter from you, and
lam sure lam not, dear. What do you say about
my coining for a week. I can hear you speak to
me at night-time. I lie awake, thinking of you
for hours, at night-time. I am always thinking
of you, Jack. It seems so long to wait. Do you
think we shall meet again, Jack ? I know you
will think me silly, but I cannot help it —I feel
taking something to-day. I have such hard
work to stop myself. It is the thoughts of you
that prevents me doing it.
Ever your heart-broken
Clara.
Dear Jack, I cannot wait any longer.
From Jack to Clara, in Sydney.
My dear and only love, —I feel so lonely without
you, and my mind is so full of you, that I must
again write; but as you are probably tired . . .
you need not answer this until you get my next
letter. I think of you all the time, my sweet-
heart. Yesterdaly I looked at your letter again,
and I was so affected that I dare not do so any
more ; it is so touching, so living, and so genuine.
I do most sincerely love you, my sweet, my
precious, my beautiful goddess. I adore you.
You called me your god in one of your letters, and
I reply that you are my goddess, my Venus; so
we have both become religious. No one can now
accuse me of being an Atheist,
I think that our separation is an outrage on
Nature, my gay little cannibal. Will you call me
a nasty thing ” when you read this ?
... I do not think that suicide is mad or
cowardly. It is often the act of a brave or
philosophical man. Forgive me for talking like
this, my love, but I am very greatly depressed.
There is' a lump in my throat, and I long, in-
tensely long, for your kisses and loving caresses.
Do not forget me, my loved one, and tell me how
you pass your leisure time. ... It is very cold
at night here. ... I talk to you at night some-
times, Clara, my love. Do you ever hear me ?
although five hundred miles separate us? Oh,
Clara, the circumstances which part us are cruel,
most shamefully cruel. My darling, my darling,
I love you ! I love you !
I kiss you . . . my sweet one, I can see your
beautiful kind face illuminated with tender lan-
guishing love, and I can hear your sweet voice
thrilling with emotion.
My dear, sweet darling love, it is now twelve
(midnight). I cannot sleep because of the thought
of the happiness I shall have when we again meet.
... I will kiss you and caress you. ... Then
we shall care nothing for the cruelty and malice
of others.
With heartfelt affection,
Your devoted lover,
Jack.
Here is “a letter written to an Australian
girl on a visit to Italy by an Italian profes-
sor of music, who was informed by girl’s
father, for reasons of his own, that his
daughter was already affianced in Aus-
tralia ”
Gevio, 11 September , hours 16 — 1896.
Adored my [say] Nita, —Whilst thou joyous
and in cheerful company go at Menzouis, I melan-
choly and lonely thee write this little words.
After two days of indescribable happiness why
thee wos olways near here are me to-day cast in
the more profound sorrow. Thou last night made
known to thy father my love towards thee and he
this morning to me said that thou art already
promised spouse in Australia. Easily canst thou
to imagine what cruel blow was it to my heart
which already very much this notice. Is properly
truth, Nita ? But, therefore, thou wast not sincere
with me. Why to my repeated demands always
saidst that wast free and that didst not love some-
body? Why till by the first turn thatmy fingercairie
near to tpe thy, that thee did the first declaration
of love thou to me not saidst, “ Professor, in vain
love me, I am already promised to a other ? Then
I should have set out immediately from Cevio for
to suffocate till by the principle of my passion.
But at present is impossible. The destiny was
willing that we might meet. I thee saw pretty
and gentle from thy fascinating look knew the
goodness of thy soul and became enamoured fool-
ishly of thee, and at present thee I love as nobody
has love ever ! I thee love of a love which the
word human can not to express. And thou wilt
be spouse of a other ! For my heart is frightful
and horrible this thought—to me deprive the life.
I weep, yes, Nita, weep. How much I suffer !
Should be willing yet to thee to say somebody
things (and shall be the lasts), but I wait before a
thy answer. Concede, adored Nita, that my
ardent kiss may depose on thy splendid eyes and
that my tears may moisten thy heart. Have thou
pity of me, Nita, which knelt hard by thy foot
thee adore. Thee kiss thy very much unhappy
G-oisefpe. ...
And here a schoolmaster’s letter, consider-
ing things too curiously—and, O horror !
advising. “All advice has an of
blame,’’and should be most carefully avoided,
says the wise Balzac. Besides, what need
of advice to Woman the imitative?—and
after Meredith has written The Egoist—
Procrustes in love. However, he seems to
have made amends—Le Medecin Malgr6
Lui—
Dearest, —You are grieved. I feared you would
be, yet how could I —we —help it ? I felt what
your letter would say before I opened it. How
can I comfort you ? By first telling you of my joy
—see how utterly I know you—if only you could
realise it, how perfect your happiness would be !
I did not go home last night after you left me,
but walked miles round to all our old places—the
hill where your hair came down (our first ride
together)—the waterhole where the old tramp
“ G-od-blest ” us—the maiden-hair creek where we
sat so long, and you told me I ought to lace my
boots right up to the top (I liked that—it seemed
an assertion of ownership)—the sweet-briar bushes
—you remember ? and back again to our gates of
Paradise, where I lay with you still in my
heart till the dawn, when I found a few
petals of the rose you wore—they were
so sweet. It is wonderful. A strange exhilara-
tion keeps passing in waves through me—
dear old mother asked me at breakfast what I was
smiling at. ’lhCn your letter came. Darling,
seriously, what a delightful little bundle of im-
pulse you are! You felt in your beautiful inno-
cent way the great reaction that follows all great
emotion, and instantly expressed it in eight "pages.
Thank you for the tenderness that makes bad
logic lovely. You wrote, because you had to; I
walked for the same reason—you feel better al-
ready ? But remember, you have sworn fealty to
the, King of Love when he rests as well as
when he rules—yet you allowed those impish
little thoughts of priest-law and social
tyranny to scamper over him during what
'should have been his sweetest sleep ! But just
wait till he wakes ! Come, let us wake him —
Darling—Flower—you feel me murmuring in your
heart—stealing your honey. I hold your hand in
mine—now. Again I press the ring down your
finger till it hurts—you flinch under the glad pain
—your lips are mine. Why, the King was never
asleep at all! It was you who dreamed the imps
were teasing him—you wake to find it is he who
guards you—now, and for ever.
Darling, you do not misunderstand my happi-
ness that overflows ? I know how keenly you felt
all you wrote, but I want you to see the vanishing
littleness of everything beside ourselves—our final
triumph—our perfect freedom—“We are made
with one nature ” —the petty imp-world has passed
in the fire of our love, and in its place shines a
star, whose hemispheres are you and me—and
what do we care for the man in the moon ! Dar-
ling, does not this bubbling love and nonsense
answer your dear foolish doubts about my future ?
Leave all other troubles in my heart—notin yours
—till we meet to-night—for you will come. I
think of your eyes and the words will sing !
Fringed violets dance
In the glamour of Spring ;
Where dew-drops glance
Fringed violets dance
At Love’s advance ;
All glistenihg,
Fringed violets dance
In the glamour of spring.
N.B.—Come as soon as it is dark, as the moon
rises at eleven—hang the man in the moon !
Yours, •

. . . Sometimes I feel as if 1 were all alone on
Earth, surrounded by an ever-darkening gloom,
and a terrifying feeling of emptiness overcomes
me. Then a merciful God allows tears to flow
from my eyes and I am soothed. . . . etc.—
N.S.—
Darling, you did not come to see me when I
wrote to tell you of what has happened. I know
that in your position you must be careful of scan-
dal, and I did not expect you to come yet awhile,
and I know you dislike letter-writing, but, dear
love, if you would only write me just one little
letter, if it is only a few lines ! I agreed with you
one time that a marriage between us two would
be foolish, and that marriage in a general way was
a mistake. What has happened has not altered
this, but sometimes since the poor baby has come
1 wish we could have been married. 1 don’t mean
to complain, dear ; I know it has all been my own
fault, but I loved you so dearly ! I knew people
would look down on me and the baby, and I
thought I would not mind it, but I suppose I
must still be weak and nervous, for I, feel some-
times that I cannot bear the things they say and
the way they look at us. If I could only feel that
you did not despise me I don’t think I would mind
so much. The boy is a fine fellow, and is so much
like you that I wonder people need to put the
question to me as to who his father is.. . . etc. —
E. ; First Time ; A.C.H.—
There was a wild hope in my breast that when I
told you of my going you would take me in your
arm 3 and say the words I would give my soul to
hear. So I told you. You did not speak for
some minutes ; then you said, I ’ll miss you so
much.” That was all. God ! I almost laughed—
then I caught my breath while the world reeled
and a hot mist swam before me . . . etc.
8.8., Echo, E.R., Jill, E.W.—
My Gear One,—ln thy presence I can be
eloquent, though silent; but parted, my halting
words can not interpret my ardent thoughts—this
cold, mechanical action is too slow, too forma l . .
etc.
Rivulet —
Calmly and coldly we could discuss Goethe’s
“ Elective affinity” as a universal sympathy that
pulsated throughout all things to infinity. To
you, Heine’s “Love of the Pine and the Palm ”
was sublime in contrast to the wailings of the
woman who repined : “ George used to fetch me
violets once, now he fetches me beef steak, but no
violets,”.. . . etc. —
Heinrich—
. . . Last night I was dreaming of you, dearest.
We were in Centennial Park, sitting on “our
bank ” near the pond where the ducks are. You
are throwing biscuits to them. The old drake
with the beautiful long wings got most • • • etc.
Y.F.E. ; Florrie ; O. ; P.C.C. ; D,W. ;
F. ; J.B.—
O, lover of mine, why is a man’s love always so
earthy ?
H.E.—
God knows I do not want you. to feel as lonely
as I, and yet I cannot play the hypocrite and hope
you are happy, I want to feel that I am missed
as much as you, and that your days are just as
leaden as mine.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Squatteroo.
A long-legged, lanky man was he, with grizzly
beard and grey,
A stranger at the shanty—for he came from far
away;
And we noticed that his harness was the product
of the ’roo,
And his boots and—strike me fat!
There was hair upon his hat!
And he said : “Oi am a grazin’ man —the only
Squattheroo ! ”
So we took him into “ Mother’s,” and we wet him
at the bar,
And he spoke about his cattle—how he branded
them with tar,
And mustered by the thousand out upon the lone
Paroo.
They were not the common kine,
Neither were they grunting swine—
“ Faith ! They only go on two legs yit,” observed
the Squatteroo.
He said he’d learnt how garden-stuff was once the
rankest weeds,
How men evolved from puny stock the very best
of breeds;
And then he crossed the Darling to domesticate
the ’roo.
He would choose his paramours,
Make him locomote on fours —
Then he’d be a source of profit to the only Squat-
teroo !
He would make of him a packer, he would teach
him not to hop,
And when he had induced the brute his former
end to drop,
He would send the horizontal fact some day to
Sydney Zoo.
“ Oi am shure compulsive use
Of the forelegs will produce
What ye’d call an abnormality,” averred the
Squatteroo.
“ There’s no dacent occupation for a baste thot
stands on ind !
But, he’ll be a perfect jewel whin his little paws
descind I
And he’ll thrott around theshanty loikeO’Doolan’s
pig—Hooroo!
He’ll be yoked in pairs like oxen,
He’ll forgit his thricks o’ boxin’,
An’ we’ll thrain him for the races,” said the back-
block Squatteroo.
Then he swung into his saddle, said he must be
making tracks;
No, he didn’t want no stockmen, he employed a
hundred blacks,
Who were out a-branding joeys now upon the lone
Paroo.
By-and-by we’d see them riding
On ’roo-back to Cobar siding,
A-driving kangaroos for him —that long-legged
Squatteroo!
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
The Cockies’ Men.
There’s a life that no one covets,
And where luxuries are few,
And the men that learn to love its
Sordid ways go silly, too:
Where kindness isn’t flowing
And they’ve hearts as hard as stone,
And you’ve got to take what’s going,
For the unions aren’t known.
Where you do odd jobs and potter
Round o’ nights till after ten,
Then to doss you plod (or totter) —
That’s the life of cockies’ men.
Then they put you on a-ploughing
When the weather’s wet and cold—
Fifteen bob a week, “ allowing
You keep going,” so you’re told.
And you go from kookaburra —
Keep the horses moving, too—
Till in gloomy night the furrow
Is a-fading from your view.
Stable horses, curse and feed ’em,
Also currycomb ’em ; then
Be at hand at night to heed ’em—
That’s the lot of cockies’ men.
Then you tramp at harvest stooking
Where the sheaves are very few,
(And the blessed cocky’s looking
For the most that you can do);
Till you hear the knock-off coo-ee
Floatin’ down the smoky gray,
And you’re tired as humping “ bluey ”
Over forty miles a day ;
And the cook’s got something sodden
Waiting on the table then,
Something shapeless, damp, down-trodden,
For the tea of cockies’ men.
You can hear the cocky cursing
At the cattle in the hay,
Or the pigs he is dispersin’
Long before the break of day,
And you turn again preparing
For another wink or two —
But someone’s tilrkey-scaring (
And it’s “ gobble gobble ” —“ Shoo !”
And the boss commences drumming
On the kerosene-tin then—
“ Oh, condemn yer soul, I’m coming !”
And they rise—these cockies’ men.
V.

Brisbane.
Ruberoid Building at Agricultural Show.
Unlike ordinary roofings P & B Ruberoid Roofing is absolutely airtight,
consequently it requires special ventilation. If you should raise the eaves
of a Ruberoid roof higher than the ridges, it would still be watertight, no
matter how heavy the rainfall; not so with other roofs, which would leak
like a sieve. It is through these holes in ordinary roofs that a great deal
of ventilation comes, which is not possible with roofs of P & B Ruberoid.
We, therefore, strongly recommend special provision for ample ventilation
at the ridges as illustrated.
THE RIGHT WAY.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
IVestralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to Tub Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maoleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the . contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceed-
ing two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are speci-
ally acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Neivspaper
clippings. All communications will be regarded as
strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To A mateurs in Made and White.
Anyone supplying to Tub Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which

SATURDAY, MARCH 8, 1902.
The Menace of Militarism.
General Hutton has lust very little time in
announcing the ends towards which his
administration of the military affairs of Aus-
tralia is to be directed. There are to be,
says the Commandant, two forces, one ex-
clusively for home defence ; the other,
chiefly of mounted men, to be raised with a
view to service in any part of the world.
This scheme, it is announced, has the cordial
approval of Premier Barton ! The meaning
of it is that Australian citizens are to be
systematically trained for service abroad
“to extend the Empire,” or to protect it
from attack ; that we are to raise an
army liable to be used against Hindustan
border tribes, against Irish agrarians,
or discontented Canadians, as well as
against Boers in South Africa ; or, under
Japanese control, against some European
Power in Asia. The idea involves an abso-
lute departure from all past traditions of
Australian politics ; sets up for the first time
a standing army to be used, not for
purposes of defence but for aggression, not
in our own cause but in the cause of others,
and perhaps under conditions absolutely
repugnant to our sentiments. Mr. Barton
is not so fat-witted that he cannot see the
logical consequences of the Commandant’s
proposals, and of the official approval given
to them by him. And Australians cannot/
plead in the future—without the gravest?*
reflection on their mental capacity—that
they endorsed Mr. Barton’s approval of
General Hutton’s plan because they did not
know with what it was loaded.
* * ’ • *
It is not to the blame of General Hutton
that he should suggest that Australians be
reduced to the grade of Sepoys and forced
to provide servile soldiers for England’s use.
is an English officer; English in ideas, in
training, in ambition, and naturally regarding
Australian interests as absolutely subordi-
nate to those of England. There i good
fighting material here. England, engaged in
the work of Empire-making without having
among her now mostly city population (her
“little street-bred people”) sufficient of the
kind of material that is required in that
work, needs servile soldiers—servile white
soldiers more particularly. Her ambition
is just now that of the Chamberlain
type of man—to stay at home and keep
shop whilst somebody else extends
trade abroad with a gun. General Hutton,
as an Englishman, with the prospects of an
English peerage, and the conviction that, for
England’s sake, all things must be done, is
naturally anxious to play England’s game.
There is no dishonor in his attitude, deep as
is the dishonor which attaches itself to those
representatives of the Australian people who
are prepared, from mere laziness or from a
spirit of truckling subserviency to the Cham-
berlain gang, to fall in with his views. Nor
will the Australian people themselves be un-
deserving of censure if they fail to oppose,
by all means possible, this proposal from the
outset. Their apathy has already allowed
several dangerous inroads on Liberty. If
they permit to have put on them the fetters
of a standing army, raised for England’s
service, led by England's officers, a fighting
caste whose hopes of advancement and
whose very living will depend on Eng-
land and her wars, when the inevitable
consequences come on them they will be
objects of contempt rather than of pity. The
Australian Federal Constitution was not
contrived for the direction of a military
power. It omits all those safeguards against
the army being made the tool of irresponsible
ambition which are provided in England. Just
now most people would laugh at the idea of
an Australian Parliament being dissolved and
an Australian Constitution overthrown by a
military leader. But, once establish a stand-
ing army, whose main purpose of life will
be to participate in wars abroad, whose
sentiments and sympathies will from the
first be detached from its own country,
and whose ambitions will centre on the
glory and loot of foreign military service ;
and a military despotism comes at once
within the range of practical politics.
• * * *
The Australian defence policy should be
the exact antithesis of that propounded by
General Hutton, if Australian nationalism
is to be conserved. There should be prac-
tically no “standing army”—only such
regulars as the exigencies of artillery and
fortress work require ; but as many citizens
as possible should be encouraged to bear
arms, so that the “army” may be as demo-
cratic in spirit and as representative of the
great body of the people as possible. Foreign
service and all kinds of swashbuckling should
be severely discountenanced. Such a force
would be a bulwark against invasion in war,
and not a menace to liberty in peace.
General Hutton’s army would be no bulwark
to us, whatever it might be for England, and
its threat to Freedom would be constant.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Ghostly March.
(Over 20,000 British soldiers have now died from
wounds or disease during the S’African war).
Five abreast they tramp
(Hold your shuddering breath,
Though your brow be damp,
Silent, look on Death ) —
On, through London’s gates,
To the Abbey door,
Twenty thousand fates,
Harvest of the war.
Home, from southern seas,
Ghosts in ghostly ships—
Requiems in the breeze,
Moaning life’s eclipse;
Requiems in the waves,
Nature touched with pain—
Home from Afric graves
Marching once again.
From the Docks they start,
Through the cheerless East;
Past Aldgate next—past brokers’ mart—
Past home of civic feast.
Rands may rise anew,
Mayoral wine be red—
What is that to you,
Twenty thousand dead ?
What is’t now to you
How the ending came—
Bullet from the blue
Or a blunderer’s shame ?
Food or clothing scant,
Fever’s dreadful glow ?
Made HER mourning, and
Made the children’s wo8.
Past St. Paul’s your way ye take
(Lo ! its muffled bell) —
Fleet-street next, whose fatal power
Fateful war shall tell ;
On to Commons’ splendid pile,
Abbey’s open door;
Ye throng awhile the storied aisle,
Then vanish evermore.
Some have yet to start
When some have reached the Fane —
Lines two yards apart,
Ghostly six-mile chain.
Six miles length of woe,
Million looks distressed
Victims of Death’s overthrow
Marching five abreast.
London n. Frank Colebrook.
Philp’s Polly.
The dreary Robert Philp, State Premier of
Queensland, appeared before a meeting at Rock-
hampton (Q.) recently to defend the thing he calls
his financial policy, but which looks more like a
child’s first arithmetic blown up by dynamite and
coming down again promiscuously, The case
against Philp, in a financial sense, is large enough
to call for a whole lot of defence, and may be sum-
marised thus:
(1) Philp has been Premier for two years. During these
two years the Queensland revenue boomed up so
gloriously as to break all past records. No previous
Treasurer had anything like such a revenue at command.
Philp had, in his ivorst year, £153,000 more to spend than
Dickson had in 1899 ; £559,000 more than Bviinbs had in
1898 ; and £714,000 more than Nelson had in 1897. It
rained glorious good luck on Philp as it never did on any
other Queensland Treasurer.
(2) And whereas Nelson, Byrnes and Dickson made
ends meet, and had an average surplus, ever since 1894,
of £71,000 a year with which to reduce the old deficit,
Philp, with an enormously larger revenue has had an
average shortage of £240,000 a year.
(3) And the cause of this huge disaster is that, while
NELSOn, in 1895, paid his way with an expenditure of
£3,308,000, Philp, in 1901, got through £4,855,000, and
yet there is hardly anything to show for this incredible
growth of expenditure.
(4) And the fair inference is that there is either, in
Queensland, the most utter incapacity on record, or that
there is a fixed and deliberate intention of running the
State into bankruptcy with a view to a suspension of the
Constitution.at the demand of the public creditors, and a
reversion to the Crown Colony system ; or that there is in
view some other purpose that can’t bear inspection.
This was the case which Premier Philp had to
answer, and his answer was just this :
He defied anyone to say that the money was misap-
plied. The endowment to local authorities was £43,000
more than in 1898 ; the interest was£9o,ooo, representing
money borrowed for public works. The next item was
£153,000 spent on the South African contingents.
(Cheers.) He was quite satisfied that a large majority of
the people of the State were willing for that money to be
spent—(applause)—but had the Labor party their way
this money would not have been so used. (Applause.)
The census cost £14,000, and the Government Printing
Office £15,000; but less would be spent next year.
Charity took £23,000, and bubonic plague £22,000, which
the Government were justified in spending in endeavor-
ing to wipe the plague out. (Applause.) Public works
took £48,000 more ; but he could assure them that much
less would be spent this year. (Applause.) Public
instruction represented £63,000 extra. Did anyone ob-
ject to the extra amount? (“No, no.”) It was quite
the other way. From all parts of the State came demands
for new schools, and the teachers thought they
ought to get more in the way of remuneration.
Surveys took t'20,000 more. The Government were
trying to open up the colony to settlement in
every part, and extra money must be spent for the pur-
pose. Agriculture represented £16,000 extra, and in this
connection everyone recognised that jve must make more
endeavors to settle an agricultural population in the
State. A sum of £372,000 more was spent on railways.
In the working of the latter there had been a loss on the
various systems. The railways had brought in more
revenue. Why had they gone back ? It was because of
the biggest drought ever experienced. (Applause.) No
place had suffered so much. The items quoted amounted
to nearly £925,000. Could anyone point to extravagance
in them ?
Philp’s defence against the charge of being the
most extravagant, incompetent, and muddled
Premier in Australia—a man who has, in propor-
tion to the population of his country, the biggest
revenue save one in the world, and can’t anything
like make ends meet at that—is to enumerate the
different departments in which the principal
mismanagements, or the principal political steals,
occurred. Supposing Dickson, convicted the
other day of a huge shortage in the funds of
Melbourne University, had professed to clear
his character by enumerating the horses that the
missing money was lost upon, he would have stood
on very much the same intellectual plane as
Philp. At the best, Philp’s plea is that the
huge expenditure is unavoidable—or at least that
he can’t ayoid it. Therefore while he remains in
office it will continue, and by Philp’s own show-
ing either Philp must go out or Queensland must
go to immortal smash.
The “Herald’s” Unwonted Excitement.
Last Friday the Sydney Morning Herald, in
its leading columns, asked William Lyne the
following questions in quite an excited tone of
voice. The old lady of Hunter-street was ob-
viously flustered, and had her bonnet wildly
cocked over her eye as she spoke :
How then can he (Lyne) say truthfully that “ as soon
as the Dibbs duties were taken off by Mr. Reid i 1895
with one spring prices went up to what they h..d been
before”? The implication here that prices were lower
under the Dibbs tariff than under the Reid tariff, or
under Freetrade before Mr. Dibbs’ Government was in
office, is one that requires to be supported by illustra-
tions. ... It is simply impossible that under protec-
tionist duties any people can have commodities as
cheaply as under Freetrade. Some time ago it was
pointed out by our correspondents that the cost
of living in France and Germany was much greater
than in England because of the protectionist duties in
the one case and the free ports in the other. . . .
If William Lyne “can, with his family, live £lO9 cheaper
in Victoria than in New South Wale's,” it certainly will
not be under equal conditions of comfort. The case of
the larger number of Savings Banks depositors in Vic-
toria, with relatively smaller amounts than in New South
Wales, proves to the hilt that the class of people who
deposit such moneys had smaller means in the Protec-
tionist State than the same class in what, at the time,
was the Freetrade State. Surely William Lyne can see
that that answers his own fallacy.
The Bulletin has great pleasure in supplying
some, at least, of the information required. It
has done so before, and quoted its authority,
as the Herald is well aware, though that
anti-Australian paper, as a servile agent of
the German and French and American and
English manufacturer, and of their local agents,
is hardly likely to admit the fact. Cogiilan,
the N.S.W. State Statistician, publishes, on
pages 523 to 527 of his “Wealth and Progress of
N.S.W.” for 1898-9 the average retail prices in
Sydney of 19 principal articles of food over a long
series of years. Comparing the Protectionist
year 1892, under Dibbs’ tariff, with the allegedly
Freetrade year 1891, under the Parkes-M‘Millan
tariff, he shows that in eight cases the prices
remained the same ; in two cases they increased a
very little under Protection ; in nine cases they
dropped under Protection. Then comparing 1895
(the last year of the Protective Dibbs tariff) with
1896 (the first year of the Freetrade Reid tariff)
the same table shows than in 14 cases prices
remained the same; in two cases prices in-
creased under Freetrade ; and in three cases
they diminished, but in two of these three
cases the prices went up again in 1897, so that
by the second year of Freetrade prices were
very distinctly higher than under Protection. That
gives a very considerable balance in favor of Pro-
tection. As regards Lyne’s statement that he
can live more cheaply in a Protectionist State,
Coghlan furnishes good evidence in support of
that also. In his article on the “ Cost of Living ”
(“Seven Colonies of Australasia” for 1899-1900,
pages 412 to 421), he declares that the N.S.W. in-
habitant, in these Freetrade days, got less food
than the Australasian average, and paid more for
it. These are his figures :
Pounds of food
annually per
inhabitant. Cost.

The result is that the inhabitant of Freetrade

than the average for Australasia, and paid nearly
£1 more for it, so that in both respects the one
Freetrade or semi-Freetrade State made the worst
probably^'nows a great deal more about statistics
than the S.M. Herald does, shows how Free-

compare in the same matter :

The food includes all kinds of grain (bread, por-
ridge, &c.), all kinds of meat, sugar, butter,
cheese, potatoes, salt, tea, and coffee. Coghlan’s
calculations are based on the investigations of
Mulhall, the greatest statistician in England
and a staunch Freetrader, and they show the
Englishman to be worse fed, on the average, than
the Austrian, German, or Frenchman, and at a
much greater price. And in a country like
Britain, where, according to the statistician,
Mulhall, “nearly 80 per cent, of the total
wealth is held by per cent, of the adult
population,” this is only what might fairly
be expected. But it is in its poor little struggle
to demonstrate that the Victorian worker, who
, has £7 16s. per inhabitant in the savings bank,
and £34 Os. 7d. per inhabitant in all his banks,
must therefore be worse off than the N.S.W.
worker, who has only £7 Bs. sd. per head in the
savings bank, and £3l 9s. lid. in all banks, that
the importers’ organ falls in most badly. And
the superior tone of pity for a weaker brother
with which the silly penn’orth propounds its
elaborate ignorance adds an extra frill and decora-
tion to the whole business, and rounds it off with
two long ears and a bray, in a manner of speaking.

Wanted, a Plague Scare !
Acting on the principle that the best treatment
for a disease is to cover it over and hide it some-
how, the daily press of Sydney is doing its utmost
to minimise the importance of the 1902 plague
outbreak. But both the business men who make
demands for silence, and the newspapers which
accede to those demands are allowing their
timidity to overcome their common-sense. The
position facing Sydney in 1902 is that a plague
outbreak is imminent, which may prove far
more serious than that of 1900 ; and that the
proper steps will be taken only if the terrors
of the situation are properly appreciated. This
year a healthy rat inoculated with the virus from
one of the plague cases is killed in half the time
taken by the poison in 1900, therefore the possi-
bility that the 1902 sample of bubonic disease is
worse than its predecessor can’t be overlooked.
The Centres of contagion are not out-of-the-way
wharves and distant suburbs as in 1900, but busi-
ness blocks in the very heart of the city, the
centres of common resort, where are situated some
of the biggest hotels and some of the chief-theatres.
The opportunities for the dissemination of the
disease are therefore much greater than in 1900.
With a disease of possibly greater virulence, much
more centrally situated, and with, too, a far
greater degree of carelessness on the part of the
citizens as to infection, it is obvious that in March
and April, the plague months, a great—perhaps a
very great—number of cases may be reasonably
expected. In 1900 it was not till the beginning of
May that the disease reached its worst. There is
nothing to be gained by silence. It is far better
to face a present loss than to blunder blindly into
an absolute calamity. During the 1900 outbreak,
Attorney-General Wise threatened to criminally
prosecute property-owners guilty of gross dis-
regard of decency and sanitation. There were no
prosecutions, and the same threat, now revived in
1902, does not frighten anyone. In 1900 it was
pointed out that only a small percentage —prac-
tically an insignificant percentage—of the premises
in the City of Sydney proper were effectively
connected with the sewerage system. The great
majority were attached in such a way as to facilitate
free circulation of sewer rats, sewer gas, and other
abominations in the interiors of houses. Many
sewerage pipes, in addition, allowed some of the
sewerage matter to percolate through floorings, and
to store itself permanently between walls and ceil-
ings. Prompt legislation was promised, at the
time of the exposure, to deal with the evil by
giving the Water and Sewerage Board the power
(which it then lacked) of forcing these filthy abuses
to be remedied. The legislation was* however,
never passed. Parliament found it necessary to
adjourn in a hurry for the Great Commonwealth
Drunk, and a little matter of public health was not
allowed to delay the rush for the banquets and the
titles. Now prompt legislation is proposed again
on this subject (only the Coronation Drunk will
be in the road); and it will probably continue to
be promised, and, like other sanitary reforms, only
promised, until there is a really effective and
continued plague scare. And when the legis-
lation arrives it probably won’t be en-
forced, whereby the public regret at its late-
ness of arrival may be lessened somewhat.
Sydney has already learned that the places
which were cleaned in 1900 are mostly foul again;
some of the fever dens which were pulled down
then have been rebuilt on the old lines ; the sani-
tary laws, the sewerage system, the want of a
decent Building Act, the divided control of the
metropolis among 50 or 60 impotent and wrang-
ling authorities—all these are just where they
were_ in 1900. Everything, in fact, is where it
was in 1900 except the scandals connected with
the £4,000,000 Darling Harbor loan.
The Sydney Water Supply Trouble.
What The Bulletin pointed out some time ago
is at last published by Sydney daily papers—that
the water-supply of Sydney is radically defective
and is administered by a grievously-incompetent
Board. It will require about £500,000 to put the
system into decent working order, and there i3 a
grave danger that, if the drought lasts, the
N.S.W. metropolis will suffer from a water-
famine. It is one of the most hopeless facts about
N.S.W. public life that public officials dread
nothing more than publicity, and that in their
hush-it-up efforts the press—influenced by adver-
tisements, by the hope of billets, or by social
influence—is always willing to aid them. Thus,
the public seldom learns of any scandal until
it is too late to apply a prompt remedy. If
Sydney really strikes a grievous famine, then
its people can think bitterly of the Prospect
Chinese gardens irrigated by the leakages from
the water-supply—of the millions of gallons of
water thus wasted in the past. At the bottom of
the whole trouble there is, of course, the financial
difficulty—that financial difficulty which is at the
bottom of everything in Australia. The Jacob
Garrard department doesn’t levy sufficient rates
to keep itself solvent. It is many years since it
paid its way. In 1899, which was an unusually
good year, the Water and Sewerage Board had a
deficit of nearly £4OOO, allowing absolutely nothing


for depreciation of the works. In 1900 the deficit
was nearly £14,000, again without including any
allowance for depreciation. As for any mar-
gin to allow of paying off the debt, and
systematically writing down the value of the
works, and for extending the works and keeping
them up to date, that was never dreamt of. So to
avoid a little extra taxation the water-supply
system has been allowed to fall into decay. The
time will come when, by reason of this decay, the
leaky open ditch which brings in Sydney’s water
will fall to pieces and have to be written off as a
bad debt, and the bad debt and the water, famine
will strike Sydney simultaneously. Now it is pro-
posed to spend a large sum—perhaps £500,000 —on
extensions. The money will be borrowed, of
course, and the interest will make the bankrupt
Jake Garrard department still more bankrupt.
And if the rates are raised they will probably be
raised just enough to meet, or partly meet, the
interest, and the old process of letting things
moulder to save a little expense will still continue.
The habit of letting things moulder to keep down
immediate taxation is at the bottom of almost
every trouble in this country.

M'Millan’s New Protected Industries.
The Bulletin is again pleased to be able to put
on record the opinion of William M‘Millan,
M.H.R., and sub-leader of the Freetrade party,
that Protection doesn’t necessarily raise prices
after all. The extraneous M‘Millan, in his place
in the House of Representatives, has long felt
called upon to denounce Australian industries, and
cry out at the top of his voice on behalf of English,
German and Dutch manufacturers, and has
declared for many years that Protection raises
prices to a ruinous extent, and that the protected
manufacturer (he is one himself now) is a vampire
of the deepest dye. But M'Millan’s people
(M‘Arthur and Cos.) have had to become manu-
facturers, as well as importers, under the Federal
Protectionist tariff, and they have to reluctantly
admit, in a business way, that they are not
putting the duties on to their prices, that
their charges are as low as, or even lower than,
before the duties were put on, that their
locally-manufactured shirt, built under heavy
Protection, is as cheap and as good as the old
imported shirt, and that they aren’t a vampire.
In fact, the admission is torn with difficulty from
M‘Millan’ s firm that, if the public buys its pro-
tected goods, none of the dreadful things that
M'Millan has foretold during all these years will
ensue, and the public receives circulars like this :
THREE GREAT LINES OF FLANNELS AT LESS
THAN FREETRADE PRICES.
28/29-inch, 60yd. lengths, at 9d. per yard.
30-inch full, 48yd. ~ lid. ~
36-inch full, 48yd. „ 13i. ~
In Assorted Colours as Samples enclosed.
Please COMPARE these with any imported or locally
purchased goods you may have obtained before the Duties
were imposed.
You will recognise these GOODS are of EXCEPTIONAL
VALUE, and it will prove to your advantage to fill
requirements for the coming season IMMEDIATELY.
Our supplies from the Mills are coming forward freely,
and we are prepared to execute your orders at once, or if
placed with us now can guarantee delivery for either
March Ist or April Ist, as you may desire.
Soliciting your favors, we are, dear sir, yours faithfully,
W. & A. M'ARTHUR, LTD.
Sydney, 20th February, 1902.
This is M'Millan, in a business sense, declaring
that his prices under Protection are less than they
were under Freetrade ; just as last week we had
M'Millan assuring the public by circular that if
it bought goods from nis new factory (where,
thanks to the Federal tariff, much labor that for-
merly didn’t know where to find a crust is now
employed) it would SAVE THE PER
CENT. DUTY. It is pathetic to think of
the years the locust hath eaten or, rather,
the years M'Millan has wasted assuring
a mentally-distracted country that a 27| per
cent, duty meant an increase of at least 40 per
cent, to the consumer. It is impossible to suppose
that M'Millan would deceive the public for a
little paltry gain made out of the sale of his shirts,
so the only possible inference is that he has found
his old theory about how prices necessarily went
up to a terrible extent under Protection to be an
unfortunate delusion.
Queensland : A Pastoral Failure.
The Bulletin receives this letter from a business
man in Northern Queensland :
I notice you take a very close interest in Queensland
matters. So doubtless you have noticed that the
Chamber of Commerce, Townsville, passed a very enthusi-
astic motion favoring the land grant system of railway
construction, more particularly in regard to the Grand
Trunk Transcontinental Railway which will develop
country now useless and bring Queensland the prosperity
which has forsaken it. The scheme is set forth in the
Townsville Daily Bulletin of Bth February. But I
am afraid that you may confuse it with Chillagoe
or some other Wild Oat mining scheme, not
that I am against Chillagoe but unfortunately its
non-success has damned our unfortunate State in the
eyes of the financial world, and I would not like you to
think that the Grand Trunk Transcontinental was a
bloated syndicate spec. Of course, the political opposi-
tion of a few frightened fish who like to see shipping
freights high, ports prosperous and the inland gasping
settler paying the piper, may cause a loud outcry. But
the people of the west sorely need the railway. We
would like your sympathy in forcing the Government
to make the necessary connections or allow others to
do so.
So if this railway is made Queensland is to be
prosperous again. It is the last big drunk that is
to save the country—there is always just one more
big loan drunk wanted to save the country.
Asa matter of fact, however, Queensland is more
saved and more prosperous than it has any reason
to expect with its present huge debt burden. If
it builds its transcontinental railway either with
money raised in England by the Government, or
with money raised in England by a syndicate, it
will be so much more debt, and the State will be
so much the worse off. The Queensland trans-
continental line won’t pay till there is far more
population, and it couldn’t make population
unless the country to be traversed got anew
climate. A comparatively rainless region can’t be
saved by ten railways, let alone one. While the
line was being built there would certainly be a
spurt of prosperity, for a lot of loan money (State
loans or syndicate loans) would be spent in wages
on the construction job. Then the country would
settle down to the grind of paying out of taxation
interest on the foreign loans, or paying rent on
the land granted to the foreign syndicate (it
certainly wouldn’t take its land grant in the
country traversed by its railway), and the
condition of Queensland would be worse than
it is now. The state of affairs in Western
Queensland is certainly bad enough, but the lines,
even in the better-watered parts of the country, do
little more than pay working expenses, and anew
line in the West would probably do less than that.
Neither Western Queensland nor Western Gehenna
can be made habitable by railways. Queensland
has tried so often to save itself and restore its
prosperity by building railways with loan funds
than it has now more line in proportion to popu-
lation than any other country on earth, Westralia
\lone oxcepted. If the enormous amount of
railway it possesses hasn’t brought prosperity to
even the more promising parts of the State, there
is little hope of bringing riches to the howling
deserts of the West with more of it. If a
man isn’t sober after drinking two bottles
of whisky, he isn’t likely to be made
sober by two more bottles. Queensland as
a cattle and sheep country is a failure. It is
mainly a pastoral land, and yet, in cattle, horses,
sheep, and pigs, it holds barely one-third of the
number contained in Great Britain and Ireland,
one-third the number contained in France,
one-third the number in Germany, and it is far
behind Spain, or even the little Danubian States
that were cut out of Turkey. It never came any-
where near Britain, or France, or Germany, in
the matter of live stock—not in .its best days,
before the drought; yet these three countries
are densely-peopled, mostly devoted" to agriculture
or occupied by towns and cities, and would all go
into Queensland’s area with much room to spare.
What Queensland requires, seemingly, is to culti-
vate its decently-watered coastal country for all it
is worth ; to push on manufactures ; and to write
off the west as a bad debt. To make railways
into a cattle and sheep country whieh won’t grow
cattle and sheep to any extent worth mentioning
is suicidal.
Labor Party Finance.
The other day G. D. Meudell, of Melb., wrote
some plain truths about South Australia —pointed
to its diminishing male population and its in-
creasing debt, and showed that a trifle over
100,000 men are left to pay an interest bill of a
million a year, mainly out of wheat harvests that
don’t average 4f bushels to the acre. What fol-
lowed was characteristic. Most Adelaide people
wouldn’t face the music at all; they snorted that
it was “ another Melbourne attack on South Aus-
tralia,” and rushed on to read the cricket scores.
A few Micawbers said that Meudell had for-
gotten the Moontas and Broken Hills that would
most likely turn up shortly. About the only ones
who did own up that Meudell was writing horse-
sense were Conservatives; and, of course, they
used his figures to show the ruinous consequences
of free education and factory inspectors. Ap-
parently the Laborites took their cue from that,
and, as usual on matters of finance, said nothing.
It is just this refusal of Laborites and a good many
Liberals to look the facts fairly in the face that
represents one of the big slices of bad luck ori
Australia’s plate to-day. The Labor parties were
hatched 10 years ago for the express purpose of
knocking out political opportunists; arid, so far
as social reforms go, they have done good work
that way. But until they honestly face this
question of public incomings and outgoings
they must also be counted among; the op-
portunists. And when the other parties had
shirked the question Premier Jenkins tendered
his explanation and alleged defence, and shirked
it too. Jenkins’s explanation is that a good deal
of the loan money has been invested in railways
and other public works which pay the full, or
nearly the full, interest on the money spent upon
them. That is the stereotyped explanation, and
the Jenkinses of this country—Australia is full
of Jenkinses —apparently quite fail to see that it
makes the case very little better. The points
which the Jenkins clan won’t understand are
mainly these:
(1) South Australia has to send away about £1,000,000
a year to pay interest on its foreign public debt, and pro-
bably £1,500,000 a year to pay interest on its foreign
public and private debt. And whether the money, before
it goes away, is filtered through the hands of the railway
department, or the water-works department, or any
other department down to the dog-tax office, the drain
on the country’s aggregate resources is the same.
(2) If the money was borrowed abroad at 3£ per cent,
and invested in public works that yielded 8 per cent, the
drain on the country would still be the same—there
would still be the same £1,000,000 or £1,500,000 a year
going out.
(3) To pay this tribute, S.A. must either learn to do
with less imports, which means less luxuries, -or work
harder and produce more exports, or else it must go on
borrowing (as it is doing), and burst later on, or else it
must burst now. If it left off foreign borrowing, and
didn’t import less, or export more, S.A. would have no
money at all in about three years.
(4) The Jenkins plea is practically to the effect that
the S.A. railways pay reasonably well, and all the profits
have to go to the foreign creditor ; that the waterworks
pay reasonably well, and all the profits go to the* foreign
creditor; in fact, that the profit on everything the
Government owns which yields a profit is mopped up by
the foreign creditor. And Jenkins is apparently rather
proud of this state of things.
The Jenkinses of Australia—which is a Jenkins-
ridden country—always profess to have proved
that borrowing is quite safe when they have
demonstrated that they collect most of the inte-
rest-bill from the people in railway charges instead
of in income-tax charges. And the strange thing
is that the public appears to accept the Jenkins
explanation in perfect good faith.
Ketcliener’s Cordon.
A million or two per week is a stiff price to pay,
and there are many signs that Britain is growing
terribly sick of the S’African war. There is also
considerable evidence that other nations are
growing tired of watching the big dog trying to
worry the little dog. The sorry hash tne big.dog
is making of the proceedings provokes remark in
more countries than Germany, and in more dialects
than are spoken through the nose. And while.the
loyalty of the Commonwealth to the Empire is
unquestioned, there are more than a few people
here whose worship of the British \rmy is not
now particularly abject. And small wonder,
either, for it ought to begin to dawn on even
the most unreasoning Jingo pretty soon that
British generals show a lot more persistence
than generalship. The “difficulties of guerrilla
warfare ” are said to be prolonging the war, but,
after all, the simple way to meet guerrillas is by
sending out more and better-mounted and armed
and provisioned guerrillas to meet them. Of
course the guerrilla who carries a piano and ain
iron bedstead and a cooking stove can’t
hope to overtake the one not thus encum-
bered, but why not leave these behind ?
Mark the changes of bowling tried since
Paardeburg: Proclamations, threats, hangings,
concentration-camps, block-houses, farm-burn-
ings, mobile columns with pianos, ditto without
pianos, night marches, subsidising of privateering
gangs, more proclamations, cattle-duffing, subor-
nation of natives, &c., &c. Every change was
vociferously applauded, and it was predicted of
each that it would soon finish the game. And
still Botha and De Wet are at the wickets. The
latest brilliant idea that struck Ketchener, the*
Resourceful, was to drive the Boers like so many
wallabies into a corner by a long thin line or
cordon of kharki “beaters,” whereby the whole
country-side was to be swept clear of the enemy
in something less than two weeks. The opera-
tions of this cordon, so far, remind people here-
abouts of the famous Tasmanian “block line” of
Governor Arthur’s time. A cordon of white
soldiers and settlers and convicts was stretched
across the island vvitli the object of secur-
ing the remnants of the aboriginal tribes.
The total bag of the costly “drive” was
two decrepit blacks. All the others slid
through the cordon on dark nights. De Wet
and his followers seem to successfully follow a
similar plan with Ketohener’b cordon. Before
the next drive is attempted some pits should be
dug in the veldt, carefully concealed by tarpaulins
or proclamations, or something. Having regard
to the stupid Boer tendency to fall in, this plan
should prove eminently successful. It is a some-
what primitive method of warfare, certainly, but
so is the cordon ; and when modern arts fail,
ancient devices should not be condemned without
trial. As an alternative, the War Office might
send out a few million bushels of those little spiked
affairs that the Scotch used at Bannockburn to
make cavalry work difficult. If a good thick
sowing of these were made throughout the enemy’s
country, business-ends up, the mobility of the
Boers would be gone for ever. While they were
pulling the tin tacks out of their feet, Ketchener
could hurry up and effect captures wholesale. But
the pit-trap is the better plan. It would be safe
to operate, and would at least capture something.
The Dogmas of War.
Cabled that “Switzerland proposes an early
revision of the Geneva Convention, to embody
the lessons of the Boer war.” If Switzerland has
the sense to see it, the chief lesson of the Boer
war is to show the absolute uselessness of the
Geneva Convention and its “rules of warfare.”
These rules, subscribed to by various nations, are
very pretty in the abstract, but, when it comes to
actual fighting, they are broken with impunity,
and there is no penalty to the transgressor.
When a couple of nations of equal, or nearly equal,
strength start a row they may, at the outset,
observe a good deal of politeness in their fighting,
because of the idea common to both that the for-
tunes of war are uncertain, and that the upperdog of
to-day may be the lower dog of to-morrow.
When, however, conditions become unequal, and
one side sees that it is bound to win and the other
to lose, the irritation at “an unduly prolonged
resistance ” on the one side and the fury of des-
f air on the other, soon sweep away all politeness.
n those cases where the nations involved are of
nothing like the same size, where the issue can
hardly be in doubt at the outset, the rules of war-
fare are only so much respected as the shame-
facedness of one party and the patience of the
other dictate: that is to say, as a rule, they are
not respected at all. A little nation is apt to lose
sight of Geneva rules in its despairing irritation,
and the bigger one, by a trait of human nature, is
apt to become very cruel and sullen if its little op-
ponent doesn’t give in after the first blow. The
Geneva Conventions of the past have never sug-
gested any means by which their very excellent
rules of warfare might be enforced, and there does
not seem to be any attempt in that direction now.
Under the circumstances the passing of further
rules is like laying down a Decalogue and for-
getting to provide a Hell. The nations of the
earth are not yet educated up to the ethical stan-
dard of obeying laws which can be enforced by no
penalties.
The Callousness of Women.
“ Gumleaf” on a subject that badly wants venti-
lating :
A false notion of chivalry, handed down from the foolish
feudal days, has blinded the world to the brutal callous-
ness of women. One of the delusions of civilisation
is that women are more kind - hearted, gentle and
affectionate than men. Yet the histpry of every
nation is black - streaked with the brutality of
women. But it is not necessary to go to ancient history
for samples of women’s crueity. The treatment of a
helpless child is the true test of kind-heartedness. Only
a fiend can ill-use a babe. Yet women, about whose
gentleness and care much mawky foolishness is written
and talked, do it every hour of the day and night.
For proof, one has only to read the newspaper
reports of how little helpless children are done
to death by the criminal carelessness of women.
These dreadful stories have a strangely hideous likeness.
They generally open with the remark “ While Mrs.
was talking to her next door neighbour her little son,
aged three and a half years, fell into a pot of boiling
water which his mother had left standing on the kitchen
floor.” At the coroner’s court a verdict of accidental
death is tamely recorded, and the world goes on deluding
itself that women are gentle, humane and full of love for
children. If a contractor leaves a heap of broken metal
unprotected on the road, and someone is thrown out ef
his cart and killed in consequence, that contractor stands
a good chance of going up for manslaughter. A man-
slaughter verdict against some of the women who leave
pots of boiling water about for their children to fall into
is badly needed to bring these careless wretches to a
proper sense of their responsibility. It may be argued
that carelessness and thoughtlessness do not prove cal-
lousness. In some cases they do not. But, as between
parent and child, carelessness is callousness spelled dif-
ferently. It is the callousness that begets the careless-
ness. There is no insuperable difficulty in keeping- & efiifid
away from a boiling pot or a boiling pot away from s cMd.
The fact that it is not done is the oiear evidence care-
lessness, arising from a heartless disreg-ard tm the
Eainful consequences to the child. And while the
oiling and roasting cases stick out conspicuously
as evidences of women’s callousness, there are other
evidences that do not attract , so much notice.
The high rate of infant mortality is one of them. What
an eloquent denial of woman’s alleged tenderness is this
record of wholesale baby murder ? Improper feeding and
neglect account for most of the deaths. Granted that
ignorance and thoughtlessness are at the bottom of this
evil, their existence is proof of heartlessness. In some
cases the improper feeding is deliberate on the part of
mothers. The writer has personal knowledge of half-a-
dozen women who, although well able to feed their
children from the breast, deliberately “ stopped ” their
milk in order to preserve their figures. Ignorance on the
part of a mother of the way to feed and look after her
child is no excuse in these days. If she had any feeling
for the child she would soon learn how best to bring
her offspring through the dangers of childhood. Instead,
how often is it found that a mother, to save herself
trouble and thought, gives a child something to pacify it
for a moment, but which will have the effect of doing so
for eternity. Everyone with eyes must have sec-n
children given green fruit to stop them crying when the
same end could be reached by the mother keeping
the flies out of their eyes or by cleaning them. The
callousness of mothers is unnatural as far as their
children are concerned. It seems to be directly trace-
able to women’s growing dislike to the performance of
their natural function—child-bearing. To the frivolous,
' pleasure-hunting women of to-day child-bearing is hate-
ful. How often are women heard to say, “ Oh, children
tie one down so.” Every effort is made to keep down
the number in the family. This prenatal murderous in-
tent has a woeful effect upon the character of women—
possibly worse than prostitution. Hence it follows that
the hatred of the unborn child results in neglect and
callousness in regard to it after birth.

MelbOur ne Age complaint
As to the claims of Christianity upon the black races,
these blood-stained Dutch trekkers treated them as
nought. 1 '- The black man to them was scarcely a huma i
-being. He was not permitted to enter their churches or
Bo mingle with them except as a degraded serf. The
1 Boer proved himself the worst of all tyrants to the weak
—an ignorant and degraded tyrant; and he proved him-
self the worst of all neighbors to the strong—an encroach-
ing and suspicious neighbor.
David Syjie is a member of an Empire which, has
encroached upon over 11,000,000 square miles of
the earth’s surface, and still David is shocked at
the little Boer State’s encroachments. As to the
fact that the Boer doesn’t allow the Kaffir to
mingle with him on anything like equal terms,
nobody has ever seen an aboriginal dining at
David’s lordly table, or noticed King Billy
Eutting his battered tile under a front seat in
lavid’s tabernacle. The white Britisher tries
to feel like a brother to his black relative,
but he makes no more real success of the
work than the Boer himself. And Britain’s chief
friend, brother and ally is supposed to be the
United States, where black men are still burned
alive by lynch law. After all, the average man
is only shocked at the way some nigger is treated
when there is a handsome profit to be made by
being shocked. If he was liable to be shocked
gratis, something pretty bad would have happened
ere this to Westralia, where the black slave is not
encouraged to go to church regularly with his
Bible under his arm and his 3d. collection-money
in his loin-cloth—when things run to a loin-cloth.
The Criminal Court trial of Dickson, late
accountant of Melb. University, brought out very
little that was news re the £25,000 shortage in
Dickson’s cash. Whisperers before the trial
hinted at a coming revelation of accomplices in
Dickson’s frauds, or a least a disclosure of large
borrowings of money by the accountant’s learned
friends at the University. But when the time
came for Dickson to look sinned against rather
than sinning, he could produce nothing more sen-
sational than a letter written to him by Registrar
a’Beckett, asking for the loan of £IOO in addition
to the £IOO which he (a’Beckett) had previously
borrowed. This was evidence of the Registrar
being under serious obligation to his inferior
officer, but the wording of the letter went
to show that the borrower thought he was
appealing to a man in possession of some private
means, rather than demanding assistance from a
dishonest accountant. Anyway the reprehensible
a’Beckett was not the first person to receive
stolen money from Dickson, nor did his total
borrowings amount to more than a vulgar fraction
of the magnificent £25,000 deficiency in the
prisoner’s cash. Dickson, it is generally under-
stood, was a juggins who fell into the hands of
Ikey Mo. The Melb. University frauds may be
instanced as an argument in favor of the Totalisa-
tor, seeing that the South Australian police
records show a decline in the annual crop of
embezzlements, such as Dickson was guilty of,
since the seductive credit bookmaker was knocked
out of fashion by the honest, plain-dealing
machine. Looks as though Ikey Mo must have
repaid a substantial sum to Dickson in order to
keep him quiet concerning the true purpose of his
big steals.

Premier Philp’s lusty barracker, Brisbane
Courier, keeps on talking with the dull buzz of a
phonograph about how the Queensland State
finances have been “dislocated by drought,” and
how its clumsy muddler has had “a gigantic task ”
to encounter, and such-like twaddle. If the
Courier will be honest for once, which is probably
impossible, and will publish a statement of the
revenue for the last 10 or 20 years, it will then
reveal the fact that Philp, in the year of his great
deficit, had more revenue than any other Queensland
Treasurer ever received—that his finances weren’t
dislocated by drought—and that there was nothing
wrong at the Treasury except that Phiup’s is the
worst, most incapable and most disreputable
gang of political drunks that even the Continuous
Ministry has ever produced. No Queensland
Premier ever had such a splendid revenue to
spend as Philp, and the shortage, the looming in-
solvency, the need for heavy direct taxation, and
the general disaster all arise from one cause—that
Philp’s proper position is not so much at the head
of the State as in the shafts of a fruit barrow.
The bubonic up-to-date hasn’t paid a neighborly
call to Melbourne ; but the odds are very much in
favor of its doing so. It may reckon on a really
lordly reception. There are just now, for instance,
in the big W. Melbourne dock several tons of fish,
in a condition of extreme ripeness. The Board of
Health is “considering ” this fish, but up to time
of writing had done nothing. The Harbor Trust
says the fish is dutiable, and so it dare not touch
it. The Customs people say it is not dutiable,
and, even if it were, it is the Trust’s business to
get the venerable fish out of the road. The con-
signees are carefully lying low, and decline to take
any interest at all in their damaged wares. Mean-
while, the rats are having a gorgeous feast, and
are stowing away in their holes a good supply of
bad fish against the coming winter. The same
rats still pass the time between meals in sampling,
pawing, and otherwise befouling the “slack”
sugar on the wharves prior to its being swept up,
re-bagged and sold by the cheap grocers.

Last year’s clear profits of the S’African diamond
fields were £2,687,999. The fields have been
working 32 years, and it is estimated they will
last another 144 years. Long ago there was a
signed agreement between Great Britain and the
Orange Free State, by which the former promised
to make no treaties with the native tribes north
of the Orange River. But the great Diamond
Fields were discovered on Free State territory,
and north of the Orange. In a very few years the
Free State was notified that the patch of territory
was claimed by a Kaffir chief named Watkrboeh,
and that Britain, as his ally, was going to take
possession. It took possession, at the point
of the bayonet. Later, the Free State pointed
out that the fields hadn’t been handed over to the
alleged rightful owner, Waterboeu, but that
Britain was itself in occupation. Then it was ex-
plained that Waterboer’s alleged title had
proved to be all wrong, after all. Great Britain
refused to return the territory seized under this
unfortunate misapprehension, but it offered
£90,000 compensation. And still there are Jingo
papers which want to know what possible
grievance the Orange Free State had to make it
throw in its lot with Kruger’s commandoes.
The recent uprising of the Bengal Chamber of'
Commerce to' protest against the white labor
clauses of fche Australian Postal Bill is a fine
example oipthe ghastly insolence of the superior
kind of person. The Australian Senate proposed
that Australia should not subsidise any mail boats
manned by colored crews, and the Bengal Cham-
ber of Commerce declared itself “ quite at a loss to
understand the motives of the Bill, or on what
principle of right and equity it was based.” The
Chamber then went on to complain against the
arrogance of Australia in dictating to shipowners
outside of Australia as to how they should work
their ships. It saw nothing impudent in the
action of a chamber of commerce dictating to a
sPlf-governing community of four millions of
people how it should spend its coin. Australia,
let it be observed, didn’t propose to dictate to
any shipowner what kind of crews he should
employ; it merely proposed to dictate to itself what
kind of ships it should subsidise. In other words it
proposed to do what it thought best with its own
money. The Bengal Chamber of Commerce holds
that Australia has no right to do what it thinks
best with its own money, which is just where Aus-
tralia and the yellow gentlemen with the curried
stomachs disagree.
