The Plague shows poor discrimination in
its choice of victims. With so many entirely dull
and humorless persons to select from, it singled out
Ada Lee and Sally Booth, of the Arnold Comedy
Co., for attack, and sent them from a world which
their bright cleverness had done much to amuse.
Ada Lee will be best remembered in Sydney as the
Swedish housemaid who “never saw anybody
gum in, never saw anybody gum out” of the
house invaded by Jones: Sally Booth as the
President of the Cook Ladies’ Union, who, by
superior bad cooking, drove a haughty mother-in-
law from the habitation of Smith. The infected
Criterion Hotel was responsible for the death of
the two chums, who shared a room there. They
were removed together to the hospital and died
within a few hours of one another, the elder suc-
cumbing first. -•
* * * •
Australia, and N.S. W. in particular, seems
to be an unlucky place for theatricals. Count
over the deaths of visiting stars: Amy Roselle,
and Arthur Dacre, Sadie Macdonald, Dante the
Great, Oscar Girard, Ada Lee, Sallie Booth—
these are but a few of the names that occur.
** * #
A large audience, some of which was re-
duced to the dress-circle steps, gathered together
to welcome the return of Bland Holt and co. at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday night, and to pass
up bouquets of all shapes and sizes to Mrs. B.
“ Hearts are Trumps,” by Cecil Raleigh, is not
quite an ideal play, apart from its numerous
pictures. The male characters are rather sketchy,
and the early part of the drama is sketchy also ;
but when once the Baccarat Scandal and the
Gymkhana are well under weigh the play takes a
spurt, and the various tall, black-coated gentle-
men, who all seem too much alike at first, sort
themselves out. “ Hearts are Trumps ” deals
mostly with high-class society ladies who scream
and yell at their card-playing in a way which
would be considered quite wrong amongst people
without handles to their names. In these gilded
halls of aristocracy high-bred countesses accuse
other blue-blooded dames of cheating, and a stout
lady of immense social standing is ordered off to
bed by her husband. Also, the stout lady goes.
All this is valuable knowledge to Bill and his best
girl in the gallery. It is a drama with considerable
good dressing in it, and when a high bred lady
says she is so dreadfully poor that she will have to
end it all, she is certain to appear in the next act
in a Frenchier hat and a longer and frillier tail-
piece than formerly. The nominal heroine is a
daughter of Miss Frances Ross, with a mania for
getting into trouble from the purest of motives.
She gets into social disgrace first because an artist
has painted a classically undraped figure on to the
portrait of her head, and exhibited it at the Royal
Academy, whereat all the ladies who scream and
veil at cards won’t speak to Dora or her mother.
There is material for an excellent libel-action in
this, but Frances Ross merely gets angry before
the picture and tears it somewhat. The classical
female who is supposed to be Dora has the most
dreadful anatomy this scribe has ever seen
on canvas ; and if Dora was really thought
to be like that, her guardians ought to
have lent her to the Zoo as a giraffe.
In the next act, Dora tumbles into disgrace once
more, when, in order to save the unhappy home,
she appears at the Frivolity Theatre in brown
tights as a ballad singer. Dora’s mamma, who is
as particular as fast ladies usually are, rebukes her
violently and publicly from a box, and all of the
Frivolity audience that isn’t dummy rises in
wrath. The fact that the manager of the Frivolity
rushes after Dora next morning with an offer of
£5O a week, isn’t much more extraordinary than
that a ballad singer should appear in tights instead
of the regulation evening dress. In act IV. Dora is
coaxed off to Switzerland, where a dim red sun peers
through the dark mists of dawn for her benefit,
and rises slowly while she is hung over an Alpine
chasm by Bland Holt. He and Albert Norman
topple over the chasm, followed by convincing
rocks, and an uninteresting curate rescues Dora,
preparatory to taking her back to the simple,
innocent village where her only troubles will be
with the choir and the weekly mothers’ meeting.
She is evidently quite unsuited to good society, as
she does not seem able to learn what sins can be
committed and what can’t.
* • * •
Mrs. Bland Holt lives up to the amount
of floral decoration she received when she first
appeared as a Friv. artist limping from the effect
of trying to learn a stage bicycle turn. It is a
good part, with plenty of business in it, and Mrs.
Bland has never had one to suit her better. She
does not overact her seriousness when she is
touched, in orthodox bad girl style, by the
friendliness of the good girl with the undressed
portrait. Miss Frances Ross has improved in her
ac ing, and harangues in front of the dreadfully-
drawn giraffe portrait very effectively. No
humor from the servants’ hall obtrudes into the
drama ; Bland Holt supplies what is needed in
that line while engaged in his occupation as a
r oney-lender with a tendency towards insuring
] eople’s lives and then arranging large moun-
' ainous accidents for them. He works some good
Blaad Holtish effects into this lightsome part,
and perishes forcibly with a pasteboard rock
pursuing him down the large, dim chasm
that looks almost as terrific on the stage
as it is made to do on the posters. W. E. Baker
is forced to hide his good looks under the guise
of a gray-haired Australian millionaire, while his
melodious voice is lost in a heavy Yorkshire
accent which he has faithfully preserved during his
wanderings. For the rest, special mention must
be made of the Earl of Burford’s dancing that i 3
supposed to get into the excellent biograph enter-
tainment, which the audience that is partly dummy
and the audience that isn’t dummy at all, both
witness. Bland Holt’s stage audience looks just
as intelligent on the whole as the one to which this
writer belongs, which observation is meant
kindly, and isn’t nearly so hostile as it looks.
** * *
Sydney Palace was announcing some
new turns when this paper went to
press. This theatre, after a vigorous inspection
by the health authorities, has been declared
absolutely clean. There wasn’t even a high-
smelling joke found on the premises.
* ifc * *
At Her Majesty’s, Sydney, the young man
of the House of Hur, whose father was honored in
the guest-chamber of Augustus, and all the nobles,
citizens, guards, lictors, camel-drivers and lepers
that move in his orbit, go toiling, rejoicing, and
sorrowing for three hours nightly. The ohariot
race lasts for the full five minutes now, and the
audience goes as mad with joy as if it stood to
share the talents that boss bookmaker Sanballat
has taken in the race from the Romans. Messala’s
and Ben Hur’s race-suits of scarlet and gold and
white and gold respectively must have taken the
eyes of female Antioch considerably. Ben Hur’s
chief fault is his mode of address. Whether as a
galley slave talking to the Tribune, or making
love to the Egyptian or Jewish maiden, or as the
anxious son demanding his mother’s whereabouts,
or haughtily stopping the Roman’s chariot, it is all
in the same manner—quick, peremptory, as if he
were seeking a quarrel. The desert tents wherein
Ilderim receives his guests are worth attention, so
is the apartment in the house of Simonides, at
Antioch. Pigeon-holes filled with mortgages,
bills of exchange, and electric-light concessions
from the Pro-consul of Syria, a money-weighing
machine (faked), a poker machine (for sale), and
through the open door a beautiful view of the port
of Antioch with furled sails and galley masts
seen across a low wall covered with purple wis-
taria. Esther, the merchant’s daughter, in this
apartment, declines to be a sister to Ben Hur.
She would rather be his slave ; there is more hope
of him matrimonially in that capacity than in the
“ sister ” state.
** * *
Sydney Royal draws fair audiences to
witness the marriage of the Emperor’s only child
with the laundry lady’s only son. The laundry
isn’t a paying spec, and the lad—who evidently
loafs on his mother—larks around Pekin, spilin’
for a fight and looking for an heiress, in the
raggedest of old jackets and the bashedest of old
hats. So the contrast when the fairies in the
cave clothe him in a superb coat of cloth-of-star-
beams, set with travelling meteorites, is dazzling.
No questions are asked as to how the fairies
have ready-made clothing of such value and
good fit in the cave. And as Aladdin walks
out o’ nights with an Emperor’s child, he
shows no desire to remain in the cave, though
it is tenanted by lovely girls and prismatic
fountains. Chow Chow, the animated pawn-
offiee, Widow Twankey and her nose (red, because
she has been reared on carrots), the Brooklyn cake-
walk, and the Porcelain Procession help the
extravaganza along joyfully.. Ada Willoughby’s
songs, “Sue, I love you!” and “My daddy’s
a gentleman” get her encores, and the Jew
and the comic policeman nightly spoil Miss
Harrington’s song to Aladdin, ere he goes cave-
dwelling, by their antics. Then on canvas there
is the deep blue Inland Sea of the far East, where
Japanese flowers and bamboos grow, and cranes,
with long.foolish legs backdrawn, and long foolish
necks outstretched, fly through the sunset. And if
only the Gotham quartette would eat some of the
phosphor-paste bread and butter squares, now so
plentiful, present penciller would take the extrava-
ganza altogether merrily.
** * *
Miss Peggy Pryde made a welcome re-
appearance at Sydney Tivoli last Saturday. Miss
Pryde is one of the few imported English music
hall stars who did not prove disappointing to the
Australian public: her vitality is magnetic, and
she rouses up a dull, indifferent audience with
her strident little voice. Since her last visit
Peggy’s singing has gone off a little in tone, but
her fun is just as exuberant as ever. The Net-
Ball Game continues to be an attraction, and is
eagerly “barracked” every night. A new item
is provided by a gentleman with a very stern jaw
who punches a ball with speed and vigor. As the
ball can’t hit back there is nothing very stirring
m this turn. A new baritone singer, Mr. James
Opie, calls for no particular comment.

THIS IS HOW.
GEORGE REID (on “South Africa Day”): “ How
was it that so many Australians went over to
fight under the British flag? ”
Runaway Girl ” beats strong. Nobody finds any
particular fault with the girl who quits a convent
and takes to wandering minstrelsy. She is a
plump, cheerful fugitive, for her own part, and
numerous distressed relativesand friends, whodon’t
suspect her of being Florence Young, and are on
the. track of the wrong female until nearly closing
time, present themselves to notice in a favorable
light. Hugh Ward is a highly satisfactory old
uncle of noble birth. Miss Grace Palotta diffuses
an atmosphere of superior gaiety and light re-
freshment. Miss Wallace has a deportment to
match her costume; and Kenningham imper-
sonates a perplexed tenor .with some sense of
humor. As far as its story goes, “A Runaway
Girl ” may be said to hang together. It has a
story. Even Laurie and Carrie Moore are distantly
concerned in the main idea, although they are not
bound to admit the fact. Lauri as Fiipper, a
jockey, amounts to somewhat the same sort of
joke as Lurcher, the sheriff’s officer in “Dorothy,”
and Carrie Moore is the agile soubrette who looks
after him when he isn’t looking after her. Two
misplaced items in the co. are Bantock and Miss
Beatty.. Bantock was all right as the ponderous
mandarin in “ San Toy,” but his attempt to play
Brother Tamarind from the convent is sadly
feeble, and the trouble with his lady help is that
she ought to be a stout, well-matured Peggy
Pryde instead of a merely buxom young Aus-
tralian girl whose cockney accent sounds like a
borrowed article. The chorus is a capable crowd
of gaudy Italians, Venetians, and British tourist
girls, _ and the orchestra and scenery rise to the
occasion.
** * *
Six nights of “ When the Lamps are
Lighted” will terminate at Melb. Royal on
Friday, after which J. F. Sheridan reverts to his
old original Widow O’Brien for one more last
week. “The Lamps” is a lurid melodrama
relieved by a series of Sheridans in various dis-
guises, the principal J.F.S. being a little
Irishman named Dan, who runs a mysterious
caravan show with the assistance of his wife Tilly.
An infant son and heir and a Dickensesque hired
gal figure in the domestic phases of Dan’s career,
whilst a murdered millionaire, an ex-convict, and
a lady who went astray for her daughter’s sake, are
responsible for much sorrow and remorse. “ The
Lamps” offers violent acting opportunities to
several people, but the co. at the Royal is not
such a gifted combination as it might be, conse-
quently the intervals of tragedy lack force and the
narrative in general is not so harrowing as one
could wish to be harrowed with. Two years ago
“ The Lamps ” was rather better treated than now
in some of its gloomiest spots, and if the present
performance were billed to endure for another
week The Bulletin would drop heavily on the
awfullest delinquents. Miss Maud Gwynne, who
has a rich sympathetic voice for expressing her
shame, shines by comparison with the others in
spite of her amateurish way of picking up her
skirt, and Miss Mavis is a pleasant Tilly.
* * - * $
Touching “ Sweet Nell of Old Drury,” at
the Princess’s, there is nothing to remark this
week except a steady improvement in the work of
everybody concerned, from Nell Gwynne and her
Royal Charles down to the deplorable alderman
who speaks three lines in the second act. Nell,
at her harpsichord and elsewhere, is an even
prettier picture than she made on the first night,
and the King, in the high-backed chair, seems to
harmonise more than ever with the furniture and
sinful sentiment of the situation. Moreover, the
artistic success has “ caught on ” to all branches
of the community, which fact disposes of Robert
Brough’s complaint that Melbourne has little or
no taste for high-class comedy nowadays.
»* * *
The competition in the Polite Vaudeville
trade proceeds as usual. It is about as fierce as
Politeness will permit. It has even arrived at
the uttermost limit of competition in the matter
of illustrated song-song, for Jennie Opie at one
house warbles the same yarn about the same pic-
tures that Frances Gwynne warbles about at the
neighboring establishment. Competition suits
the hungry populace all the time. The h.p. gets
great value for prompt cash and lives in hope of a
happy future, when Rickards will be too polite to
charge for admission, anyhow. Up to now that
prodigal manager has been content to keep on
increasing the expenses of his show, but he might
as well reduce expenses and distribute the savings
in the form of silver souvenirs.

Adelaide Royal lately broke out with
Paradise Lost,” set to music, in five acts
aid 34 scenes! And the city of churches
duly rose to the opportunity of observing Satan
reproving Sin —which it had often heard about,
bat never expected actually to see; also of learn-
ing the exact distinction between Lucifer and
Beelzebub and Belial, and of being able to allude
to “ all the devils in Hell ” without using impro-
per language. The Tivoli continues to present a
peaceful show, mostly revolving round the
Jackson family’s musical turn.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
It has been announced that the Federal Govern-
ment is about to float its first loan of either £250,000 or
£500,000. There are other loans to follow—in fact, the
amount already authorised is £1,000,000, for post-offices,
telegraphs, telephones, rifles (£100,000), field guns
(£44,000), buildings, &c. The first loan is to be for 50
years, but a 1 per cent, sinking-fund is to be provided
which will redeem it in about 47 years—unless, which is
almost certain, some impecunious Treasurer gets Parlia-
ment to sanction the seizure of the sinking-fund, or its
virtual suspension, as happened in Maoriland. The sink-
ing-fund is to be invested in bonds of the Federal debt.
And the whole business is cumbersome, stupid, and
thriftless to the last degree.
Suppose the Federal Government borrows, on a
steady average, £500,000 a year (though any other average
sum would point the moral just as well) at 3 per cent., with
1 per cent, sinking fund. Interest and sinking fund will
cost £20,000 the first year, £200,000 the 10th year,
£500,000 the 25th year, £BOO,OOO in the 40th year, £900,000
in the 45th year’, and so on. If the Federal Government
charged the whole £500,000 annually of public works and
military and other expenditure to revenue, the country
would have to pay a little more for perhaps the first 24
years, and a great deal less for all the years after that.
The relief afforded to the revenue by' the loan policy
would be considerable for the first 10 years, but it would
be less every year, until after 15 years or so it would be
hardly perceptible. This is how things would go :
Charge on revenue if Charge on
the Commonwealth revenue if
borrows £500,000 an- the money
nually (3 per cent. is provided
interest ; 1 per cent. out of taxa-
sinking fund). tion.
Ist year .. £20,000 .. £500,000
” ” 200,000 .. 500,000
» •• 400,000 .. 500,000
30th „ .. 600,000 .. 500,000
40th „ .. 800,000 .. 600,000
47t h » •• 940,000 .. 500,000
And every year
after the 47th .. 940,000 .. 500,000
This doesn’t really show all the badness and silliness
of the loan policy—the great postponement dodge—for it
will cost, in addition to the interest and sinking fund a
large amount in expenses of floating loans, in discounts
exchange, management charges, &c. Discount on loans’
alone, in a bad year, may run to £50,000. Then there is
the possibility—almost the certainty—that the money
can’t be procured at 3 per cent., or anything like that
price. But, taking none of these things into account,
the situation as regards the loan and sinking fund dodge
is that it will save, at most, £480,000 the first year’
£300,000 the 10th year, £103,000 the 20th year, nothing
in the 25th year, and that thereafter it will mean a loss
that will swell up till it reaches £440,000 a year, and will
remain at that for ever. It is a pitiably foolish idea in
every respect.

* * • *
The A.M.A. (Miners’ Association), which was
a big thing when Vic., Broken Hill, Newcastle, theS. Aus.
copper-mines, and others were under one roof, has
dwindled sadly since it disintegrated. In Vic., where
the association was cradled, five branches alone have
700 members less than they had 12 months ago. It is at
once a striking illustration of the dangers of the miner’s
work and of the excellence of the association that last
year the society “ benefits ” totalled £13,000: And yet
fatal accidents were much fewer than usual; only 14
members were killed as against 29 in 1899. Apropos, the
A.M.A. formed 12 months ago at Kalgoorlie is doing well ;
it has now 900 members.
In addition to its other mining specialties,
N. Guthridge, Ltd., have now taken up pumps, having
been appointed agents for the Pulsometer Engineering
Co., England. The manufactures of this company are
well known, but it does not confine itself to the celebrated
Pulsometer Steam Pump. Pumps of various kinds are
manufactured by the Pulsometer Engineering Co., and
kept in stock by the agents at 71 Clarence-street,
Sydney, and 486 Collins-street, Melbourne.* ,

Mr. A. Gilfillan, B.Sc., C.E. (Edin.), Mining
and Metallurgical Engineer, Stock Exchange Buildings,
Melbourne, is leaving for London about April next, for
the purpose of establishing a London office, and attending
to the flotation of approved mining and other proposi-
tions. Before leaving Mr. Gilfillan will visit the various
colonies for the purpose of inspecting any options which
may be offered. Correspondence invited from miner
owners who have properties worth handling.*

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the YVesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary' spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts,nuts, forgings, andall sorts of iron-work.*

• * • *
The Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and
Building Co., despite the heavy writing-off done last
year, appears to go down hill even faster than before.
This institution is in the unique position of paying no
interest whatever on its deposits—it has paid none since
1896 ; and as it, of course, pays no dividends, it has only
working expenses and interest on a mortgage of less than
£lO,OOO to struggle with. And property to the alleged
value of £129,000 doesn’t earn enough to pay these items.
The retrogression since 1896 has been as follows :
Capital
Profit on Loss on Total nominally
Business. Properties. Deficit. intact.
1896.. .. .. £16,395 .. £86,360
1897.. £lB7 .. £1,522 .. 17,730 .. 85,025
Loss on
Business.
1898.. £32 .. 3,383 .. 2i,145 .. 81,610
1899.. 33 .. 2,323 .. 23,506 .. 79,249
1900.. 340 .. 34,372 .. 58,218 .. 44,539
1901.. 562 .. 2,723 .. 61,503 .. 41,254
The co. has managed to call up about £2 of new capital
in five years.
It would be difficult to imagine a much more
hopeless-looking situation than that of the Sydney Per-
manent Freehold Land and Building Co. The loss on the
current business grows larger every year, despite the fact
that the deposits, which still amount to £77,496, cost
nothing in interest, and the co. professes to have £41,254
of capital still intact, making over £llB,OOO available for
trading purposes which costs the co. absolutely nothing.
The assets are nominally still worth about £129,000, but
the last year’s earnings (£1096) represent only about
18s. per cent, per annum on this alleged value,
so the real present value is probably about one-
fifth of the figures shown in the balance-sheet.
The co. apparently collects the rents from £116,500
worth (nominal) of property, and the net rents collected
last year were £3Bl, equal to about 7s. per cent, per
annum. The deposits are being slowly paid off, but, on
present appearances, even with the considerable amount
of capital which professes to be still intact, the chances of
depositors getting 20s. in the £ are very remote.

The position begins to be at least respectable. The
amounts realised on property sales can’t fairly be regarded
as profit in the ordinary sense of the term ; the properties
were written down heavily out of capital, and when some
of them realise more than the written-down value it
simply means, not that a profit has been made, but that
the loss has proved to be rather less than was expected.
But the earnings of the business represent, for last half-
year, over 8 per cent, per annum on the reduced capital of
about £48,000 ; and the reserves, after a long period of
decline, have begun to grow again at a respectable rate.
They will have to grow for a long while before the lost
capital can be restored, but still, with care and moderate
dividends, that result should be arrived at in time.
« « * *
Maoriland Agricultural Department is raging
about alleged frauds perpetrated with its State’s butter.
Maoriland butter has now acquired a high repute in Eng-
land, and it is reported that the inferior Australian
butter-maker buys it, planes off the brand, put his own
degraded Australian superscription on the box, and sends
it Englandwards so as to work up a reputation that will
help the sale of his own inferior cow-grease. But for this
admixture it is claimed in some excited quarters that the
Australian cow-fat would be so uniformly poor that it
wouldn’t sell at all. Strong measures, in the way of huge,
deeply-indented brands which can’t be planed off without
wrecking the box, are threatened. After all, the best
way would be to prohibit the export to this country,
which, apparently, doesn’t deserve Maoriland butter.

half-year to keep things on a sound footing hereafter, it
is difficult to form any estimate of the position. The

highly promising one.
Bullion is conveyed by rail between Melbourne
and Sydney without much precaution. Frequently con-
signments of 100,000 sovs. go frorh Spencer-st. station in
charge of a couple of weak-looking bank officials. Pub-
licity as to the coin being on board does not appear to be
guarded against in any way. Only thing that keeps
robbers off, seemingly, is the thought that the sickly-
looking bank-clerk with a huge collar is, as shown by
practical experience, very often a cayenne devil to fight.
Statistics on the subject show that it is dangerous to
monkey with the Australian bank-clerk.

The Industrial and Provident Permanent Bene-
fit Building and Investment Society and Bank for Savings
(Sydney), which has the most lengthy name on this
continent, still appears to continue the fading process
already alluded to on this page. It is a very slow
process, and with occasional breaks, but the general
tendency appears to be that way. Recent figures are as
follows :
Profits. Dividends. Written Re-
off. serves.

There is also a mysterious “ Stock Suspense Account,
£1579,” but it doesn’t appear to be anything in the nature
of a reserve, though what it maybe is none too clear.
The society with the long name is apparently shrinking
off the face of the earth by the withdrawal of paid-up
shares. In 1895, the paid-up capital was £113,525, but,
by constant withdrawals, it has now got down to £53,350.
Deposits have remained almost stationary at the very
small figure of a little over £4OOO. If the present rate of
retrogression continues, the institution will apparently
cease altogether in a few years, though vvhether, in that
case, the iast shareholder takes the reserve fund is an-
other question. Meanwhile, the reserves are also disap-
pearing in a gradual sort of fashion.
* * » •
The latest move of the Maoriland Farmers’
Union was to deputationise the Underwriters’ Association
and request that the insurance rates on farm buildings be
reduced, and to suggest that if the Association suffered
any loss thereby, it could be rftacte up 'b-y increasing the
rates on town properties. At the close of the interview
the union showed its claws. It intimated that it hoped
the underwriters would see their way clear to make the
reduction so as to enable the farmers to continue doing
business with the existing companies. Presumably the
union is trying to screw up its courage to put up its own
money and have an insurance fund of its own. If it does
it will be a new departure, for hitherto the Farmers’
Union has not shown much courage ; only amazing
impudence.

The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building
and Investment Association (Sydney), which has been in
trouble for a long time, has recently decided to face the
situation. It has done this by writing its paid-up capital
down from £145,981 to £42,083. Of the £103,898 thus
provided, £75,250 was written off the book values of
properties, £14,964 was transferred to a reserve _ fund,
£5750 to a contingency fund to meet future possibilities,
and £7934 went in writing off accumulated losses. The
recent record stands as follows: —

the greatly-reduced capital, so, despite the material
improvement which has apparently set in, it would be
rather a sanguine proceeding to decide yet that dividends
are in sight at any early date.
* • • *
Harrold Brothers, the sensational S.A. insol-
vents of five years ago, have just been wound up in the
courts. Their assets realised £28,000, but, largely owing
to the bitter opposition of various creditors (which re-
sulted in imprisonment for one partner), expenses were
enormous. Senate-President Baker’s firm took £4600 in
costs, insolvents’ solicitor £2200, and an opposing creditor.
£lOOO. Eventually, there was £BOOO left to divide, and,’
as this only meant a dividend for the creditors of Bd. and
one-fifth of a farthing in the £, the extent of the liabili-
ties can be imagined.
*• • ■
The mysterious closing-up of the Broken Hill
zinc works 12 months ago, and the stoppage of further
purchases of zinc product, was, it now appears, due to
another of the numerous understandings among the
spelter-distillers of Belgium and West Germany. The
arrangement was to limit production so that the market
should not keep below £l7 ; and, naturally, the foreign
works were shut up first These spelter-producers of
Belgium, Holland, and W. Germany, who are responsible
for more than a third of the world’s total, have worked
together so well that the increase of output for many years
past has been insignificant. Since 1895 the lead output
has gone up 27 per cent., and the copper ditto 42 per
cent. ; but the output of zinc has increased only 17 per
cent., and that there has been even that much is
America’s fault, and not Europe’s. Whether the com-
bination can afford to produce at a much lower price
than £l5 or £l6 isn’t clear, and .therefore , what sort of a
lion it represents in the path of the new Australian zinc
industry isn’t clear, either. This much is clear : the
present price is artificial, and that doesn’t sound too
pleasant.
• * * •
The Miners’ Accident Relief Fund (N.S.W.),
which lately completed its first year of service, hits the
miners’ unions in one place where they didn’t expect it;
it is resulting in heavier calls on their funds. For a time,
the officers couldn’t account for the steady shrinkage in
the bank balances, but they know now. Before, when an
injured man was drawing only the union benefit, he got
“ off the funds ” as soon as he could stand up to his work ;
now, with the additional 12s. a week which he gets from
the State-subsidised fund, he lets himself quite recover
before he takes on hard graft again.. Already, in conse-
quence, one of the biggest of the unions has had to cut
down its benefits.
• * * •
The new development at the B.H. Junction
looks like a pretty large thing; but half its immediate
value is lost because it cannot be economically worked
on account of the extraordinary windings in the under-
ground workings. The British developments are better ;
this co.’s mill will probably start when lead gets to £l2
and looks like staying there. It is reasonably certain
that there is two years’ ore available. The North restart-
ing is also promised at £l2. The Junction cannot do it
at that figure, though ; neither can Block 14, whose
prospects continue dismal. The rooting-out of the
carbonates from this mine should be stopped ;.in fact, the
whole policy and management need overhauling. Legd
continues to pick up a bit in price ; but for all that
statistics look very sick still.
■ ■ • *
The Yanco Glen Water Co., which has so long
been threatening the none too profitable monopoly of the
Broken Hill Water Supply (none too profitable, that is,
considering its short concession), bobs up again with ap-
plications for leases of dam and pumping sites. It
was stated in support that Sydney capitalists (at present
unmentionable, though it was added that one is a leading
Sydney merchant and an M.L.C.) would underwrite the
scheme if the promoters could present a good title. The
matter is, however, again hung up indefinitely.
* • • *
“Sessil”: Bora Creek silver fields (N.S.W.)
are suffering from the general want of water. The
Conrad—bought three years ago for an alleged few hun-
dreds—seems a promising show. New ground is being
opened up at the 250 ft. level—lsoft.-below, .present, work-
ings. Manager now projects opening up the mine 1200 ft.
northward, which, with the present 1200 feet to south, will
uncover the lode right through the property. The later
prospects show the richest and biggest lode yet struck.
There has never been a break in the lode—which runs
right through the Conrad and the adjoining South Blocks.
The mine has paid “ from the grass-roots,” having pro-
duced cost of all works, machinery, etc., from its own
interior. In addition, 3000 to 4000 tons of concentrates
are in hand. The old dam is being enlarged, and a new
one of 6,000,000 to 7,000,000 gallons capacity is nearly
completed.
“ Sessil ” : Jno. Howell and H. E. Moore’s
South Blocks (Conrad Extension) is on the same lode as
the big mine, and a 1200 ft. tunnel connects pup with
parent. The former’s deepest workings are now 300 ft.
below the surface, and drives have exposed thou-
sands of tons of ore ready for stoping—apparently
a finer and richer body of stone than the Conrad’s.
Bora Creek is almost on the verge of the valley of the
Big River—across which is that copper country that, I
believe, will yet be found to extend from Gulf Creek to
Bundarra. Between the Conrad and the river lies a
promising minor show —McCrohon and party’s German
Gully. Here, at the 65'feet level, the lode is 20 feet wide
and not yet cut through. Assays 39ozs. of silver and
some lead. A number of other small shows promise very
fairly. The Gwider, of Gall and party, is on a 4 feet lode
at 33 feet. The owners are reticent but not unhappy. In
all probability Bora will be a big place in a year or two.
* * * *
“Sessil”: Boggy Camp (N.S.W.) diamond
fields are moving again. Alleged that some co.’s were
under contract to supply stones at very low price and
hence output has been limited. Contracts having ex-
pired, more are coming on market. Local price runs up
to £l, or even 255., per carat.
• • * *
Writes a man who has just been to White Cliffs,
the opal mining camp in far-west N.S.W. : “ I know which
is the last township before you get to Hades; I’ve just
come from there on the White Cliffs coach. A thousand
men, and about as many others, are emptied out on the
field. Not a decent habitation in the place, though it is
10 years old ; for few of the people who go there stick.
Not a single tree. Not even a pepper-bush ; and in the
West that is a sure sign of most things that are bad. For
a mining camp fewer pubs, than you would expect.
Not less drinking—good Heavens, no ! ‘ The club ’
keeps the pubs. down. A remarkable institution {s’
this club—‘ the working-men’s club.’ Half of the
male population are members; and the thirstiest
half. The profits have gone in billiard-tables, reading-
room, library, and so on. It offers the best accommoda-
tion of this sort on the field. Its political atmosphere is
lurid with radicalism. Also, it is a hand}' place to do a
deal in opals. Mention opal, and one-half the population
prepares to take down the other half. Apart from that,
it is a typical mining camp. I had to call at about the
biggest store to do business with the boss. Of course, it
was ‘ Well, let’s come and have a taste first.’ It always
is that, even if you only call to buy a packet of candles.
‘ S’pose so,’ says the boss ; then, ‘ Come on, chaps,’ to his
three or four assistants. That’s the rule, too. We all
tasted; the missis looked after the shop till we had
finished. This place, which, if it were in a suburb, you
would reckon carried a stock of about 15 penn’orth of
lollies and three flypapers, is packed with £4OOO worth of
stuff. There’s an aristocracy, of course—mostly with the
front name of Abraham or Moses. But A. and M. don’t
gouge for opal; they buy it.
*’• • *
Editor Bulletin. — Drake has just had its hopes
shattered again. The Mt. Carrington battery, which
occupies the most accessible site for the Hill mines, has
lain idle for the last 12 or 14 years, the owners having
declined to crush for the public. They have left the
plant so religiously alone that the tubes of the boiler had
corroded till they could be picked to pieces with the
fingers. Well, a month- or so before Christmas, Messrs.
Lovely and Crabb, who had tried in vain to get this bat-
tery on terms, bought an interest in it from a shareholder
who had left the district, and started to get ready to con-
centrate ores for the public. They spent over £2OO in
re-tubing the boiler, erecting a concentrating-table, re-
pairing the dam, etc., and were on the eve of starting to
crush,“when Fate, in the shape of the biggest shareholder,
put his foot down on them and would not allow them
to carry on the work any further. Litigation followed ;
the case was decided the other day before Warden
Martin ; and. to the disappointment of 99 per cent, of the
population the obstructing shareholder won. Result:
There are thousands of tons of copper ore on the Mount
waiting treatment, and instead of employment being
found for at least 100 men, there has been a large addition
to the long list of unemployed, while the best battery-
site is occupied by an idle battery. No person wishing
to erect a battery can get a site to suit his purpose so
well, as there is a tram-line running from near the top of
the hill so that ores could be landed from the mines to
the battery at a very nominal cost, and plenty of firewood
and water handy. What a shame that a whole district
has to suffer because one man, not having the interests
of the field at heart, puts bis foot down on an enterprise
that would have done such an amount of good ! —Stamper.
* » *
Perth Morning Herald, which of late has had
occasional signs of a progressive spirit, bewails the fact
that “ Westralia does not reap so much advantage as it
should from its gold-mining industry, owing to the fact
that the bulk of the shares in all the leading mines are
held in London, and most of the profits are paid to people
residing outside the State. Numerous instances have
arisen in which local holders have been deprived of their
interests by the means of hastily-conceived recon-
structions. Scarcely a single reconstruction of a West
Australian mining company has been effected without
some Australian holders being left out in the cold.” It is
a refreshing thing to find one daily paper in Australia
which does not look upon the influx of British capital
(which is only the importation of debt and embarrassment)
as an unmixed blessing.
Warnings of a seriously-overcrowded labor-
market come over from the off-side State. The slump in
lead and copper drove men across in hundreds just when
some of the Kalgoorlie mines were themselves easing off.
* • * *
Now, you couldn’t have it put more delicately
than this, from Coolgardie : —“The King Solomon mine
has closed down for a reason not yet assigned. For
November and December this mine milled 1006 tons of
stone for 2170 z., and from tailings (1800 tons) 1450 z.
were obtained. It would, appear, therefore, that
one of the main factors of the trouble would be the
paucity of gold.” The popular cure out Coolgardie way
is to “ let her on tribute.” Even the dear old Plunder-
derry has gone that way.
m m * »
Zeb. Lane has engineered the purchase of the
Wallsend mine (Westralia) for the Collie Proprietary ; so
the only two coal producers in Westralia are now in the
one lot of deft hands. The output of the two (aided by a
third small show, now out of the business) was in 190'),
118,410 tons ;in 1901, 117,835 tons. The co. (the Collie
Proprietary is a mostly-English co icern) is bringing out
a briquetting plant from Europe ; this will probably end
the excessive waste in carrying the softer stuff. It is a
pure coincidence, no doubt, that Lane completed his deal
just before the report of the Westralian select committee
on local coal was published ; and it is just his sheer good
luck, no doubt, that this committee recommends that
instructions be given for exclusive use of Collie coal on
Westralian railways.
* • • •
Officially, Westralian gold is now reckoned at
£3 13s. an ounce. This is the second change. First it
was £3 165., which in the days of alluvial and surface
slugs was too low ; then for a time £3 175.; and now, in
these cyanide days, 4s. less than that.
• • • •
The big and growing demand for mineral
phosphates to manure poor old South Australia’s de-
teriorating farm-lands has set a few prospecting for the
stuff. Its occurrence isn’t rare, but mostly it is in small
pockets not worth picking. The best are on Yorke’s
Peninsula, and the Govt. Geologist thinks them good
enough to be worked at a profit.

Ebagoola (N.Q.): From starting of crushing to
end of last year put through 1770 tons for 50460 z. _ A
second battery is now going up and additions are being
made to the old mill. Some alluvial is now going through
and the return has averaged 300 oz. a month for the last
half-year. Still at present not a dozen men are making
wages at the game.

Will the churches follow the theatres in
the Rat trade ? A well-known lady musician was
manipulating a Sydney church organ the other
day when she became aware, by various scuttling
sounds, that a member of the bubonic tribe was
joining in the entertainment inside. Some time
later the rat made itself still more obnoxious by
dying, and then the organist took her courage in
her hands and proclaimed that she would discourse
NO RESPONSIBILITY.
CONSTABLE: “Hey! fwhere's yer loights? ” SHAFTER: “ Arst t h’ dri 'er. I'm the ’orse."
no more sweet sounds till the organ left off doing
double duty as a tomb and became an infeoted
area for a time. Weird stories are going about re
dogs which used to catch rats in back yards and
which now shudder and turn pale when they see a
rat and won’t give chase to any of the tribe. Every
second dog-owner in Sydney is beginning to have
a dog of that sort. Cats do not seem to have this
subtle instinct, but the cat-owner is generally a
woman, and women haven’t had their inventive
powers strengthened by a long career of fishing, as
men have.
“ J. 0.” : If the Greenlander was known
to kneel by his icy hearth and ask for an average
temperature of 60 degrees we should say “Poor
savage.” Yet, parsonically, his position is just as
strong as ours when we pray for rain. Why, then
still totters the Request Aquatic on the brink of
the abyss that has practically engulfed all other
Begging in Physios, and why does the illogicalness
of the Supplication Watery survive in these days
of logic? J
• • * •
As a rule, when a bank official is found
bound and gagged on the floor, with a thrilling
tale to tell about four ruffians who came in and
looted the bank, the police, after wandering in
their minds for awhile, come back and arrest that
banker on spec, and look under the bed for the
money. They have just done it again in Tas-
mania, at the same time taking in the local post-
master—apparently suspected of tying up the
banker by friendly arrangement.
• • • *
.Re the Melb. case in which a couple named
Stowers stand charged with a serious crime on a
clue secured from a laundry. A case of bank rob-
bery was sheeted home against two out of three
men the third escaping arrest —at Dunedin, years
ago, on similar information. A well-dressed indi-
vidual called at a small branch bank for change
when, there was only one official in attendance.
An authoritative knock was heard at the side door,
and the clerk attending to it was accosted by an
alleged inspector, who complained loudly about
the filthy state of the premises. He succeeded in
getting the indignant clerk into the yard, a fierce
and insulting discussion ensued, and the official
returned only to find that his cash had disap-
peared. A third man had kept watch in the
street. The bank-clerk’s recollection of the men’s
faces was hazy, but he remembered their new and
elegant linen. So the detectives haunted every
laundry in that economical city of frayed shirts
and well-worn collars, and A ce d every new shirt
which seemed to have cost 114 >re than 3s. 6d. to its
lair. When they had run the third new shirt to
earth they captured the two principal offenders.

Cheering extract re a dairy inspector’s
report to Benalla (Vic.) Shire Council:—
Out.of 39 premises inspected not one contained proper
provision for the storage of milk. The Council considered
the statement “ somewhat startling.” Then it postponed
consideration for a month !
The good old postponement!
* * • *
Diminutive Te Aroha, an almost imper-
ceptible flyspeck on the map of Northern M.L.,
burned Campbell-Bannerman in effigy with
riotous enthusiasm lately, owing to his anti-war
opinions—it will be necessary yet for some of these
Jingo towns to burn the Saviour in effigy for His
peace principles. The same town, which is won-
derfully indignant for its size, violently threatened
France over the Dreyfus case.
* • * •
The church is getting down in some places
about as low as it can fall. Anglican Bishop
Wallis farewelled Maoriland’s eighth cohentingent
by telling it that he “thanked God that He
had given us this war to fight, because it had
brought Englishmen together.” A bishop, the
well-paid and absurdly-gaitered preacher of the
gospel of peace, thanking God for war and blood
and butchery is surely a unique degradation.

WOMAN’S FAVORITE PASTIME.
“I am going fishing to-morrow “ What for?” “ Tom !”
Byron, the “Byroniter,” who failed to
fulfil his counsel’s promise that he would experi-
ment with his new vegetable “explosive” before
the jury, at Melb. Criminal Court, was found
guilty of fraud, and sentenced to two years’ im-
prisonment. The depressed patentee carried his
air of mystery into the dock with him. When
asked to show cause why he shouldn’t be cut off
for awhile from his incomprehensible motive-
power,he made a rambling statement to the effect
that his late partner had not paid him all the
money needed to make him (Byron) reveal the
strength of his invention. Also, he assured the
Judge and jury that he was, until recently, a
Government inspector of railways in N.S.W., at a
salary of £75 a month, and would one day enable
the British navy to carry on its business without
the assistance of coal. Byron certainly possesses
a talent for persuading people to take him seri-
ously in some respects, judge Johnston discovered
an off-chance of madness in his method, and gave
orders for the state of his Byronio mind to be in-
vestigated by the gaol doctor.
* * * •
For a long while the lodger has been a
pest, but now he threatens to grow into a positive
danger. Some little time ago, one in Wellington
(M.L.) walked into his landlady’s room after 11
p.m. (the lady was living apart from her husband)
and, picking her up bodily with her bed-linen,
carried her into his own room, where he adminis-
tered a first-class thrashing. The lady, instead
of taking the walloping in a proper, dutiful and
submissive spirit, resisted, whereupon the gentle-
man, very naturally, became exasperated, called
her a cat, and finally opened the window and
threw her out into the street below. She fell on
to a verandah-roof, and rebounded thence on to
some passing citizens. If this sort of conduct
it to be a regular thing among boarders, the
necessity of raising the price of Roast and Roost
is obvious.
* * * *
Coroner Dickson spoke out plainly in
Melbourne lately, at his daily inquest on a
boarded-out infant:—
The matter is a very serious one, and the attention of
the public and the Government should be instantly and
forcibly directed to it. The mortality among these
infants is increasing daily, and there is no reason why
this should be so. Something should certainly be done
instantly to remedy this state of affairs, and the estab-
lishment of a public institution where boarded-out
infants could be treated with a certain degree of uni-
formity is a question that should be taken up by the
Government or some public-spirited persons.
As The Bulletin has pointed out repeatedly, a
State Foundling Home is the only remedy for the
dead baby on the corner allotment; and the par-
sonical element which opposes such an institution
has a good deal of the spirit of the late unlamented
Herod. The idea that virtue is to be encouraged
by baby-murder is too absurd to be taken as a
serious excuse.
• • • •
A certain dramatic lady came from Mel-
bourne to Sydney to found a Ladies’ Club and
coax into it all Sydney’s womankind whose talents
run to the buskin and pen. Latest news is that
the club has suicided in a manner characteristic of
womankind. The easily-flurried sex came to a
sudden conclusion that the club was insolvent, and
separated hurriedly for fear of being put in prison
for debt. Now it discovers that it was solvent
all the time and had thirty shillings to the good.
It had simply added its accounts up wrongly.
It follows that each precipitate lady is going
about blaming the other for the occurrence. When
the club was born, a discussion was held as to
whether men should be admitted, or an Undesir-
able Immigrants’ Bill be passed against the sex.
A lady opened the ball by remarking casually
that the more she saw of men the more she
liked dogs. This animal item was allowed
to drop, and several dames and spinsters of
the type that speaks of its reputation as
if it were something portable, remarked that
they would consider themselves liable to be re-
garded as Lost if gentlemen were admitted to a
ladies’ club of two rooms. To one brilliant mind
it occurred, however, that if men were admitted
ladies could meet their husbands there, and under
the exhilarating expectation of seeing their own
husbands the married ladies revived and the risen
hair of spinsterhood settled down again. After
all this excitement it is lamentable to relate that
the club managed to get itself into rooms where
tall, black-hatted gentlemen appeared at unseemly
hours wanting Flossie (not knowing that Flossie
had moved), and smiled when informed that this
was a ladies’ club, saying that was just what they
expected: Several gentlemen wanted to join if
their wives could be kept out, and a mysterious
personage said he would come if he might appear
under the alias of Bloggs—but why Bloggs he
refused to state.
• * • •
The habit of giving troublesome hospital
patients in custody is spreading. A Melbourne
hospital made itself celebrated by sending for the
police to arrest a delirious inmate on a charge of
“disorderly conduct.” Narrabri (N.S.W.) has
gone one better. An aged patient, in the tailoring
business, wandered out of the hospital and sat
down in front of the premises, and an order was
issued that he shouldn’t be re-admitted, and
that the police should be sent for if he proved
“troublesome.” A policeman was sent for,
though the tailor seems just to have gone on
sitting, and to have given no great trouble in
any other sense ; and finding the patient
in a very dilapidated state the officer carried
him. back to the hospital. Being refused
admittance he humped his burden to the gaol
where “life was found to be extinct’’—partly
through old age. Whether the venerable tailor
only died after arrival at the gaol, or whether
the policeman had been carrying a corpse
around with him the published information
doesn’t show. Anyhow it is an uncomfortable
thought that any one of us, in his old age, may
have his remains held in an upright posi-
tion outside a hospital gate while a policeman
va-mly argues the point about having them ad-
mitted for further treatment and finds that it
can t be done because the remains, in their last
moments, violated rule 857^.
' * * .
At the recent Jingo meeting at Mel-
bourne Town Hall there was in the audience a
silent pair who seemed to understand nothing of
what was going on. They gave no sign of having
an opinion either way, and when the meeting
bellowed “God Save” at frequent intervals the
stoical pair remained silent and uncovered. Then,
in this free and enlightened land, they were set
upon by a howling crowd of well-dressed citizens ;
one was floored, seized by the crowd, and thrown
out of the hall, considerably injured. The other
was saved from damage by being collared by a
brawny policeman and hauled ignominiously to
the door. Even freedom of silence is suppressed
now when the Jingoes are gathered together,
• * * •
The N.S.W, military people—
Whilst observing the day of humiliation and prayer,
did not allow the necessary work of the contingents to
be interfered with.
That was the one work which was thought to be
so pleasing to the Almighty as not to call for
interruption.
* * * *
Sydney looked mournful on Wednesday
last, the day set apart for humiliation and prayer,
for the shops were closed, and the humiliated
ladies who do the Block had put on their best
clothes and gone haughtily to church. But “ down
the harbor” it was a real gala day, Even the
metropolis brightened up considerably in the
afternoon when the ambulance wont round to
remove plague cases. _ A big crowd gathered in
Jritt street, m the anxious hope of catching sight
of a patient as she was carried out, and through
this crowd a fruit-hawker moved selling 24 dis-
graceful bananas (in a bag) for sixpence. Every
tram brought up a fresh contingent of spectators,
and a small posse of police had hard work to keep
the ambulance from being rushed. It was almost
as good amusement as a body-dragging.
THE STEERSMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
via.
I saw the ship of Time go by,
And God was dreaming at the helm ;
Dreaming of old theology,
Asleep He sailed Pate’s stormy realm.
All sail was set; the masts above
Were bent by giant winds that fled
Across grey seas. A laughing Love
Sang songs unto the Pilot dread.
God slumbered on ; the vessel tacked
And veered from starboard round to port;
The sails were filled and then were backed,
White combers broke the decks athwart.
God was the crew and captain both,
And still He slumbered careless on ;
’Mid happy dreams He laid, unloth
If tempests blew or fair suns shone.
A Love lost holding now and then,
And now and then a Mortal fell ;
The sea was full of graves of men,
The beacon-light ahead was Hell.
Phalanxfed winds march’d o’er the sky ;
Great seas rose up to overwhelm—
I saw the Ship of Time drift by
With God still sleeping at the helm.

In the struggle to manufacture war heroes
this country does strange antics. Some time ago
a young Victorian joined the Bushmen’s con-
tingent and went to Africa. It isn’t recorded
that he did anything particular while he was with
the Bushmen, and he soon left them to run a farm.
While peacefully running the farm he went
bathing in a river and was drowned. And the
other day, at St. Kilda, 2000 people gathered to
unveil a monument to the young man who was
quietly drowned on a farm, and Treasurer Turner
delivered a stirring speech about him as one of
those_ who fell nobly and grandly in defence of the
Empire. Victoria produces a hundred more
remarkable heroes than that every year, and the
best that happens to them is that the push gets
up ashilling “soshul.”
* * mm.
The Australian generally flies to his revolver
when he is in love, or is jilted or deceived by his
best girl, but there was a notable instance to the
contrary in the recent inquest in Melb. on the
slaughtered butcher Latham. “ When you con-
fessed your infidelity to your husband, what did
he do ?” asked Elliott’s counsel of the woman in
the case. “I don’t remember,” replied Mrs.
Elliott, “he was busy at the time.” If Ibsen
were to make one of his characters postpone the
consideration of his wife’s unfaithfulness until
after he had hung the beef up in its proper place,
how would the critics view the matter ?
• * * *
The Victorian Inebriates’ Cure Committee
has had about 120 absolutely reliable remedies for
drunkenness offered it to date. The latest is the
cure by hypnotic suggestion, which comes via
America. It is cheap and easy. You catch your
dipsomaniac, fix him with your terrible eye, tell
him he can't taste, touch, or handle, and then turn
him loose. One sample collected from the Yarra
bank has been submitted. He was loaded with
the hypnotic impression that he utterly hated
drink, and, although he has since wandered into a
pub. by force of habit, the spell held good. He
ordered one long beer, lifted it to within an inch
of his chin, and then suddenly set it down again.
“By Jove!” he said, “I’d forgotten. I don't
like beer.” Then he passed out. This was a
man, the committee was assured, who couldn’t be
pulled off a pewter by an ox-team a month ago.
And yet the committee does not seem to think
much of hypnotic suggestion.
CRICKET ITEM.
A neat leg glance by Lil(ley).
At Weld Club, Perth, the servants are
Cninese, even to the billiard-marker. In the
early days of the club the white people available
as servants knew far too much of the history of
the first families, and the bland Chinese was about
the only waiter who could be bossed round by
the rising generation without talking back and
becoming reminiscent to an unpleasant extent.

How long ought the paternal strap to be
swung? To two rt. Australian country louts, each
2 1 years old, whom lie had just been compelled to
acquit without any credit to themselves, Chief
Justice Way said he hoped their fathers would
thrash them to within an inch of their lives. The
C.J. himself stands 4ft. llin. at least, and he
probably had no positive evidence that these two
fathers were any larger. If they weren’t, the
small C.J. was simply inciting them to rush on
suicide.
The other day Panton, P.M., sentenced
two men to six months’ hard for throwing-tobacco
over Melb. gaol wall. If either of them had
thrown his wife over he’d have got seven days.
DOUBTFUL IDENTITY.
INEBRIATED PEDGOGUE (not receiving expected
salute from the boys): “Do you know who I
am?”
1ST. BOY: “ Yes, you're the schoolmaster.”
2ND. BOY: “Say, Bill, he's got 'em again. He
don't know who he are!”
The satellites of Preacher Dowie, of
Chicago (U.S.) —the individual who flaps a pair of
red wings on the platform and says he is Elijah—
are deluging Victoria with literature like this,
from Leaves of Healing :
A Corn on tub Tob Healed in Answer to Prayer.
Writing at 1348 Michigan Avenue, Chicago, 111., Miss
Christine Erickson says :
“ I asked you to pray for a very bad corn on my toe
about one week ago, and the pain left it shortly after
that. East evening the corn came out, leaving a large
hole in my toe. I have no pain at all in the toe. I thank
God, and'thank you for praying for me. It was very
painful. It gathered and broke also.”
The corn on Christine’s toe away in Illinois is a
very remote excresence, and a more adjacent
bunion might do more to whoop, up the cause.
But the Dowie cult is growing even on evidence
like that. The rabbit-faced people with the re-
treating chins and the half-rapt, half-silly ex-
pression in their eyes—the people who look like
the dancing dervishes and the 70.1 naked prophets
of Israel, the folk with shambling knees and half-
open mouths and fish-like hands, the semi-
epileptic, the hysterical, the loose-jointed, the
people who nod their heads sideways when they
meet you, the half-finished citizens, the inhabi-
tants who have jumps and Can’t Keep Still, the
galvanic residents, and the fellow-creatures whose
arms are too long for their bodies, and whose
minds are away back in the trees where their re-
mote progenitors cultivated the higher branches of
knowledge—all these make a great congregation.
And when a vulgar charlatan comes to them with
two big wings on and a tale about a corn that was
cured by prayer in America, they say “ Lor ! ” or
some similar feeble exclamation, and fall in.
Two years have made a bit of difference
in the Jingoistic thermometer in Adelaide. Half-
holidays marked the departure of the earlier con-
tingents, and woe to the shopkeeper who traded
during the sacred hours set apart for the tramping
of swaddies through the street. This time there
was no half-holiday. One contingenter owed a
man some money, and promised to meet him next
night and settle up. The creditor kept tryst, and,
after long waiting, a friend happened along.
“ Why, they went away to-day,” said the friend.
“ Bet you drinks they didn’t,” said the creditor.
Then they asked several bystanders, who knew as
little about it as they did of Hittite hieroglyphics.
One man said “Damn 1” which, two years ago,
would have ended in a hospital case. A policeman
was handy ; he would know. “ They didn’t pass
nybeat,” said bobby. And so they kept on in-
juiring, until they found that the contingent had
really drifted away unnoticed, unhonored, un
kissed, without applause or beer, a dry, neglected
band. What a dropping away is here !
» * * •
Two consecutive items from a recent
Sydney D.T. summary column :■ —
The City Coroner yesterday initiated inquests on the
bidies of two women alleged to have died of illegal
operations.
Cardinal Moran yesterday laid a foundation-stone in
connection with the Waitara Foundling Home.
If the second par. mentioned that John See had
set in place the stone of a big State foundling-
asylum, the first par. would soon vanish from the
papers, of which it now forms almost a daily
feature.
According to the published accounts, the
great tenement-houses which O’Sullivan proposes
to build as working-men’s dwellings on the Rocks
(Sydney) are to be dreams of loveliness and
palaces of delight. About 500 families
are to be packed in these huge barracks,
one lot on top of another —but, then,
there are to be bathrooms, laun-
dries, hot-water apparatus, hanging
gardens (vaguely suggestive of
Babylon the Great), and other ele-
ments of Paradise. But, unfor-
tunately, all the hanging gardens
in the world won’t hang up the fact
that, in a cheap neighborhood, pro-
bably one house in every 10 or 20
contains a drunk on Saturday night,
and if you condense these drunks
into a huge tenement, then it is a
very drunk tenement. Also, every
time a plague outbreak causes
some row of terraces in a cheap
neighborhood to be overhauled,
the result is a revelation of
unspeakable filth—and if you
concentrate the filth of a dozen
, terraces into a huge tenement,
it will be a very dirty tene-
ment. The writer knows the
tenement house where it be-
longs — the dirty Glasgow
tenement house. There are,
perhaps, six families on each
flat. Each housewife has to
take her turn of cleaning the
stair, the front entry, and the
front steps twice a week, and
a large policeman indignantly
inspects it, says it isn’t done
well enough, hunts out the
defaulting female, and makes
her do it again, and interferes
in a way that would be more
tlum a policeman’s life is worth
in slovenly, ill-controlled Sydney.
And, despite all interference and super-
vision, the main products of the Scotch tene-
ment house are drunks, and strange, small
animals, and indignant females chastising their
large families on the common stairways. Unless
O’Sullivan has his tenement houses cleaned regu-
larly out of loan funds, his samples will probably
be the same —or thereabouts.
* * * *
Time was in Dunedin (M.L.) when the
“ unco’ guid ” were a power in that city of strong
whisky and Calvinism ; but the times are chang-
ing, judging by the experience of the last session of
the Council of Churches, an institution which
was once a power in the land. The chairman of
the Evangelical Campaign Committee resigned in
disgust at his inability to convene the committee,
which was otherwise occupied; the committees
are mostly laymen, and they won’t leave their
business to hold council nowadays. The rev.
head of the anti-gambling committee regretted
that he had been unable to get his little lot to-
gether ; the meeting had possibly been called on
a race-day when the committee had gone out to
the course to put its little bit on and see that it
got a run for its money. The Bible-in-Schools
junta failed to roll up. The convener of the
Temperance Committee did get a meeting, and it
made him wish he hadn’t; it consisted of two un-
interested-looking men who adjourned early.
Rev. Sinclair called together the Social Purity
committee and found himself the only attendant.
The one bright spot in the dry waste is the
fact that the purest person present was evidently
the Rev. Sinclair. When even Dunedin has thus
backslidden what hope is there that other towns
will slide forward ?
• * • *
Reported, with considerable circumstantial
detail and much horror, by Tas. newspapers, that,
on Governor Havelock’s recent tour through the
island State, the representative of the Cabinet
who acted as escort was, “all through the trip,
deliberately, to say the least, ignored by the lead-
ing members of the party.” Lands and Works
Mulcahy, the Minister in question, is rather a
genial little chap, but a bit fussy, and addicted to
somewhat extravagant fawning on dukes, kings,
emperors, and such. It may be that Mulcahy
overdid himself in obsequiousness, and Governo
Havelock found it necessary to check him with a
frozen stare and a cold, forbidding “ haw.”
The plant houses and fern conservatories
in the Botanical Gardens—one of the most appre-
ciated Sunday resorts of Melbourne—are crumb-
ling to decay with no chance of improvement, for
funds are slow in being allotted to Conservator
Guilfoyle’s department. Some day when Peacock
takes the missus and the kid to see the swans and
the big lily at the Gardens, and finds that they
have perished because the money was wanted for
African farm-burning, and the kid is removed
howling bitterly in disappointment and making
remarks about his father’s policy, a change for the
better may set in.
On the 19th, Thomas Donnelly was tried,
found guilty, and sentenced, at Sydney, for caus-
ing the death of Richard Nagle. Nagle —it was
shown in evidence —was helplessly drunk outside
an hotel in Castlereagh-street, when Donnelly
“ stoushed ” him. The police found Nagle insen-
sible (he had a fractured skull and a lacerated
brain), and took him to Sydney Hospital. A
doctor saw him, dressed his lip, which was cut,
but did not admit him. A constable then took
him in a cab to the lock-up, and Richard Nagle
(with a fractured skull and a lacerated brain) was
put in a cell. Next day, he was fined, and went
home. After several days of intense suffering he
was taken back again to Sydney Hospital, and
was told to wait upstairs, when a doctor would
attend to him. His wife left him for a while, and
when she returned, she found that he had been
allowed to wander away (still with his fractured
skull and his lacerated brain). Nagle did not turn
up again for two nights and a day. On the third
night Mrs. Nagle heard a rap at her door, and
found her husband on his knees, holding his head
with his hands. He was again taken to the
Sydney Hospital. The doctor, who held a post-
mortem on the body three hours after Nagle was
admitted, found the fractured skull and lacerated
brain before mentioned —for the first time ! All
these things considered, was Thomas Donnelly
the only man who should have been in the dock
charged with contributing to the death of Nagle

AT DARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Haste, O heart o’ me, darkness grows;
Leave your garden of riotous rose—
Velvet-black are the nets of leaves—
Nets that Night in the tall tree weaves
For the wind-held slave
That sighs to be free ;
And stirs with its wings the silver-white path on the
wave,
The boat of the one-day-old lingering moon, in the sea.
MERIDEN.
A PLAINT FROM THE SOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wind that blows from the North Country
Bring ye some word of my love to me ?
He sailed away on a feverish quest
Of a yellow dream in the barren West
He sailed away to the wintry South
To try his luck at a river’s mouth.
Then he heard the call of a Northern field
To chance anew what his luck might yield.
And I have not seen his face again—
Though I be waiting, I wait in vain 1
Then bring me, Wind from the North Country,
Some message of love to comfort me !
