A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, March 4, 1902.
Mr Dr.vr Arini, —
If you glance through a bundle of late London
newspaper's you will notice that they do not in the
least boom their plague of small-pox. Our papers
give columns, and scare headings, and sub-leaders,
and notes, and boom our plague to the verge of vain-
glory. Every Mayor or other public functionary
who goes on a tour of inspection travels with a full
chorus —aldermen, policemen, reporters, small
boys, and stray dogs. And most of what the prin-
cipals say gets into the printed page, and a fresh
lot of scared readers run away from the city, and a
lot who were coming into it go another way.
Anyhow, the exclamation “ Rats ! ” may no
longer be used to discredit a statement. Other
day in a side street of the city I stopped to hear a
bit of the Word as interpreted by a lunch-hour
preaoher. He was on the plague from a visita-
tion-of-God point of view, and mentioned that the
Almighty had employed rats and mice to spread
the disease amongst those who filched the Ark of
the Lord, and that he was employing the same
means to-day to punish a wicked city. A man on
the fringe of listeners offered the expounder fifty
to one that rats are not once mentioned in the
Scriptures. How the suggestion of a wager
aroused the sporting instinct in the kerbstone
congregation ! But the preacher was not pre-
pared to take the offer, and went on expounding,
probably Ashing that he had brought a con-
cordance.
When the first plague case was taken out of the
Criterion Hotel poor Sally Booth and Ada Lee,
then in good health, were entreated by the
managers of their company (Willoughby and
Geach) to leave the plaguey premises, but Miss
Booth scoffed at the request. She had been in
Bombay with plague all around her, &c., and she
wasn’t taking any now, &c. A few days later and
she and Miss Lee were stricken, and within the
week they were dead. Miss Booth was a grand-
niece of the great Sara Siddons. She was married
many years ago to an actor named Clyndes, who
had appeared in Australia, but husband and wife
had not met for a long time. Miss Ada Lee, a
sister of Jennie Lee, of Jo fame, was engaged to
be married to a man in South Africa. Only a few
days before her seizure she told an intimate friend
she had quite intended to go out to her fianci at
the close of the Arnold season, but that she
thought she would wait till The Wrong Mister
was Righted. Sally Booth’s age, 63, was against
her recovery, but Ada Lee, some 27 years younger,
was in splendid condition and looked fit to fight
all the bubonic microbes in Pitt-street. “ Wrong
Mr. Wright” will open at the Bijou Theatre,
Melbourne, on Easter Saturday. "
Miss Ruth Mackay, the terra-cotta complexioned
Egyptian Iras in “ Beil Hur,” is ruining her
beautiful mousseline-de-soie under-dresses by
wearing them a yard long on the ground in front
as well as at the back. The robs swathes her
ankles so tightly that she looks like a mermaid try-
ing to walk on her tail, and can only take a few
pinched, hobbled steps at a time; but she flops
gracefully, with the lazy grace of a big Persian
cat sampling different cushions. The sheik
Ilderim, with his great voice and fine large oath,
“ by the splendor of God,” is a great favorite with
the audience. Somehow he looks as if he ought
to make love to the Egyptian, and by-and-by
Othello her with his jewelled blade. The few-
lined part of Metellus—the Roman who comes off
deck to tell the Tribune that the pirates are in
sight, is done by a young M.L. singer, Sydney
Bell, who was heard once and remembered favor-
ably on a Sydney concert platform.
From the rising of the star in “Ben Hur” to
the last note of the Hymn of Praise on Olivet,
galleries, stalls and circle of Her Majesty’s
will go unfed. No more oranges, peanuts and
lemonade, or chocolates. No sandwiches and bis-
cuits behind the scenes. Any rat coming into the
theatre henceforth will have to bring his own
rations, like the crow that flew across the Shenan-
doah valley after the U.S. civil war. Even the
make-up vaselines and lards are now kept in ver-
min proof boxes. And every night after Romans,
Jews, Australians and all fancy nationalities have
departed, burning brimstone makes the theatre
smell like a terrestrial edition of the Pit.
Now the railway people know that a Cardinal’s
going away will draw a bigger crowd than the
Gov.-Gen.’s, or the Commonwealth Commandant’s
departures. What did that crowd of several
thousand persons which swamped the Redfern
platform on the occasion of Cardinal Moran’s
departure expect ? If the majority was of the
faithful, then doubtless pure love and affection
prompted the rush. But one has told
me that quite half the crowd had never seen
a Cardinal before, and was amazed to find a
dignified elderly gentleman instead of a stained-
glass window personage with no boots and a nim-
bus. in lieu of a hat. Scenes of hysterical
excitement occurred among women passengers
who could not get through the crowd to the
express platform until a body of police carried
them in. The State Premier and members of the
Ministry called and said “olive oil” before the
Cardinal left the presbytery.
The Great Smoky Mountains are a study in
black and tan, charcoaled tree-trunks, and yellow-
ochre foliage. If that old wooden approach that
precedes the Nepean River bridge is safe the
Commissioners ought to make the fact known.
Nervous or strange travellers nearly, get fits
just before that span is reached. An old traveller
on the line looks round the carriage and explains
“ they always slow down here, this patch isn’t
considered safe.” And he gets up and hangs out
of the window, watching for the timbers to give
way, and forecasting details of what would
happen, if they did. Presently he resumes his
seat with a sigh, as if disappointed that
he has again missed being a mangled remains.
Sparks from the railway engines start most of the
fires on the mountains. From Wentworth Falls
to Katoomba hundreds of navvies are working at
the duplicate line ; their camps are by the road-
side or down amongst the bush off the main
tracks. Some of the men are young settlers from
drought-stricken centres; they can do nothing
with their land till rain falls. But these are easily
picked out from the men who have been navvying
since boyhood, and whose fathers, perhaps,
navvyed before them. The man with the hoe
looks a G-reek warrior by the side of the man
with the shovel.
In a letter to Mr. See, the Gov.-General says
that he and Lady Hopetoun hope to be settled in
Sydney by the time the State Gov. arrives, and
that “ they will be delighted to receive Sir Harry
Rawson and party as their guests, to enable them
to look round and get completely settled in their
new home.” But the Rawsons ought to be able
to do their own “ looking round,” for “ Cran-
brook ’’ will be quite ready for them, down to
fresh violets on the dressing-tables and the after-
noon tea-kettle just on the boil. It is not likely
that they will be dumped down on the wharf, and
that Sir Harry will have to go house-hunting
whilst Lady Harry minds the luggage, the poodle
and the parrot. Also, “Lord Hopetoun wishes
to place his carriage and other things at the dis-
posal of the State Governor.” “ Other things ”is
good and comprehensive.
The convent at Wyalong was discovered to be
in flames at three a.m. the other week and,
according to the telegraphist, the nuns were in
some danger, for the building was encircled by a
wire net fence, on the inside of which the ladies
were huddled, unable to escape. This idea of a
nun-proof fence is funny. How had they got in ?
Or was the guard put up after they were in ? And
did the tradesmen throw their supplies over
the fence ? And were the children who attended
school thererthrown over also ? It is to be hoped
that those convents with nun-proof fences will
take a lesson from the Boers, and in future keep
a warrigal or two to stampede against the en-
trenchments in case of fire.
Awhile ago there was a fashionable wedding in
one of the States. But the amber of the bride-
groom’s moon was spoilt by a butterfly, who
would not fold her wings and keep quiet without
a big money inducement. The money was paid to
a, solicitor acting for the ’fly, and now comes the
news that he has flown with the whole amount.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Politicians, actors, and a great muster of
smart people saw the beginning of the final test-
match at the M.C.C. grounds, on Friday after-
noon. Several unremarkable-looking members
of ‘ Sweet Nell ’ watched the play from under
the awning—the dress-circle of the grounds.
Tasmanian ex-Premier Braddon, looking less
shaggy than usual in his locks and very stooped in
the shoulders, moved round in a mystified manner
under the pilotage of the brisk but old-fashioned
figure of ex-Premier Siiiels. Sliiels is much the
younger of the two, but he is ten times more ‘ ex.’
than Braddon, despite his semi-ancient youth.
Better-not Wally had a front seat in a sunny part
of the reserve. Mrs. Philson—the only other
member of the Governor-General’s party present
—was in dowdy garb with Mrs. Dunbar Hooper
—a smarter vision in cream. Up aloft in the
stand, out of everyone’s range, were perched the
Governor Clarkes. There was a vast muslin
brigade of whom Miss Vera Clarke, Miss Daisy
Chomley, Miss Rita Kiddle, and Miss Guthrie
had the best claims to smartness. Miss Fisken
was one of the neatest of corseted figures in a
wonderfully-fitting frock of grey voile, slashed
with lace hurdles.
“ The M.C.C. girls made a bid for fresh laurels
in rose bloom and smart millinery at the test-
mitch. The frocks were of familiar summer
build, airy about the neck, scant about the hips,
large in the waist. But the top rigging was all of
autumn architecture. The majority consisted of
soup-plate hats abruptly banked up in the neck
with plots of bright blooms, or bunches of radiant
ribbon—nothing breaking in on the front blank-
ness. W^it o gossamers—dear friends of the bad
complexion ! —covered every second face. The
Jewish maiden was everywhere—dressed in the
height of fashion, with jingling wrist and neck
trinkets, and generally inviting attention to her
luxurious appointments. Captain Maclaren is
not popular with the Melbourne crowd. He puts
on side and arrogates much to himself. Gunn
does not go off well. He is shy, and shrinks from
feminine advances. Braund is considered a
‘ splendid chap,’ and is a tremendous favorite with
a certain Toorak set.
“Small boys have their uses, after all. They
often serve as the sprat to catch the mackerel of
the smart girl. At the cricket matches it has been
sport to watch the ingenuous youth make overtures
to the champions of the pitch, and after some con-
versation (?) tow them over to the party of guile-
less angels who were so impatiently waiting for
the prize. One tall, skinny youth, well-known to
all the tea rooms, makes himself invaluable in this
way. He touts for “good things” in modern
hims, 'and then engineers them to the company of
some of his smart girl friends who call him a dear
boy, and pet him ridiculously, and turn their
backs immediately to bask in the light of the in-
troduced one. Can American ‘cuteness’ surpass
the Melbourne girl’s guile ? She would turn even
the pips and stem of the fruit of knowledge to
good account.
“ The fierce sun that beat upon the first of the
Autumn races at Flemington called for universal
light raiment. The transparent yoke and sleeves
flourished, and the protective gossamer was
first favorite of complexion covers. The Guv’nor
Clarkes and the Commandant Huttons were the
stars of the occasion—but they failed to dazzle the
crowd. The theatrical element prevailed for
dressiness and interest. Nellie Stewart was a
strange, weird, wonderful vision in cream—a
curious garb buttoned up the front, and spreading
smoothly over hips and back in a way that made
people tremble for its staying powers. A stoop
forward —and the tragedy of the day would have
occurred. A very shady hat was abloom with
lilac ; Nellie’s day complexion is a marvel in flesh
tints, but one is never vouchsafed a satisfying
look at it. Lauri had Carrie Moore, very sallow-
looking in strawberry and black, at one elbow, and
Maud Chetwynd, in shining white silk, at the
other. Florence Young was buttoned up the back
in shrimp pink, and her figure was tightly laced.
Little Sheridan cut the dash of the male mum-
mers. He was in large checks, the fancy vest
being elaborately braided. Johnnie Sheridan has
a lusty taste for suit trimmings.
“Sassiety was in full force. Mrs. Harry
Osborne boasted the complexion of the day.
When everyone had given up the task of powder-
ing and trying to keep in crimp, the jolly member
of the Madden family showed an unruffled pink
and white complexion! a coiffure in crimp, and a
cool, calm brow. Her duck-egg green garb helped
the crisp effect. Miss Ruby Madden simmered in
white. Miss Muriel Williams tossed a saucy head
that was mounted in nutmeg tulle and pink daisy
punctuations, and which rose out of a collarless
white muslin frock. Mrs. Robert Power carried
the gamp of the day—a colossal affair in green
and white linen, edged with a kind of pillow-slip
frill. Miss Mars Buckley sported the latest of
London conceits—a curious grey linen elaborated
with eyelet holes.
“Col. Tom Price’s daughter—Mrs. Ainslee
Mills, was a wintry figure in heavy grey
freize and a fiery hat of squat shape in scar-
let velvet. Mrs. Mills, senior, was the grandest
dame on the terrace. She wore a superb gown of
cornflower blue voile, with plenty of silk beneath,
and ornamental strips of expensive-looking lace.
A toque, showing blush roses in front,, was
skewered at a very smart angle. Mrs. Roy
Greene was fair to look upon in white, with a
tricky hat slapped back from the face. Mrs.
Lawson blazed in blue, and Mrs. Ernest Raleigh
appealed to a world that likes pallor and lean
lines in a frilly garb of cream, with flowing angel
sleeves and sac blouse of Oriental silk and lace.
Several bookies’ wives and two or three dress
designers flashed on. the terrace now and then in
raiment that surpassed the sumptuous Solomon
in all his glory. One of these wept copiously at
losing on all the events of the day. She appeared
unashamed on the terrace, with swollen eye-lids
and red nose, dragging disconsolately after her a
train of chiffon and flowered silk worth a power
of money.
‘ ‘ The Hobart season for Melbourne and Sydney
girls has resulted in the expected crop of engage-
ments. It was a foregone conclusion that the
naval youths of the Squadron should fall victims
more readily than the wily landsmen to the en-
chantments of that determined army of sirens.
And they have—two cases being fatal, the engage-
ments duly ratified. One of the successful sirens
gave a Thanksgiving Service in the form of a tea-
room party the other afternoon, all her less fortu-
nate friends being expected to make merry that
unto one net a man had come. There is no more
difficult task in the world than to try and see
happiness, as Father Shakespeare pointed out,
through another’s eyes—especially through a
woman’s eyes. The girl who, after a campaign of
failure, can look bright and pleasant at the cele-
bration of another’s success is a dark dissimulator.
“ Talking of tea-rooms. This summer season
has destroyed the ice-cream and soda-squash con-
sumption. The days have been too cool for iced
drinks, and yet too warm, in a way, for afternoon-
tea. So the tea-tables have been deserted, and
one well-known place in the Block, run by a
young and winsome widow, has been actually
closed up. Quite sad to see the windows in mourn-
ing behind bereavement shutters—windows once so
gay with bright draperies and the glimpses they
revealed of the animated scenes within. At an-
other establishment the band has been struck off
the list of attractions for economical reasons, and
luxuries have been cut down on all sides in these
bun coops. However, the Melbourne tea-room
will always be a going concern as long as the cuff-
and-collar brigade is attracted by the pretty
Hebes who do the table-waiting.
‘ ‘ Hughie Trumble has good taste and as dis-
criminating an eye. for a figure as for a cricket-
stump. Miss Florence Christian, whom he is going
to marry on the 12th, is a pretty little girl, and a
moneyed little girl, in height about passing
Hughie’s elbow. But Hughie is such a towering
figure—over six feet two when standing up to
every inch. As a rule, he stoops, and slouches
rather in walking. One imagines how awkwardly
the favorite cricketer will face the chant of ‘The
Voice that breathed o’er Eden’ for the greatest
test-match of all. Fortunately, the ceremony
comes off at a distant -church, or the Melbourne
populace would about crowd Hughie out from his
own wedding. The Christians are wealthy people,
and have travelled a. bit. It was on the last
voyage of the Australian Eleven from ’Ome that
Hugh Trumble met his fate, and shyly returned
to Melbourne branded ‘ Engaged.’
“The engagement of Miss Ethel Fitzgerald,
eldest daughter of' Melbourne’s most important
surgeon, to Commander Lumsdaine, of the Royal
Arthur, is the most interesting of all recent con-
tracts. Miss Fitzgerald is her father’s right hand
—a sensible sort of maiden who plays tennis well,
makes pets of poodles, but otherwise adorns
‘ Rostella, ’ the well-appointed residence of surgeon
Fitzgerald, with her practical young person in a
way that is quoted as a model of good manage-
ment by all the smart mothers about. Very black
hair, a pair of grey eyes, and a pale complexion
are sufficient to cause her friends to talk of her
Irish beauty.
Perhaps it is as well the dowager of ‘Clive-
den is off to Europe, for some Melbourne people
are complaining that she sets too exalted a value
on her natty self. The latest royal act of the
dowager s was to summon stage-manager Tapping
after the performance of 1 Sweet Nell ’ to express
her approval of the dainty little show.
“Melbourne Sassiety is quite agitated that a
cousinly couple of much prominence have ‘ made
it up again.’ The climax was reached at ‘Sweet
Nell the other night, when the wealthy moth
handed up a posy tied with flaming ribbons to
match his relative’s gorgeous gown.
Benjamin Hoare, the churchy-looking Age
leader-writer, is going to try matrimony
again. The lady is an old friend and playmate—
or something of the sort, from Geelong— Miss
Emily Tuite. As both are members of the Lenten
religion, the knot-tying has to hang over till
Easter Tuesday, when the season of sackcloth and
ashes is done with. The lady is of towering
height, and healthy Irish spirits.
“In ‘Sweet Nell,’ it seems rather a weak point
to make Lady Gastlemaine and the Duchess of
Portsmouth (Gwynne’s rivals in Charles’ favor)
such unattractive frumps. It would be all the more
to Nell’s credit if she could beat two charming and
wily women on their own ground. As it is. her
victory is cut-and-dried from the start. Always
so in plays! In ‘Sans Gene,’ Napoleon’s sisters
try to crush the ex-washerwoman, but she is
given all the fat by the play-wright in the shape
of telling things to say, while the Buonoparte
sisters are doomed to sputter spitefully like angry
cats. Also Sans-Gene is radiant in white satin,
diamonds and golden wig, while her tormentors
are pallid in funereal splendor and inky Empire
cork-screws to match. A stage conversational
duel between three rivals for a king’s favor would
be warm work if the combatants were all treated
fairly. Castlemaine and Portsmouth must have
been attractive or Charles would have sent them
packing, and, as a matter of fact.it was the Duchess,
not Nell Gwynne, who hung weeping over the
king’s death-bed. '
“ I learn that the local clergy resent the present
Nell Gwynne cult. The vision of a historic
sinner, enthroned at a king’s left hand, in a heaven
of haberdashery, is apt, in the case of slie-
beholders, to draw the sting from sermons about
the Unprofitableness of sin and the alleged fact
that iniquity is a drug in a falling market.
“One of the biggest fish hooked to date by
‘Rita,’ of the Herald, with her artful little bait
of a letter asking ‘ Society women ’ for their views
on domesticity and the kitchen-range, is Mrs.
Claude Macdonald, of Wantabadgery, N.S.W.,
formerly Miss Green, the Society clothes-builder,
who good-naturedly penned a gratis col. on how
she thinks the daily tucker ought to be served.
Mrs. C.M.’s advice is, briefly summed up: ‘Keep
up to date ; get a move on you, and don’t be
wedded to the same meals year in, year out.’
‘ln most houses they dine to-day as yesterday
and many yesterdays. Every day new notions
are introduced in the shops ; new potato chippers,
new boning knives, new ice-cream freezers.’ The
only drawback to above good advice is that the
said luxuries and conveniences are only within
reach of the lofty circles. The humble thousands
battle along as best they can with six injured-
looking saucepans and a canvas butter cooler.
And while meat is at current rates, they’re only
too glad to keep the bones inside to make the
joint look larger, and they buy their ice-cream
by the penn’orth from the remote descendant of
Julius Csesar with the gaudy cart.
“ A quite large audience turns up now in Melb.
Town Hall on organ-playing days. Organist
Mansley _ Greer, who was pedalling away at the
cross-grained old instrument on Thursday last,
had a numerous clientele listening to him.
Mansley G. is choir boss at Wesley church; young,
slim and enthusiastic. His hearers were mostly
men. Most extraordinary thing, men shunt
all kinds of music but that of the organ. It’s the
downright hard graft of playing it that appeals to
them, 1 believe. They look up to the organist as
they do to Clem Hill or Trumble.
“ The charming manners and sensitive delicacy
of the Woman’s Hospital Committee led two of
its members into serious trouble once. After
marching through a ward and putting Mary Jones
and Ellen Brown on the well-oiled rack of inquiry,
they graciously smiled on Mrs. Smith, and
bustled out, announcing in loud and shocked tones
what a shame it was that that nice little woman
should have to herd with these disgraceful
creatures. It was a mere detail—but Mrs. Smith
had been some years a widow. Great are the uses
of a prefix !
“ What do you think of this as the very cream
of social amenities? The other day, a milk-
vendor’s wife called at the house of some new resi-
dents in her locality, and politely solicited their
custom, but was told by the maid that another
milkman had already secured the order. ‘ Then,’
rejoined the cream canvasser, ‘give your mistress
my At Home card, and tell her that I shall be
glad to forget everything but the fact that we are
neighbors. I hope she will call. Thursday’s my
day. Good afternoon ’; and the lady with the
social intentions made a dignified retirement from
the suddenly-slammed door. And this in Mel-
bourne, where the Toorak dame haughtily passes,
with a vacant stare, her own face artiste and the
tailor who has been handling her figure for cloth
creations.

Related was it of Jay Gould (?) that his
handwriting was so bad as to be illegible to the
old man himself. A letter of his replying to an
application for employment on one of his numerous
railways was on the strength of the signature
used by the recipient as- a pass over the line for
many years. In later days the typewriter has
done much to prevent fraud and mistakes
formerly traceable to bad writing. Had J.G.,
like The Bulletin, used a “New Century”
machine his reputation as a letter writer would be
better to day. New Century Typewriters, the
latest from Yankee-land, are sold only by the
United Typewriter and Supplies Co., of Sydney,
Melbourne and Perth.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Westralian Worker alludes to a well-
known N.S.W. politician as “ this miserable
convex-bellied swamp-eel.”
It is a certainty that, as High Commis-
sioner, Forrest, K.C.M.G., would be the worst
form of Jingo greaser. Wise on the other hand,
is too slippery to be reliably greasy.
Sydney Rabbi Davis spread himself into
Hebrew poetry in his prayer for rain. It was
distinctly dithyrambic, but, after soaring among
“ the Pleiades ” and the “ bands of Orion,” and so
on, came down to Earth with a heavy thud by
addressing the Lord “ who stayeth the bottles of
Heaven.”
Clement Wragge gave as his N.S.W. fore-
cast for the 24 hours after Humiliation Day:
“No data available owing to the observance of
the Day of Humiliation and Prayers for rain.”
On the Saturday following he predicted “ probable
rains working from the Southward owing to purely
physical conditions .”
Melbourne University embezzler, Dickson,
was no exception to the rule among neat young
men who fall from grace—he kept a straight
upper lip until the last moment. Only when be
was handcuffed to a particularly uncouth specimen
of criminal with an artful dodger leer did it seem
to dawn on the sprucely tailored accountant that
things were not as they had been.
Radical politicians, and politicians who
want the Radical - vote, have long realised the
value of a familiar name—“ Toby ” Barton and
“ Charley ” Kingston, for instance. But here and
there the more daring Laborite goes one better,
and signs himself that way. Thus Broken Hill’s
municipal notices—Broken Hill still has a Labor
Mayor—are just now subscribed “Tom Jackson.”

Old Samuel Davenport still appears in
public, although he was 84 on March 5. He
was called to act as one of the vice- regal
advisors in 1845, on© of his colleagues being
Charles Sturt, the celebrated explorer. His wife,
whom he married early in 1842, died last month.
Davenport is the only surviving member of the
Legislative Council which, on J an. 2, 1856, passed
the S. A. Constitution Act.
Dr. A. Oronhyatekha, Australian manager
of the Independent Order of Foresters’ Life
Assurance Office, has been gaoled for three
months, in Melbourne, for assault on his_ type-
writer girl. The Mohawk gentleman defended
himself vigorously, but possibly the jury had read
the advertisement about the 1.0. F. ’s alleged
£1,030,000 surplus, and had lost faith in every-
body responsible for the concern.
A case came before a Melb. court, the other
day, which resembles in a small way that of
Peace, the famous English burglar, who lived in
a mansion and was looked up to by his neighbors.
There were 30 charges of larceny from backyards
against Leonard Fairhall, who was leading a re
putable life as a hard-working gardener in receipt
■" of good wages, and who not very long ago was
warmly recommended for the position of verger
at a suburban church. His dual career was cut
short, and for the next two years the only yard he
will be in will be the one at the gaol.
Robert Byrne, aged 80, and once worth
£200,000 or so, applied for a certificate of dis-
charge in Melbourne Insolvency Court last Friday.
Byrne was Treasurer for a few weeks m Mac-
pherson’s Ministry in 1869-1870, but his consti-
tuents ejected him when he went for re-election,
and Graham Berry succeeded. The Ministry
only stood for seven months in all. That was
Byrne’s only experience of what the late Parkes
used to describe as “hoffis.” He left politics, and
Berry stayed in them, and the results have been
none too good in either case.
“Cover the bald and cure the deaf,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ and kudos and coin shall be thine for ever more,
for your patrons will include, on proof of genuine
efficacy, the King and Queen. It seems sure to
certainty that both these irritating cursed pin-
pricks of life to-day will be removed at no cen-
tury-hence fieriod, and the rewards will be
enormous. Queen Alexandra can hear well by
telephone or electrophone, and through the latter
she enjoys the opera, as do hundreds of the
wealthy afflicted here. There’s a hint to elec-
tricians.”
A curious commentary on the present state
of law was that given at the trial of Dickson, late
accountant of Melb. University, for fraud. While
the first charge was under consideration, Dickson
and Dickson’s counsel made strong assertions of
innocence, claiming that all the manipulation of
the books and documents was merely to cover
careless bookkeeping. “From beginning to end
nothing was further from my mind than to rob the
institution,” concluded the prisoner. The jury re-
turned a verdict of guilty, and Dickson, who had
just asserted that “From beginning to end, etc.”,
promptly pleaded guilty to three further charges
of embezzlement and larceny.
Judge Johnston, now permanently raised
to Yic. County Court Bench, is a son of an ex-
proprietor of the Argus, and to that respectable
journal’s influence he owes much of his success in
life. He is one of the stolidest and most plodding
men who ever made a jury wish it was dead. He
always held the Crown briefs, and now gets a
position over the heads of many senior lawyers,
who, in the present “rockiness” of the “profesh,”
would have jumped at a place worth £l5OO a
year, with a prospective pension of £750. John-
ston’s only political escapade was an attack on
North Gippsland—Allan M‘Lean’s stronghold ;
but the Hielander defeated him by nearly three
to one.
A Sydney suburb was suddenly aroused
the other Sunday about 7 a.m. by a fusilade of
pistol shots. The natives, disturbed from their
rest, rushed to their doors and windows and be-
held an excited M.L. A. in pyjamas hanging on to
his front gate busy emptying a revolver, ap-
parently at the houses opposite. Investigation
proved that his target was a dog fight. Next
door’s mastiff was engaged in making mince-meat
of his pet fox-terrier, and he adopted this method
of rescue. None of the shots went near their
mark, and no casualties were reported, except
from the firing line which tore its pyjamas on the
gate. The disgusted neighborhood, which ex-
pected a tragedy, went sulkily back to its Sunday
morning nap sarcastically recommending the
M.L.A. to get a pom-pom to protect his pup.
Some years back, C. 0. Kingston was told
at an election meeting by a swan-gizzarded bank-
clerk that he (Kingston) had “a neck to come
there for votes.” Kingston regarded him for a
second with That Look; then spoke out: “My
friend, had I a fathom of uncomely turkey gobbler
larynx like yours, I’d have tied a slip-knot in it long
ago and hanged myself for the benefit of the land-
scape ! ” The discussion then ended.
A now intensely dignified State official
was very raw material when first appointed as a
wild and whirling youth in the N.S.W. public
service. He announced an ex-Premier as “a
bloke out here to see you, sir.” Shortly after he
staggrered the very great boss potentate of the
department by breathing over his shoulder in a
leery, confidential manner : “A young lady out-
side wants to see you, sir; not a bad-lookin’ little
piece, either.” The “piece” happened to be the
boss’s daughter and the youth nearly ended his
Eublic career there and then. If he had he might
ave taken the downward road that leads to
driving a cab instead of rising to be one of the
most inaccessible dignitaries in the service.
John Lucas, M.L.C., who has passed
away, at 84, was a sort of colossal obelisk of old
Sydney. Forty years ago he was a political wire-
puller of the cutest kind. He was a cousin of
George Lucas, who died lately at 87, and also a
cousin of ex-Premier James Squire Farnell. John
Lucas decorated Parliament more or less since
1860, and he once held office for two years, as
Minister for Mines under Robertson. He was
not invited to hold a portfolio again. For the
last dozen years at least John Lucas would have
been impossible as an elected representative of
the people—he never adapted himself to the new
order of things. Yet he made or hindered laws
for N.S.W. in the Upper House for 22 years
without the people’s permission being asked.
One of the most paragraphed persons
to take part in the Nelson (M.L.) Diamond
Jubilee celebrations lately was Julia Martin,
the “Grace Darling of Maoriland.” She
was allotted a carriage in the procession
and was presented to visiting notables. In
’63 the brigantine Delaware was wrecked
during a gale on the rocky coast between
Pepin’s Island and Wakapuaka, and there
appeared to be no hope of saving the crew.
The mate tried to swim through the surf to
the shore, but was dashed back on board
again more dead than alive. Five Maoris
appeared on the beach, four men and a
young woman, and, led by the latter, they
swam to a rock near the ship. A line was
thrown them and they swam ashore with
it. Two held the shore end while the other
three, including Julia, swam out and as-
sisted the seamen ashore. A gold watch
and £2O were bestowed on Julia by an
admiring country.
“ Allarym ” : Bulletin says Reid is
a failure as leader of the Opposition. He
may be, but cocky little M‘Millan is worse.
M‘Millan, when acting leader, constantly
sets the teeth of the democratic section of
his party on edge and causes much squirm-
ing in the Labor corner. A N.S.W. Foreign-
trade Labor Representative told writer that
M‘Mi I lan’s overbearing high - and - mighty
condescending treatment of Laborites
nearly caused a rupture on several occa-
sions. Also the absentee-importer’s dele-
gate has an aggravating habit of saying:
“ The proposal is preposterous. It is abso-
lutely opposed to reason and commonsense.
No sane man could vote for such a thing.”
It isn’t so much the saying of this as the
nasty way he says it. A sour, cocky little
man, who wears his silk hat on his left ear, and
who looks down with a shudder on the bulk of his
own party from the pinnacle of his imported bale
of calico isn’t likely to be popular.
David Syme’s son, Geoffrey, prospective
literary director of Melb. Age, who has been for
twelve months broadening his views by travel,
has selected an English bride for himself. Geof-
frey spent much time doing work for a London
paper in order to pick up English press methods.
He is one of three sons whom David keeps at the
Age grindstone.
Arthur Griffith, M.P. (N.S.W ) frightened
the wits out of his first Labor audience with his
dandified airs, but the Waratah workmen sat
tight with their eyes shut, and heard sincerity from
the young fop. But if they could have applied
the Hare system of voting to him, they would
have rejected his three-inch collar, and drawn a
blue mark across his shirt-front, and indignantly
thrown out his tan boots, and sent him into the
House clothed only in his native diffidence. Now
they don’t mind, for with all his frill Griffith is
one of the best—as sturdy a radical as there is in
any Australian Parliament.
A well-known Yic. squatter, who recently
wrote the Lands Department to pay off some
money due, began civilly enough by saying,
“Enclosed please find my cheque for
£154 17s. 6d.” Then his feelings got the
better of him, and the good old man of
acres added, “You and your d d offi-
cers can conie and take the blanky land if
you like! ”

Maorilaud is beginning to fancy that some-
thing will have to be done to Judge Conolly, a
learned and irritable gentleman in nis 81st year.
Conolly appears to suffer from a conviction that
everybody is guilty, and from great indignation
because other people don’t always see it.
W. Dick, M.L. A., Newcastle, went straight
from the University (where he attained honors in
mathematics and science) to wharf-laboring.
After several months hard graft he started a
private school and had one pupil the first six
months ! A free night school for men, which he
ran at the same time, was crowded, and one of his
pleasantest recollections is the polite way “the
boys ” spat into their hats rather than dirty the
school floor.
Premier Seddon’s enemies may scoff, but
the Bun Tuck man is still a power. During one
of his recent tours he received a telegram which
read : “ Registrar’s office closed for last two days.
Cannot get married. Please fix matter up.” Bun-
Tuck did it —that Registrar opened suddenly.
Somewhere in the Otira Gorge a telegraph mes-
senger overtook him with another missive : “My
husband enlisted in Bth contingent. Stop the
brute.” Bun-Tuck did it. At Kumaraa wire
arrived, evidently from an enemy: “Wife bad
twins to-day—both girls; wanted a boy. Please
arrange.” But this time Bun-Tuck didn’t do it.
Perth has its “ disgustingly rich ” man,
too, in George Shenton “ Knight,” as he delights
to be addressed. He has an income of =£20,000 a
year, and candidly admits he doesn’t know how to
get rid of it. He gives lavishly to the religious
body to which he belongs—the wealthy Wesleyan
sect—but he does not entertain nor help up strug-
gling charities or suffering individuals. It is told
of him that he bought a carriage and a coat of
arms, which latter his brother proposed to use as
being the “ family escutcheon.” “Yer can’t do
that, Ned!” said Georgie. “Oh! yer can’t do
it, yer know. Leastways, not unless you have a
bar sinister on it.” A few people laughed when
they heard this, and the rest of Perth ran to look
up the bar sinister in its dictionary to see where
the joke came in.

“I am a victim of over-legislation! ”
old man, who rules a population that would about
make a second-class British city, trying to pose as
a Bismarck or a Pitt, making more war orations
than there are men in all his contingents, hustling
up and down his little State like a breathless ant
sprinting up and down its blade of grass, dropping
the democraticprinciplesof alifetimeinthefussand
heat and struggle of despatching another two men
and a dog, and behaving generally like a church-
warden in armour or a grocer at the mouth of a
disused cannon ! It is a sorrowful and undignified
close to a great career. Seddon is a weary old man
now who doesn’t get his proper sleep through
flying from place to place, getting off the same
old oration about the “Empire”—an old man
who is talking himself to death—a Wandering
Jew whose whitened beard looms through the
dusk on every high-road as he sprints sleeplessly
along like an unquiet spirit to make his great
speech about the Empire all over again, with
hardlv anybody listening all the while. And to
Joe Chamberlain, who runs England now, he is
just about as important as one of the screws that
Joseph used to make in Birmingham.

The widow of District Court Judge
Callaghan, now forty years dead, is still a resi-
dent of Ranrl wick, near Sydney. She was a
daughter of Justice Milford, of the Queensland
Bench, 'or of the Bench in the Moreton Bay
district, as it was then called. Callaghan was a
Dublin man, who achieved some distinction as
author of a legal work. He was killed while on
circuit at Braidwood by the kick of a horse.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
S.S. : Be that Victorian couple whose
gamp took fire by reason of the sun’s rays and was
burned to the ground. I remember the case of a
man up Bundaberg way whose whiskers suddenly
burst into flame at noonday when he was taking a
bath m the sea. It was subsequently found that
the conflagration was caused by the sun’s rays
reflected from the ocean.

During the first day’s running of
the steam trams at Broken Hill, the Barrier
urchins simply overwhelmed the officials,
swarmed all over the cars, and amused themselves
by hopping off and on while the tram was in rapid
motion. A struggling mass of kid stood on the
footboards, and cheerfully defied the conductor to
collect their fares or the police to shift them off.
The luck of the great aggregate kid was good, and
the one aocident happened to a man, who got in-
terested as to the whereabouts of a nipper that
had tumbled off, with the result that he himself
got among the wheels. The Sydney fellows who
went up to run the trams were satisfied that the
Barrier kid is in a class by himself. Local resi-
dents ascribe this to the fact that many of the
youngsters are reared on goat’s milk.

Myall : Cannot any horse-doctor dis-
cover a cure for the effects of Darling pea ? Waa
recently passing through a
selector’s paddock in Coon-
amble district, when my at-
tention was attracted to the
peculiar capers of a horse at-
tached to an old dray a short
distance off the road. The
animal was plunging and
rearing in a most eccentric
manner, the while a small
boy in charge was ministering
to it with the back of a long-
handled shovel. Above the
rattle of the dray and the
thuds of the persuasive weapon
could be heard the screech : “Yer
blanky pea-struck cow! ” Even-
tually the gee-gee decided on a short
sprint through the bush, and finished
up by landing himself and dray in a
huddled heap amongst the fallen timber.
I learnt that about four-fifths of the
horses in the district are this season
suffering from the effects of the pea,
pobably owing to the scarcity of feed.
The malady is, so far, incurable, and.
though the animal affected may enjoy
long lucid intervals, like George 111.,
there is no telling when he will go crazy
again, and become a snake-haunted horse
tunning amok.

“ Elma ” on old St. Kilda :
Sight of an omnibus on St. Kilda Road, the other day,
recalled old times decades ago, when the coaches ran from
St. Kilda in and out of town over bush tracks, past over-
hanging gum and wattle trees, and swamp land crowded
with water-fowl. In 1851 the Nicholsons were about the
only people living at St. Kilda. Afterwards the bush-
rangers haunted the environs of Melbourne ; they took
possession of theswamp, nowthepark, and bailed up people
who tried to get to their suburban houses. One day they
had 14 people tied to different trees on the site of the
present park. Nicholson always escaped the attacks of
the bushrangers. He was a man of great charity (at the
time of the great rush to Melbourne he threw open his
store for the free accommodation of the homeless), and
the bushrangers spared him—tempting booty though he
might have provided.
• • * •
“ Weider ” re calf-planting and other
matters: —
I think “ Jack-a-01ubs’” method has been rather
severely condemned. It acts very well in most cases,
but, of course, there are some cows too cunning to be
taken in. I have tried “ E.X.O.’s ” idea, taking a dog,
but that fails more often than the other way, as the cow
will chase the dog in any direction from her calf.
“ Meera” is right about an experienced cow leading you
on a blind trail. “ Mulga Jack’s” way is the best so far
—to watch the cow all day, but that does not pay if your
time is money. I have tried all ways, and I find the
best and cheapest is to yard the cow over night, and let
her go at daybreak the next morning, and she will make
a bee-line to her offspring. I have never known this
device to fail. Re the ’roo lapping. “ Werrimee "is
right, the ’roo does lap his drink. I have a tame one,
and just out of curiosity I got a deep glass dish,
filled it with water, and watched the ’roo drink. He
puts his lips slightly in the water and then out darts his
tongue. I could not hear any lapping noise, and I would
not have believed he lapped if I had not seen his tongue
through the glass.

Fraulem : Have never seen any printed
reference to the kangaroo-fly. It is of slaty-brown
color, somewhat smaller than the common house-
fly, and it invariably accompanies the kangaroo.
When our dogs would get away out of sight on a
chase, we would search them well when they re-
turned, and if a ’roo fly was found on any of them,
we knew there had been a kill and searched round
until we found the ’roo. !Mo fly; no search.

“ Riverina ”: Re mirages. A com-
mon sight is a clump of trees, a strip of
sky above it, and then another clump
of trees, the exact counterpart of the
first, above that again. Have been in-
formed, ureliably, that when dropping
down (lost and almost beaten) on an
Australian plain, a swagman saw a pic-
ture in the sky of an old mate of his walk-
ing into a public-house with some friends.
The weary one staggered to his feet and
managed to travel 15 miles in the direc-
tion of the reflection, when he struck a
publichouse, only to find that it was the
wrong one. The pub. he had seen in his
vision was Casey’s, 14 miles further on.
This item doesn’t cast much new light
on the subject of mirages, but as an
essay on the disgraceful scarcity of public-
houses it is all there.
* *• • •
“Bob R.” : Can endorse your re-
marks (B. 15/2/’O2) re the wholesale de-
struction of the N.S. Wales forests. In
the Temora (N.S.W.) district, there ex-
isted a magnificent forest of ironbark, but
it has been ruthlessly destroyed, the finishing
stroke being put on it when the Railway authori-
ties permitted the cutting of “round-back”
sleepers. The young growing trees were then
attacked, even if only one sleeper could be cut
from each. The result is that the forest is gone
beyond redemption. The Forestry Department
is going to appoint a commission to inquire into
the preservation of our forests. It will take that
commission all its time to find forests to inquire
into now.

** G.S.S.” : Nest-mak-
ing is a characteristic
of cat-fish. The nest
is generally a ring
of small stones
or pebbles, where
the spawn is de-
posited in season,
and where the
fish “sleeps” a
good deal during
the day. Where
stones are scarce
the fish shoves
or rolls pebbles
along with its
mouth to the
desired spot.
The nest is
generally cir-
cular or oval
in shape, and
is made near
the shore.
“Hi-Yah”: Re M'Cooey’s recent out-
break, or portion thereof (B. 14/12/’01) I don’t be-
lieve he ever saw a full-grown eel. A man caught
one the other night at the Bairnsdale (Vic.) wharf
which scaled 251 b. It is a common thing in the
Mitchell River to land eels weighing from 10 to
301 b. Saw one brute, when hooked, drench a
boat full of anglers a few days ago. Have fre-
quently, when sculling in the evening, bumped
into an eel big enough to stop the way of the boat,
asleep on top of the water. You’d think they
were snags till the water started to fly. M'Cooey’s
111 b. eel was probably a “bull-head” or a very
young eel that got away from its guardian before
it was able to take care of itself. He wants to
come up here to see the real article.

Christian De Wet,
We ain’t got yer yet,
But Ketchener vows
You’d have been bagged,
Transported and scragged—
But for them cows.
You’re always to blame ;
Yer don’t play the game.
Who’s heard of wars
Where a toff, with a sword on,
Would dare charge a cordon
With a mob of “ stores ” t
Twenty columns there,
Blockhouses everywhere.
What right had you—
Yer plans confounded,
Yer men surrounded—
To drive them milkers through t
I’ll never, never, never,
Try again to stop a heifer
(D.W. on the shoulder).
Blockhouses is pretence,
And I think a six-rail fence
Round Africa won’t hold yer.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial pair-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

All the best-known Australian writers, for the

surpassed in this country.*
Dear Bulletin.— Witnessed in Sydney
Domain what was probably a miserable survival
of that noble game of “crack-skull” mentioned
by “H. J.M.” (Bulletin, 15/2/’O2). It was played
by a crew of German sailors, who got down on all-
fours, but as they could not trust their thin mado-
in-Germany skulls to come skull to skull, the
head of the one mildly butted the rear part of
the other. Instead of the lively whacks and
blood-stirring yells of the combatants described
by “H. J.M.” there was a dull, flat sound like a
football dropped on corned-beef, and gutter:il
German grunts. When one combatant fell and
barked his nose the game broke up. “H. J.M.’s ”
Mick—the pride of Burrowa—could knock the
lot of them out in one butt. Ah, me! how the
grand old games of our youth are going under,
but the grand old liars still keep bobbing up, so
all is not yet lost. —Spring.
* * * *
“T.J.G.” : A correspondent (B. 15/2/’O2)
says “ white ants never attack a living plant.”
On the South Coast, N.S. W., white ants attack
several kinds of plants at various stages of growth.
Have had quantities of potatoes destroyed by
them. Corn and other crops are occasionally
damaged in small quantities while growing. The
ants also destroy large quantities of growing
hardwood trees.
» » * •
A frivolous iguana. On 18tli Feb., as the
mail-coach was being driven from Albury to How-
long (N.S.W.), an iguana climbed up on to the
back of one of the horses. The team, becoming
wildly excited, bolted, and capsized the vehicle
against a tree. Pretty nearly the only thing that)
got away uninjured was the iguana.
* • • •
An Australian mother, giving evidence
before the Newcastle (N.S.W.) bench of magis-
trates (she had been summoned for neglecting to
send her son to school), told this story :
The lad was over 6ft. in height, and weighed over 1 Ost.
He was 13 years of age, had passed the fifth standard,
and was now employed at an iron-rolling mill. They had
been compelled to put him to work, as he could eat more
than all the rest of the family together, and was still
growing.
The Bench, however, held that “ the child ” could
not leave school till he was 14, and made an order
for him to attend.
• • * •
“ F.C.G.” : Canvassing, a while back, on a
N.S.W. northern river, I struck a farm about
tucker-time, and found a woman trying to patch
up a calf that had its leg injured. She invited me
to dinner, and had just started to operate on the
salt-horse when the calf began to bleat with pain.
Whereupon up she jumped, rushed out, cut the
brute’s throat with the carving-knife, came back,
and resumed operations without even wiping the
knife.

“Thurla”: “Wallaroo” (8., 27/4/’01)
writes of teamsters in wet weather travelling 12
miles in three days. This is express speed com-
pared with some journeys made in the wet nineties
on Gunnedah-Coonabarabran road. About half-
way there used to be one bad spot—known
locally as the Glue-Pot—which was about i-mile
long and thickly studded with sunken basalt
boulders in a black-soil patch. Have known
carriers to get through in three days—never in
less than one —in those wet times. Coach-
travellers, also, on that road, have vivid recollec-
tions of walking and digging-out, etc. This road,
being one-half black-soil and the other half
through sand, the carriers always had some bad
road to travel in any weather.
THE DIFFERENT MUTTON.
WAITER: "Great change in the we’ther, sir?"
CUSTOMER: “ You’re right; it was a very different sheep that my last chop was cut from."
A Northern Territory butcher is a China-
man and brings beef round twice a week in an old
four-wheeled buggy. A resident who hadn t seen
him for a week asked what was the matter. John
said his “good fiend” had died and he wanted the
buggy to cart him to the nearest cemetery. As
the good fiend didn’t go in a coffin, but bounced
along to his last resting-place loose, that resident
didn’t have any beef that week. He wanted the
buggy to get a bit of an airing.

“ A.Gr.D.” : I watched a boy “ lagging ”
to school after 10 a.m. this morning. There
was no undue haste in his manner. He
freely applied saliva to his hands, and
i then tried the effect of a strap on
them. The philosophy and futility
of it were so thoroughly Aus-
tralian !

“Tonbur”: Hungry and
tired and covered with flies, one
terribly hot day I struck a station
in the Gulf country. The manager
was sitting stark-naked under a
bough-shed, while a lubra poured
water over him with a quart-pot.
Two picanninies were knocking
the flies away with boughs, while
a number of niggers (male and
female) sat around and admired.
My appearance did not in the
least embarrass the gentleman.
“Let your horse go and come
and have a douche,” he said.
“ Here, Lottie, bring a bucket of
water and give the gentleman a
rub-down ! ” He was the only white
man on the place, and had been there
25 years without going further than
the nearest village, 125 miles away.
The table at meals swarmed with ants
and flies, and at night the mosquitos
were so awful that the “humpy,
called a “station,” had to be sur-
rounded by cow-dung fires, the smpll
from which was stifling. Choose be-
tween the Grulf and Sheol 1
“ Myall ” : Shrimps are leaving Namoi
River in vicinity of Walgett and perishing in
myriads on the water’s edge. Apparently, the
water is now too bad for even the tough and
enduring shrimp to stand it.

THE CRICKETER HAD SEEN HIM HOME.
“ What’s the good of telling lies, Joseph? The
idea of telling me you went to the cricket-
banquet when I distinctly heard you say,
‘Good-night, DARLING,’ just as you came in
at the door. O you wretch !” (Turns on
the tap.)
A Much-Troubled Printer.
A compositor on one of our leading provincial
papers—the Maitland Mercury— has had a hard
time, according to all accounts, and in giving an
outline of his life he detailed some extraordinary-
experiences.
“For over twenty-five years,” said he, “ I have
been a victim to chronic diarrhoea, and notwith-
standing careful dieting, I suffered terribly. I fell
away to a mere skeleton, lost all confidence in my
powers, and finally got so debilitated that I became
subject to fainting fits, which left me helpless. So
unusual was my case that ordinary medicines
proved of no avail, and as a last resource my
medical adviser ordered me a change. In New-
castle I met a gentleman who, afte*- suffering
from diarrhoea for ten years, had been
cured by Dr. Williams’ pmk pills for pale
people. I procured a supply, and after taking
one box my head grew clearer, my appetite
increased, and I gained in strength. Four
boxes freed me from every symptom of diar-
rhoea, and to-day I feel better than I have been
for the past thirty years.” During the present
hot weather diarrhoea is especially prevalent, and
a reliable tonic is necessary to enrich the im-
poverished blood and tone up the relaxed nerves.
Dr. Williams’ pink pills do this and more besides,
for' their strengthening properties are such that
they enable every organ of the body to carry opt
its functions regularly. They also cure anaemia,
debility, skin diseases, St. Yitus’ dance, hysteria,
heart palpitation, rickets, scrofula, consumption,
inflammation of the bowels and lungs, ladies ail-
ments, loss of vital strength, etc. Obtainable
from chemists and storekeepers, and the Dr. Wil-
liams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two
and nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three, post
free. ‘ ‘ How to cure Diarrhoea ’’—special letter of
instructions free to all who write.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The mixed condition of S.A. politics is
proved by the fact that Premier Jenkins, Opposi-
tion leader Darling, and Labor leader Price are
all candidates for the same district (Torrens) at
the coming general elections in April, and each
reckons he is sure of success.
Latest development of the subscription-
cadging racket was an effort in Maoriland to
stick up the old-age pensioners for subscriptions
to a social to Premier Seddon. Many of the poor
old men were meanly cadged out of shillings for
this object by an official in the pensions office.
About the W.A. goldfields water supply
scheme The B. is informed that, about 111
miles from Perth, a gang of 60 men is opening up
the ground around the joints of the pipes to
enable the wonderful caulking machine to go over
them again. Will that water supply ever reach
Coolgardie?
Robert Philp now says that the Federation
into which he helped so cordially to lead Queens-
land a year or two ago is a great mistake and a
public calamity. Taking his word for it, should
any sensible community entrust its destinies to a
man who makes mistakes like that ? Philp, ac-
cording to his own declaration, couldn’t see two
years ahead, and a statesman needs to have longer
vision than that.
ALWAYS GOT
IT.
SNAGGS : “ Our
town is much
more 'prosperous than yours. Our
Minister’s salary is nearly twice as
much.”
B AGGS: “Perhaps so; but ours gets his money.”
Qaeensland is still waiting to hear Premier
Philp’s explanation of the Seaforth Estate job
—that unclean proceeding by which a property,
declared by the Government’s own valuer to be
worth £1 os. lOd. per acre, was purchased by the
State, out of loan moneys, for £3 13s. per acre,
allegedly for purposes of closer settlement. And
now the State can’t sell an acre of its disgraceful
white elephant, for no farmer will look at it at
the price.
As a general thing, so tired is the Murray
nowadays that the more active sea is steadily
pushing itself further in; and settlers down
towards the mouth who, a few years ago, paid for
fresh-water frontages now find their frontages are
brine. Of course, in a brotherly spirit, they blame
the.“excessive irrigation” in the other States—■
which must sound like a joke to those who know
how miserably little the real effort at irrigation has
been so far.
Since their inception both Federal Houses
have been responsible for many more or less silly
proposals. But about the most ridiculous of the
lot is the agitation set afoot by the Melbourne
Argus to amend the Constitution so that the
Federal capital may be located in Sydney and
Melbourne alternately every three or four years !
Think, of having a portable Parliament that is
periodically moved on—like a vagrant or a
Wandering Jew !
Victorian Government contemplates search-
ing in England for a railway commissioner. Seeing
that England has just greedily grasped at the not
too brilliant Mathieson as a great prize; seeing,
too, that English railways have been on a steady
down-grade now for some years, dividends
falling and ratio of expenses to earnings ever in-
creasing, it would be more sensible if the State
made it one of the conditions of its advertisement:
“No English need apply.”
Shortage of trucks on the Westralian
railways has been driving consignors and con-
signees mad for a year past, but the Sabbath calm
of the department is unruffled. The biggest
carrying firm in Perth (H. H. Stinton) received a
bill the other day for “removing loading from
crippled trucks to sound ones, £2.” The depart-
ment’s own shocking trucks had broken down, and
it seriously demanded that the consignee should
pay the above bill in consequence.
Sydney’s first “ South Africa Day ”
typified, as usual, the relation of soldier Tommy
to his gabful bosses. The bosses ate the luncheon,
the Sees, Reids, and O’Sullivans getting the same
heavy old speech off their chests. The ex-Tommies
looked on. Reid rubbed the insult in by telling
them that he hoped next year there might be
something for them to eat. The speeches were
mostly inaudible by reason of the disrespectful
yells of the ex-contingenters for their alleged
arrears of pay.
In view of all the disputes and recrimina-
tions about the reported doings of British
soldiers in South Africa, the matter
might be reduced, in some sense, to
a mathematical formula. Like this:
(1) Soldiers, in time of war, are likely
to be times less agreeable than in
time of peace—at least, war is sup-
posed to demoralise men and make
tnem indifferent to human suffering:
(2) They are likely to be
times less tender to enemies
than to their friends and
countrymen ; (3)
They are likely to be
times more un-
pleasant in a place
where, when they get
out by themselves,
they can do pretty
much as they please,
than in one where
there is a strong
police force to keep
order; (4) The mass
of the rank and
file is likely to be
times more
callous and unfeeling
than the “ good-conduct
men” who, by reason of
their good conduct, are
above the average.
Then take the con-
duct of the Imperial
“ good-conduct ” sol-
diers — the specially-
selected specimens who visited
Sydney at Commonwealth
time, and are still remem-
bered there. Say their
conduct was . Multi-
ply that blank by the
other blanks and you are
about there.
Governor Tenny-
son began his farewell
address to the just-
departed S.A. squad-
ron of the Federal army
with “ My soldiers of the
seventh contingent.” He
has evidently forgotten that
he isn’t even nominally commander-in-chief any
more, now that the military have passed from the
States to the Commonwealth.
Brisbane Courier, imploring the people
to stick to Philp of the Deficit:
Is it possible that the country will swap horses in
crossing the stream that lies ahead of us ? Dare we trust
ourselves to the party of experiment ?
Apparently the Courier doesn’t regard Philp’s
as the party .of experiment. Yet if increasing the
public expenditure by £1,200,000 a year inside
five years isn’t a mad enough experiment what is
an experiment? Did Opposition-leader Browne
ever propose anything half so experimental ?
FOND OF DISPLAY.
CONFIRMED CELIBATE No. 1: “I hear Jack Brown is to be married on
Tuesday." C.O. No. 2: “ Ceremonious beggar !”
It has been discovered, at this rather late
hour, that the awful yarn about the wholesale
outraging of Boer women and girls by British
soldiers was not a German but an American in-
vention. The tale started its tempestuous career
in a Jackson (Mississippi) paper away back in
May, 1901, and travelled a good deal round the
U.S. before the German got his spectacles on,
and read it, and was so horrified that it required
even more beer than usual to keep him up.
The S.A. Constitution Act, which has
just received the King’s assent, comes into force
on March 31, when all members of both Houses
vacate their seats and two Ministers go out of
office. The general elections for Council and
Assembly all take place on one Saturday in April.
Henceforth there are to be 18 Councillors instead
of 24, with 42 Assembly members in lieu of 54,
and only four Ministers as against six at’ present.
Judge Rogers (N.S.W.) the other day
suspended sentence on a returned contingenter
found guilty of a very mean theft (the stealing of
a workman’s tools) so that he might go back to the
war. “I should imagine,” said his Honor, “that
he would be a very good man among the Boers.”
Probably there are other Judges who recognise
that a thief is just the kind of man that is
required as the Hen-stealing War is now con-
ducted, and that he will be a congenial item in
congenial company. But very few Judges have
the courage, as Rogers had, to say it openly.
The particularly brilliant natui’e of the
Philp finance ! His manifesto to the electors
states :
It will be a grave reproach to us if at the close of this
decade there remains a single large estate in the vicinity
of the railway fit for agricultural settlement.
■And the same Philp passed an Act of Parliament
giving the Govt, power (which it had not before)
to sell lands in large estates right up to the railway
lines. The Philp plan, in short, is to sell land
and pay the proceeds into the revenue account,
then buy it back at a much higher rate out of loan
money.
The O’Sullivan crowing jubilautly that the
present N.S.W. Ministry was the first to proclaim
St. Patrick’s Day a public holiday is wrong. Be-
fore lunatic O’Farrell shot the Duke of Edin-
burgh at Olontarf in 1868, St. Patrick’s, St.
David’s and St. Andrew’s days were all pro-
claimed holidays. After that Parkes wiped all
out—a worthy action, though with a bad motive.
St. George’s Day was never a holiday, has indeed
been only recalled to memory in these late de-
generate days. It is to the credit of the English-
man that he is generally above that sort of folly,
anyhow.
The Sydney Telegraph of Ist March,
1902, says something that The Bulletin drew
attention to a good six years earlier :
Mr. Reid created a 30 years’ loan expenditure on
works nob properly chargeable to debt, because his
revenues were insufficient.
Considering that the Telegraph incited Reid, in
the importers’ interests, to leave himself with
insufficient revenue by abolishing Dibbs’s tariff,
and lied like all the sons of Ananias in support of
the honesty of Reid’s finance, and jeered with a
loud, vacant jeer at everybody who called Reid’s
doings in question, and swore that Reid had
abundance of revenue and a genuine surplus, what
good is this belated discovery now ?
Premier Peacock makes a feeble pretence
of inquiring into the question of amalgamation of
certain Victorian municipalities. During last
session Prendergast, M.L. A., obtained a promise
from the Govt, that a Royal Commission would
be appointed. Gillott, Attorney-General and
Mayor of Melbourne, was opposed to Prendergast’s
proposal; because he knew it was aimed at forcing
Melbourne to join with some of the weaker
suburban municipalities— among them North
Melbourne. Gillott in Cabinet has been allowed
to nominate the members of the Commission, and
these members are so distinctly Tory that when
Prendergast saw the names he declined to accept
a proffered seat on the Commission. The inquiry
will be a farce, and Peacock knows it better than
anyone else.
A Jingo revi-
val is being orga-
nised in the New-
castle (N.S.W.)-
district with some
degree of success.
Its main promo-

of them of the

are either jealous
of Arthur Gri-

Besides, there is
among the Nor-

strong pro-Gore

sarily anxious for
Boer gore, but
anybody’s gore—
men of the dog-

believe in “blood-

and regard any

There is something unbelievable about
that cablegram as published by many Aus-
tralian dailies last week, which alleged that
the British authorities had transported General
Viljoen—“fighting Ben Viljoen ” —to St. Helena
for life. He had no trial, and nothing has been
alleged against him except that he is an Afrikan-
der and one of the grandest patriots and fighting
men of the 19th century. If Britain could
possibly treat such a man as a mere criminal, it
would have got down to a depth of degradation
so low that even Joseph Surface of Birmingham
could hardly drag it lower. The Russians,
in the dark ages of over 100 years ago, treated'
Kosciusko more generously than that.
The meat contract, and the cold invitation
to drop in at the Coronation if he happened to be
passing, have done something to reduce Premier
Peacock’s Jingo swelled head.' After his stupid-
declaration that a White Australia was a little
concern alongside of Britain’s desire to foregather
with the brown men of Asia, and his other
breakings-out, Peacock expected better treatment.
Still, it seems that old M'Culloch, Minister for
Works, who didn’t receive even the most frozen,
kind of invitation, will go to London. M‘Culloch
hankers badly after a decoration, that he may
hang it on his bedpost to soothe his declining
years, and he unfortunately failed to catch York’s
eye during the recent visit. York had a difficult
eye to catch.
The National Liberal Union of Q., a weak-
ling developed from Philp’s foundling, the
National Ass, is having a cold time up North.
In Brisbane South the blatant Jackannear was de-
feated in the election of an Ass candidate, and'
straightway called a meeting of malcontents
and declared his intention of being a National Ass
candidate whether the Ass wants him or not. At
Fortitude Valley “ Jock ” Mac Master, a rebellious-
grocer, is doing the same, and a want of discipline
is visible all round. The Labor party meanwhile
devotes all its energies to prevent “dead” being
placed against its supporters’ names on the various
electoral rolls. But it isn’t doing much good.
Even one of its leading candidates has been struck
off the roll by the shameless Boodlephilp gang on
the ground that he is dead.
If the M.L. Opposition doesn’t raise a
row over the scandalous appointments to the Bth
and 9th contingents, it had better go out of the
business, for it can’t oppose hard enough to hinder
a grasshopper. Such flagrant jobbery hasn’t been
known in Maoriland for the last 30 years. One
special case, out of many, is that of the son of a
big Hebrew brewer. This youth had never been
even in a volunteer corps, and rides like a
Jew (the Hebrew was a good pedestrian soldier
once, but he knew so much about his deficiencies
on horseback that he put a special clause in his
religion prohibiting the horse, so tbat he wouldn’t
have to get on it). He is made a lieutenant. And
the other commissions mostly go to rich men’s
sons with no experience and a tendency to hang
on by the mane.
The voice of Liberty is not quite smothered"
in Maoriland. One well-known Auckland weekly
dares to print this about the departure of the
eighth contingent:
The general opinion expressed was that the time had-
arrived when the colony should cease the cruel and,
indeed,.criminal deportation of our young men. Maori-
land had no hand in bringing about the war, and as a re-
markable coincidence, when we were engaged in war with
the Maoris the Imperial troops were withdrawn, and the
inhabitants were left to fight out their own destiny. The-
Premier should abstain from further Jingoism and the
gag about the “ unity of the Empire” and the “claims
of Imperialism,” and such highfalutin’ bunkum and play-
ing up to the gallery-. We are weary of it. With
237,800 men in arms against a comparatively- infinitesimal
number of the Boers, surely- it is fair to presume that
England has at present sufficient material, properly
handled, to do the trick without further calls on-
Maoriland.
N.S.W. Public Service Board has given
another little surprise to the Service. In 1898
the Board instituted regulations providing for a
yearly £lO increase to members of the clerical
division qualifying by a set examination and a
certificate of competency from the departmental
head. The idea was manifestly impracticable
from the start, and it soon broke down—in fact,
it was apparently only invented to help George
Reid at a difficult election. On December
31, 1900, these regulations were repealed.
Later, the Board calmly announced that its
gazette of 31st December, 1900, was to date six
months back, to 30th June, 1900, so that increases
earned in the interval would be cancelled. This
was clearly an act of repudiation, and, on the
grievance being discussed, Parliament agreed
to vote funds to pay the sum of £5 docked
from them to each of,th,e public servants affected.
Tlie Board asked for £l5OO to cover all demands,
and that sum was at once voted. Now the Board
has tardily published a list of those entitled to the
£5, and announces that it has allotted to each fifty
shillings. Of course, there will be a row, and after
a long series of appeals and explanations the
other 50s. may be wormed out of the Board in
1903. But it is easier to collect money from a
Scotch Jew named Aaron McDonald than to get
the P.S. Boai-d to be squaye in its dealings.

Twelve Yea.es a Sufferer.
Doctors Differed.
VITADATIO
CURED.
Read Mr. Edward Ellis’ Testimony.
Newcastle. April 24, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—For the past twelve years I have been a
sufferer. I-have' been treated by doctors in Wallsend,
Hamilton, Sydney, Rockhampton, and various places, but
without success ; they all failed to diagnose my case.
Some said I.Suffered from kidneys out of order, ulcers on
stomach—enlarged liver—pleurisy, &c. ; but VITADATIO
soon showed the disease, and I am now heavier than ever
I was in my life. The hydatids have come away in great
quantities, as witness to this I have given two bottles of
same to your manager in Newcastle. I am thankful for
the good health VITADATIO has given me, and will be
willing at all times tcj tell others of its marvellous virtues.
(Signed) EDWARD ELLIS,
Vitau-street, Lambton, N.S.W.
Formerly of Rockhampton, Queensland.
Witness—W. J. FIST,
Hunter-street, Newcastle.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

COLLECTIVE INVESTIGATION.—The ex-
Eerience of millions of people in all quarters of the
abitable globe during the last quarter-of-a-century
has confirmed the value of Hunyadi Janos as the
best and safest natural aperient. Invaluable to
diners-out.*
Some people have no reverence for hoards,
or even for aldermen—in fact, there have beeil
cases of disrespectful allusion to a Town Clerk.
A Sussex-street (Sydney) firm has written to the
Government stating that its locality,is dirtier now
that the State has resumed it than it was before.
And the Sydney Municipal Council has received
cheek and back-talk from a person in an office
somewhere, who says that the municipal rat
poison isn’t fatal after all, and that the gladdened
rats are saving it up to tide them over the winter.
The man who claimed that he used to
wake himself at exactly the same time every
morning bv mechanically pulling in his sleep a
string connected with the spring blind in his room
—take breath—was a liar. And it is a lie of our
own that there ever was such a man; but our
innocent idea was to lead up to an advertisement
of our nickel-plated alarm clocks with 4in. dials,
post free, ss. M‘Lean Bros. & Rigg, Ltd., 107
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne.*
Put it to the credit of the little party of
Laborites in the Westralian Assembly that it
alone voted solidly against the proposal to pay the
Governor £2OOO beer money; and put it to the
credit of Opposition leader Nansen, that, though
most of his party forsook him, he moved the omis-
sion of the item. Even without the beer allow-
ance this handful of people pays the Gov. £4OOO a
year, besides providing him with free housing,
free staff, free telegrams, free lighting, free water,
free stationery, and other free things too numerous
to mention in one issue.
The fifth and last test-match has been
fought and won, and, for the present, the question
of supremacy in The Imperial Sport (caps., please,
and nevermind “The muddied oaf”) is decided.
But no such series of tests is nowadays needed to
prove the supremacy of D.C.L. Whisky. In pro-
viding mankind with wholesome inspiration in
business, in love, in politics, and poetry, D.C.L.
is the great Imperial beverage. As the keen old
experts of Maoriland say—“ The Whisky of the
Wise,” of the wise in general, and the wisest in
particular.*
Treasurer Turner, unveiling a monument
to a Victorian ex-contingenter the other day :
They did not stop to inquire whether the war was
justifiable or not. It was enough that it had the support
of the press, people and Parliament of Great Britain.
Turner should read Kipling’s ballad of Tomlinson,
the stodgy, conventional, bell-toppered ghost who
never thought for himself, but followed, sheep-
like, in the wake of the majority. Turner is
supposed to be some kind of a religious man.
When his spectacled wraith appears on the
Judgment Day, if there is one, how does he think
the above excuse will serve to explain the blood
that is on his baldish head? There is unlimited
excuse for the man who does wrong thinking it is
right, but the man who acknowledges that he
started killing other people by deputy, without
inquiring whether it was right or not, but merely
because the Jingoes of Britain said it was right,
is a witless and gory oaf, and the ghost of Tom-
linson is his brother.
