A Victorian in the fifth contingent relates
that, on New Year’s night, a body of them sallied
forth, and, early next morning, the Boers captured
about 30. After going through them for every-
thing, and swopping clothes, the Dutchmen let
t iem go. One Boer surprised the narrator of this
yarn by remarking, in a tone that seemed familiar,
“ Now, old man, the best thing for you is to do a
get back' to camp ; it’s a good distance.” The
Victorian regarded him more closely, and was
suddenly “flabbergasted.” “Why, blime, it
can’t be Bill -—But the Boer just then re-
membered that he had other business, and lost no
time in congealing himself among his comrades.
This was what the Family ’Erald would call “a
strange recontre.”
A squalid, two-roomed tenement, lately
discovered in a cul-de-sac of Wellington (M.L.),
sheltered two married couples, who occupied a
room apiece, and a man who slept in one of the
apartments on a sofa. The occupant of the sofa
paid the rent.
At the inquiry as to the management of Pt.
Halswell (M.L.) gaol, one of the prisoners stated
that the blankets were so infested with fleas that
it was necessary to kill them with sticks. Even
the inseoticide money has apparently been spent
on war,
‘ ‘ Pleasure steamers and yachts are now
being fitted,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “ with turbine screws for motive
power, with highly-satisfactory results as regards
speed and the comparative coal consumption.
On the Clyde estuary a passenger steamer has
steamed 12,116 miles at the rate of knots for
8'47 tons of coal per mile. She has five turbine
screw propellers, and is 250 ft. long by 30 wide.
As most people know, the turbine screw is practi-
cally a shaft with a screw at the water end, the
whole being encased in a sealed cylinder. The
shaft is decked like a porcupine, with blades so
arranged that on the raw steam, generated by the
furnace, being driven into the cylinder, it hits on
the blades, forcing the shaft to revolve. The
driven flow of steam is continuous, and is forced
the whole length of the cylinder, still imparting
motion to the screw, until it escapes under water.
Of three new steam turbine yachts now being con-
structed, one is to do 24 knots, while another is to
be of 700 tons measurement and 1500 horse power.”
| Returned cohentingenter, describing Boer
| tactics: “Yes, the blankers ! They rise out of
! the blanky ground, and blanky well empty their
blanky magazines into us, and then the blankers
blanky well do a blanky get! ” (“Blanky” ex-
tremely varied.)
Of late years, the notice, “No English
need apply,” has been a common sight in the east
end of London, the cheap foreigners being pre-
ferred as wage-slaves. British workers are begin-
ning, therefore, to have some understanding of,
and sympathy with, Australian objections to alien
immigration.
Since last New Year’s Day marriage in
New York State became possible at the cost of a
shilling. Under a new Act, the parties have
merely to sign a certificate that they are man and
wife, in the presence of any notary, who is limited
to a charge of 25 cents. Even this fee attracts
some of the lower-class lawyers, who are already
advertising that marriage is “ now within the
reach of all.”
Just when such great efforts are being
made to point out that it is quite impossible that
British troops could give way to cruelty or blood-
thirsty fury, or loot and burn houses because they
liked doing it, or slay when there was no need of
slaying, we come across inopportune little things
like these. Says the Indian Planters’ Gazette :
Should we slay our brother Boer? 'He should be slain
with the same ruthlessness that they slay a plague-in-
fected rat. Exeter Hall may shriek, but there will be
plenty of it and the more the better. -Tire Boer resistance
will enable us to find an excuse to blot out the Boers as a
nation and turn their land into a vast shambles.
The correspondent of a London daily writes in
.favor of looting:
Next to the fierce joy of fighting, that of satisfying the
primeval instinct of robber-man is the highest pleasure
which war affords. Add the promise of plunder to the
certainty of a fight and you increase by tenfold the
efficiency of any army in the world. If war is right, then,
in ancase let the boys loot. If for policy or principle it
be wise to let a man murder, then let him for his private
gratification be a thief.
The correspondent of the Morning Post writes:
I felt a joy of satisfaction when the smoke (of a rebel’s
farm) went up. . . . These unkempt, ill-conditioned
rebels, these human vermin, have been treated as though
on a level with respectable Kaffirs. ... A beast of a
rebel was getting his deserts. ■ . >
Captain Barker (Montreal Herald) writes :
We had a pleasant little fight on New Year’s Day . . .
we killed fourteen Boers . . . others fell with pleasing
little holes in them. . . . The Boers who remained brought
out white flags and threw down their arms: I would have
let the beggars have the bayonet at that, only Colonel
Richards stopped us.
When British papers advocate the extermination
of the enemy, also thieving, murder, the turning of
the land into a shambles, and such like, why get
so excited because certain Germans talk about us
in a somewhat similar strain ?
The plaint of a young member of the
London Sports Club re Kipling’s latest poem :
The worst of it is that you’re not safe to do anything
without old Kipling finds some fancy name for 3’ou. I
don’t quite know what I am. I play cricket for my
county, so I suppose I’m a “ flannelled fool ” ; I play half-
back for the school', so that makes me a “ muddied oaf ” ;
and when I went out with the first lot of Yeomanry he
called me an “ absent-minded beggar.” And look at my
young brother. He’s everything that I am, and in addi-
tion to that he’s just got his commission in the Marines.
So he’s a “ sort of giddy Harumfradite,” besides all the
rest. Old Kipling is getting to be too much of a slogger.
Somebody ought to tell him.

Ex-King Prempeh of Ashanti, who is now
interned at Seychelles, is so enamoured of the
Sictures of Joe Chamberlain that he has sent to
Ingland for a monocle, a frock-coat, and other
essentials to that imitation which is the sincerest
form of flattery. Orchids grow locally. “Of
course you can never be like us, but be as like us
as you’re able to be.”
Marwood, the predecessor of the late
English hangman, Billington, possessed a certain
savage humor. When responding to a number of
pressmen who formally congratulated him on his
appointment, possibly with leg-pulling intent, he
expressed the hope that he would yet have the
pleasure of hanging a reporter.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
y® ar ’ as the Newmarket Handicap
fell to a nag- which looked the best of good thin«-s on
form and once again punters were reminded tha°t the
six-furlongs straight at Flemington is a more reliable
test course than any mile with a turn in it. The fall of
Drawbridge went to prove the rule. A similar mishap
occurring in a field of horses bunched against the rails
would probably have brought more than three of them to
grief. All the same, the disaster that horrified the crowd
on Saturday was exceptionally unlucky in its results.
The Newmarket scamper more often than not is such a
Widespread event that two horses, running ten yards
apart, might fall without seriously troubling any of the
others.
Sir Foote was some distance to the left and in
front of the accident when it happened, and doubtless he
had the race already won ere Drawbridge became non est.
The time, lmin. 13|sec.—J-sec. faster than Amiable’s
record—hardjy admits of the suggestion that the winner
didn t hold them all safe on his merits. Aurous certainly
was going faster at the finish, but Sir Foote appeared to
be easing up in the last 20 yards. Besides, Aurous and
the others had been outpaced by the favorite before they
got to the distance post, and it is not by a rush at the
finish that Newmarkets are won. Sir Foote set them
a 7dj seconds task, and they couldn’t catch him. Never-
theless, the final effort of Aurous, the “ unfancied,” was
an impressive surprise for Isaac Moses, Esq. Also it
was even more satisfactory than surprising. Because
Aurous had receded in the betting from 6’s to 16’s any-
thmg short of a brilliant performance by the offspring of
Wallace and Aura would have been received with snorts
and sniggers. The stable didn’t “ back ” the mare or
advise friends to “ back ” her in the full sense of the term.
It was known that the stable connections had backed Sir
t oote for a little. And on the strength of what they
really to the “books” formed a quite confident
assumption that Aurous was not a goer Under the
circumstances the punting pubhc might at least have
spared an all-round cheer for Aurous after she had beaten
everything bar the “ dead-bird.” Leslie Macdonald and
co. gave the public more than a straight run for their
Aurous money. They gave a straight tip about Sir Foote
by making no secret of the fact that they were not dis-
posed to take a short price about their chance of beating
the imported flier. The candor came from the Mac-
do™ IkeyMo ? he unsavor y insinuations proceeded
Sir Foote’s easy victory induced a good many
cautious people to lay the odds on Wakeful for the Ksseii-
don Stakes. The mare’s failure to give 3 stone (to the
, . orse on *'i le previous Saturday was no longer a
reflection on her character. She had been attempting
the impossible. Wakeful’s price would have been sto I
on, instead of a modest 2 to 1 on, but for the inclusion of
La Carabine among the starters. The good old mare
from Ballarat had followers who took it for granted that
S Rupert was too proud of her to let her go forth to be
badly beaten and distressed, but the result proved that
La Carabine was not fit to struggle against Wakeful.
Macdonald s generous little friend romped along in her
happiest style, and did the miles in 2min. 37sec. with-
out being pressed. Barbarossa seemed to fairly beat
Limber for second prize..

, *, ke Earnshaw selected Sir Foote on the pedigree
of the animal. In the first place, he sent home an order
{°l Sl I * oote s eider brother, but the deal didn’t come
what he wanted in the not very sound
colt that shook ’em up last Saturday. Such compara-
«ve*y cheap and conspicuously successful samples of
English blood stock as Positano, Voyou, April Fool and
Sir I oote should create an Australian demand for the
imported article for racing as well as stud purposes. Sir
Foote has the smattering of white hairs at the tail-butt
which one always likes to see in a bay or chestnut. The
fancy is absurd, perhaps, but it seems full of wisdom
when the tail is attached, to a big winner.

• Twenty-three stripped for Newmarket Handi-
cap, Saturday Churchill’s year (’9O) saw 36, ditto
Laundress and Maluma’s. Bungebah showed 34 the
way; 32 opposed Wild Rose, and 30 carried silk when
William Tell and Fortunatus each won. Latest winner
was an unknown quantity till he got away
with Futurity Stakes, t’other day. Before leaving En°--
land his best form was fourth in Westminster Plate and
eighth in Newmarket Plantation Stakes. Sir F. bears a
strong resemblance to Carbine in appearance. Through
some alleged unsoundness (in the feet, ’tis said) he has
always been described as a hard ’un to train : this time
however, wily Ike Earnshaw seems to have brought him
to perfection. s
*.* • *
Evidence ig that accident in Newmarket H’can
was brought about through Drawbridge (just clear of a
big bunch galloping strongly in the centre of the course)
crossing his legs and falling. Moonbeam toppled over
heavily, hurling rider R. Manning hard to the ground
Progedior and Ampier fell in quick succession. Three
nags were on their feet instanter, ditto their jocks -but
Manning and his mount, Moonbeam, lay motionless side
by side. Moonbeam had to be shot and Manning died
six hours subsequently. “ Little Bobby.” had just turned
i 3 and was a capable rider, with the head and hands of an
old un, albeit he was the lightest at the game Melbourne
owned. Bobby won several races for brother-in-law E
Davis—most important being Oakleigh Plate (on Moon-'
beam) at Caulfield, Feb. 8. Drawbridge’s rider (Wheeler)
had his shoulder injured, Ampier’s (U. Cooper) got a bad
shaking, while Progedior’s (Smith) came away scatheless.
* * * *
Newmarket Handicap facts. Of 29 races run to
date 11 have gone to horses, 12 to t’other sex and six to
geldings. Aged nags have never “ been there ”at the
right end °f the “long” six furlongs, and only once has
a 6-year-old (Sedition) triumphed. Four and 5-vear-olds
have won 12 and 5 times respectively. Top weight was
Bungebahs 9.3; Fortunatus carried only lib less
Previous to Sir Foote’s 1.131 last Saturday, Amiable’s 1.14
was the record for the race, but she had 8.12, while
f VaS Bungebah (2 to 1) ranked
kott |, st favorite that ever got away with the money
tall Sir Foote s 6 to 4 against at barrier rise. Biggest
(successful) outsiders were Churchill, 33 to 1, and For-
tunatus and Laundress, 50 to 1 each.
* * * *
Double and straight out, Moses were heavily hit
over Sir Foote’s success in Newmarket, as heaps of money
had been secured about that win and he was coupled
with everything having a chance in the Australian Cup.
Sir* oote has other engagements ahead and will probably
lighten Mo.s satchels still more if they’ll lay him but
they re commencing to squeak loudly already ’
• • * ,
Early fancy Tip Top for Newmarket ran more
prominently (fourth) than 19 other starters, and yet she
imed up so little fancied that 500 to 2 was laid against her
two days before the race. This gallop indisputably demon-
strated that if her preparation had not been interfered
with she might have given the ultimate winner trouble
• • * *
Scratching (Monday) of M‘Donald’s Aurous for
Australian Cup (Tuesday) was a big set back to punters
as for some time they piled the money on—more particu-
larly after her second in Newmarket Handicap, previous
Saturday, when ‘twos” (to big tunes in some places)
were eagerly closed with. The happening was fore-
shadowed early m the day, for while Aurous’ stable mate,
Revenue, hardened to 6 to 4 in Sydney, she bobbed about
a lot in the market till, just before her elimination was
wired, sevens were literally forced down one’s throat ;
but the thing’ had become nauseous by that time. Only’
inference dedueible (without full information) was that
Aurous had broken down (and this assumption proved
correct), for the race looked hers beforehand—Mo had
christened it the “ Aurous .Stakes.”
* * * *
The Leger (3.10 f) was run to suit Grasspan
who bolted past the plodding Haymaker at the finish
With a pacemaker in the field Haymaker might have
outstayed Grasspan, although the Sydney colt certainly
won in good sl.vle. But, anyway, Haymaker is only a
second-rater. He looks smaller than when he finished
fourth for the Melbourne Cup.
* * • «
Apropos Grasspan’s V.R.C. St. Leger, Satur-
day, Sydney nags haven’t often caught this plum. Late
Hon. Janies White had a succession of wins with Match-
lock, Trident, Abercorn, Volley and Dreadnought: then
Gibraltar won next year (’9l), and, in the' Ion" gap
since, onlv Cabin Boy (’96) .and Parthian (’00) cropped
up. Grasspan was bred add is prepared by Randwick
trainer, Tom Payten, but belongs to Victorian owner
Raby.
* * * *
Looks as if Sydney crack jock S. Callinan will
get a fall ere long. Warwick Farm stewards, stimulated
to action (Saturday week) by the apparent daring of the
thing, carpeted Callinan for “suspicious” ridino- of
Merralie on which the public punted heavily, only to see
it run last; but nothing was done. Last Saturday same
rider was up on long-waited-for and alleged “ good thin"”
Always (a stable chum of Merralie’s) in Ladysmith
Stakes at Roselnll. Backers crowded to “get on ” and
quicker than one could say knife, Mo. wouldn’t lay’ better
than evens, and, ere barrier hoist, he wanted odds.
Adways behaved wretchedly ; further the race progressed

team for England are still “ probables,” though Hopkins

Howell s, through his higher bowling average this season

Travers, and Clarke. Latter has proved a disappoint-
ment lately, and Travers has yet to earn international
spurs.

After this tour, MacJaren’s lot will be bracketed
with Stoddart’s last combination as the worst-defeated
team that has come Australiawards. Stoddart’s men won
four, lost five, and drew two. So far, Maclaren’s side has
won four and lost six.

Ex- “ Demon ” Spofforth thinks wicket-keepers
are.the present great want on Australian and English
fields. But Spoff. himself had something to do with the
shortage. He set the fashion in fast bowling, and players
soon sought healthier stations than behind the stumps.

teresting, was far from exciting. Central Cumberland, at
the wickets all the afternoon, ran up 329 for five down, in
opposition to Paddington, to whom absence of Noble
Trumper and. Kelly, with the Australians in Melbourne’
Was a heavy handicap. Pye notched 121—his second cen-
tury on successive Saturdays. Farquhar just missed the
hundred by one run. Looks now as if Paddington will
go under this time. At present, last-mentioned club and
Leichhardt-Balmain (bracketed together) top the list, and
latter also seems in for defeat. Meeting North Sydney
Leichhardt-Balmain only accumulated 216, while t’other
side have 51- made by Iredale and Clarke—without losino-
a wicket. Present position of Redfern-Sydney maton
seems a “ cert.” for Sydney. If this summing-up turns
out correct, Sydney will go from third up on top, with
the two already there. Leichhardt-Balmain and Padding-
ton, and Sydney and Waverley come together in the final
round, therefore Leichhardt-Balmain’s prospects are still
good. Only the three clubs named are in it now. Most
who have the good of cricket at heart hope for Leich-
hardt-Balmain’s success, but, in the light of Saturday’s
and previous week’s occurrences, Sydney’s chances are
entering strongly into the thoughts of tipsters. Burwood
scored 361 against Waverley, towards which Barbour
brought 95.

Second day of big swimming in M.L. brought
the Quarter Mile Championship to Dick Cavill, with
Ceorge Read, 50 yards behind, second, and Gailey third.
Read led to 325 yards, when Cavill passed him. Read
must have either stopped dead for a time, or only drifted
in to be squelched by 50 yards in the remaining 115 of the
journey. Cavill toppled another world’s record over.
Rrior best was Read’s 5.42, set up when he gained the
Quarter premiership of N.S. VV. at Balmain in Decem-
her last, and then beat Cavill by 7.3-ssec. This time
cavill got through his contract in 5.37 1-5. Another in-
stance of a world’s best being lowered twice within acom-
paratively brief period. Previous to Read’s brilliant
ettort, of December last ex-amateur English champ. J.
*•'“> established eight years ago, was the
speediest, but the English A.S.A. withheld endorsement
through some informality ; then his 5.431-5 stood.

(M.c. probably) who traversed 43ft., Smith (also -M.L.)
was second with 40ft. 6in. Previous holder (Dunn
lctoria) went over to compete, but the wires are silent
about him W. McDonald’s 69ft., at Sydney, Nov. 30 last
year, is the continent’s record, with duration of effort
united to lmin. Though Cavill and Lane competed they
profess nothing above the ordinary at this game.

excess of the figures credited to Lane at Bondi (N.slw.),
Jan. 11 this year. Cavill never before put up as fast a hun-
( red as his latest. Cabled, also, that only Read and
Cavill tackled the mile, and they were locked together
some tune, Cavill gaining at the turns, and Read making

2-ssec. This if right, is 3mins. 35 2-ssec. better than the

“Trudge”: Sensation of Australasian Swim-
ming: Championships, just concluded in M.L., was remark-
ably fast times clocked for Cavill in the Half, Quarter,
and Mile. Every race showed astonishing improvement
on his N.S.W. form, and that he has come on wonderfully
is evidenced by Read having to play very much second
fiddle, though he too, excepting in the 440, shaped well
throughout and reached his highest standard, hast
season it was foretold herein that as Cavill matured he
would turn the tables on his older rival and this has come
to pass. Read’s heart is not over big ;he funked palpably
in this season’s N.S.W. Half-Mile and on the occasion of
his first appearance in a champion race the year before.
That defeat by 50 yards in the finishing 115 of the
“ Quarter ”at \\ anganui (second day) reads uncommonly
like “ chucking ”it when caught. Apropos this par-
ticular event, Cavill showed an improvement of 12 2-ssec.
over the distance as compared with his time when Read
beat him easily in the 440yds. championship of N.S.W.,
December 21st last. Fact of Wanganui baths being only
25vds. long will, in part, account for these marvellous
speed achievements, but that Cavill has much improved is
demonstrated by the easy manner in which be settled
Read each time and the way htTSWam up to Lane in the
hundred, which was much ahead of any sprint
swimming he has previously done. Read is booked for a
trip ’Ome with Lane this month and swimmingpeop.e are
subscribing his exs., but in the light of-recent events
they’re certainly sending the wrong man away, and some
day they’ll be sorry for it too.

Concerning some breakages : Crack bowler J.
Saunders broke the point of his' left shoulder in the Yic.-
England match last week. So he couldn’t play in the
fifth test—a deplorable fact in the face of his perform-
ances. He is unquestionably the best Australian left-hand
bowler since Ferris. Jake Gaudaur,.. once world’s chain-
pion sculler, fell on the ice at Rat Portage and broke his
shoulder-bone; and famous fist-fighter Fitzsimmons,
coniing home from the Orpheum Theatre in Brooklyn,
stepped on a little bit of orange-peel, and jarred lus spine
so badly that it is unlikely he will ever put ’em up again.
The Maoriland freak was at the time preparing for an
almighty scrap with giant Jeffries, which is now, of
course, off.

Sydney Gaiety A. C. was packed from floor to
roof Monday night to watch “Cocker” Tweedie . and
Melbourne Jack M‘Gowan fight for the continent’s light-
weight supremacy and 75 so vs. There hasn t been such a
house at a fight since the.Cribb-Dunn and Cribb-Sturgeon
affairs, and there won’t be again for many a night if one
might judge from the way the audience boo-hooed their
chagrin And disgust. Both pugs looked well enough
before the scrap started, but though notning terrible was
done, Tweedie sat in his corner after the first round
looking as fagged out as Samson after that gentleman
had slain his little thousand all on his own. M’Gowan
hadn’t turned a hair, and looked quite ready for the
remSihder of the stipulated 20 rounds. And he thumped
Tweedie, on the ribs unmercifully, and pushed him all
round the ring showering left and right hooks which,,
rarely got past the clever defence. It was near the end
of the fourth round that Tweedie, rushing in, met a
soother that sent him among the stars from which he
scrambled up too late. ’Twas a fizzle, but ’twas fair :
Mac. is as good as ever, and Tweedie has gone utterly
bung and won’t be worth looking- at in a ring any more.
» * • *•
Indications are that the next' bruising struggle
for Australian heavyweight supremacy will be between
Armidale maintenance man Billy McColl and present
champion Bill Doherty. Latter wants the. match and
former says he is wiliing and will be ready in April. If
Doherty is as good as when he fought Felix in Sydnej two
years ago McColl will get licked. But then arises the
question : Is Doherty as good ?
* * * *
Balapce-sheet of last Austral bike meet, was to
have been submitted Wednesday this week. Gathering
proved remarkably successful. - Receipts reached £3392,
and, after all claims were met, £1621 remained to the
good. Gate-money was larger, and prize-expenditure
£IOO less, than on any prior occasion.
Absolutely the best side-show in London during
Coronation week should be that arranged'by the Nationa j
Sporting Club. Boxing at all weights, and the heavy*
weight championship to carry a £IOOO “ Coronation Belt.
Every event open to the world. International tug-of-war
(Australia is already announced as sending a team).
Oxford and Cambridge to box the American University
champions, and the U.S. army to wrestje with England s
best soldiers. The open wrestling to include the latest
developments from Turkey and Japan, and French
“ savate ” kickers to be matched with the best English
boxers of their own weight.
* * •
S.A. League of Wheelmen’s meeting finished
Saturday. Hot winds and dust-storms kept people away,
and the venture showed £B7 on the wrong side. Half-
mile Championship of Australia fell to Don Walker, in
lmin. 11 l-ssec.—4 2-ssec. slower than his preliminary
heat. Walne and Cameron followed as named. Cameron s
riding 1 stood out; Walne was only inches ahead of him for
second place in the big thing of the gathering. Cameron
secured the final of the Adelaide Wheel Race (two miles),
with attendant £IOO, brilliantly, putting up a record •
4min. 0 3-ssec. Rolfe was second, and A. E. Ohampness
third ; then came Cole, Burton, L. Corbett, Walsh, and
Shearer. Final of Mile First-class Handicap brought a
new “ best.” E. F. Wilksch just pipped Morgan by
inches in 1.58, with Maorilander Burton third. Five
Miles Scratch race also produced good riding. Wilksch
and Barker tussled desperately, and worked excitement
to breaking tension till la! ter barely won on the line in
llniin. 20 2-ssec. Continuation of M‘Donald - Aunger
match was deferred through illness of Aunger, who,
medical men asserted, had been hocussed. After first
event previous Saturday he was delirious for two days,
and couldn’t leave his bed till Wednesday. McDonald
came on the track to go over the course, but was blocked.
He, however, claimed the stakes, and the racing board
on Monday paid them over, at the same time informing
M'Donald that he wouldn’t be asked to race in S.A. again
through his unsportsmanlike action.
* * * *
The Adelaide people have a great affection for
“ Bobbie” Walne. He had disappointed the Adelaideans
much of late, but they still hoped on, and when, the other
Saturday, in the Commonwealth Mile, he sprinted as of
yore, leaving MacDonald and Aungur anywhere, the crowd
were prepared to tear the place down from sheer joy.
Mrs. E. T. Smith laid the blue and gold ribbon across his
steaming shoulders, and E. T. Smith wobbled Bobbies
arm about for a while and said something unintelligible,
and meanwhile the cloud of steam that rose up from
Bobbie almost blotted out the view of Smith.

N.B.W. League bikist, C. Melrose, had, on
Saturday, a second shot at Goulburn to Sydney road
record of lOhr. 28min., created by T. Alsoff, and beat it,
but with only s|min. to spare. ’Tis safe to foretell that
this won’t exist long, as there’s nothing extra good about
it, and those proved good amateurs, Green, Toose, and
Lyon, and Simon, the country league man, purpose
having a cut.
Mr. B. R. Wise writes : “ With reference
to a statement in last issue, that ‘two leading
Federal Ministers were pledged to appoint liim
High Commissioner,”’ that no Minister has ever
given him such a pledge, nor has he asked for it
from any. Bulletin statement was that pro-
minent Federal Ministers are definitely pledged
toB. R. Wise’s appointment.” It was not said,
or meant to be suggested, that any pledge had
been given to, or sought by, Mr. Wise himself, or
by anyone acting for him. The suggestions for
his appointment have proceeded wholly from
public motives.
From an Indian paper :
Yesterday Nobogopal Bose of Birpore was sentenced to
pay a fine of Rs. 400 by the Deputy Magistrate of Alipore
on a charge of defamation. The accused falsely and
maliciously circulated statements concerning a Hindu
priest (the complainant) to the effect that he used to nux
with Christians and eat food cooked by them.
The European is supposed to be spreading the
blessings of Christianity in India, but when a ma n
can still obtain legal satisfaction for. being charged
with associating with Christians the spread is
apparently very slow.
Editor Mac-Dunstan’s mullet, and mutton
hash, organ, the WATCHMAN, after referring to
The Bulletin’s influence as “ pestilential, then
remarks that The Bulletin doesn’t affect public
opinion at all, and so has no influence, pestilential
or otherwise. Eater the consistent Watchman,
in a bitter article on Cardinal Moran, asks :
Who does not remember the cartoon of The Bulletin—
the little boy frofn Manly looking down into the well
of truth,” on theiedge of which the Cardinal s cap rested,
the little boy soliloquising, “ The Cardinal seems to have
fallen in here ” ; ’and the Cardinal’s voice irom the depths,
“ Well, if Oi have, Oi won't admit it.”
So apparently every one remembers the sayings
of a paper which exercises a pestilential
influence, and also no influence at all. When the
Watchman shows such signs of obfuscation on his
beat how can anyone sleep in peace, confident
that the burglarious Jesuit isn’t coming softly
over the back fence ?

COMMON WCALTH
Since that lamentable episode when
Methuen marched in a circle and overtook and
captured the convoy at the tail of his own oolumn,
anything seems liable to happen. How would
this cablegram look, for instance:
Lord Methuen mistaking himself for somebody else,
surrounded himself, and took himself prisoner.
A few years ago, when the rare burglar
appeared in Adelaide, the papers always referred
to him as a visitor from the eastern States. lo-
day Adelaide has as many burglars in proportion
to population as any other Australian
the park dosser, particularly the lady dosser, is
becoming an institution. Cortes, by wa,y of co -
vinoing the King of Spain of the advanced civilisa-
tion of the Aztecs of Mexico, wrote “ A®, 1 ”
all other civilised cities, there are beggars in
streets.” In the City of Churches the number of
respeot ably-dressed men who say they have s
better days is surprising. They are a eompa
tively recent development, too, like the burglars.
What a number of better days there must have
been in Australia, and what a lot of peop e

The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co., Limited, report to Feb. 28: —Wheat: Though no
alteration in values has occurred since our last report
the market has assumed a quieter aspect. Arrivals have
been considerable, and though many sales have been
effected ex trucks at Darling Harbor, a fair portion has
been taken into store, owners’ views being above current
rates. Quotations may be thus given—Prime, 3s. 3d. ;
f.a.q., 3s. 2d. to 3s. 2jd. : chickwheat, 2s. lOd. to 2s. lid.

“When the Maddens left vice-regal bliss, cast-
ing * one longing, lingering look behind,’ they
probably reflected (with truth) that the authorities
had failed to add to gubernatorial glitter by sub-
stituting new lamps for old. Trim, little, grey
General CJjjrke isn’t half so florid a figure-head as
blarneying Judge Madden. The new Governoress,
with her grey hair and dark eyes, is certainly
something of a high-stepper, but, on the other
hand, her daughter can’t hold a candle to the
Madden Ruby.
“ The deputy sub-King is conscious of the same
matrimonial responsibilities as the King himself,
though in a less degree. Hence, marrL.ge with a
vice-subject, though common, has its disadvan-
tages, and that’s why certain marriage-mongers
moot, as a desirable alliance, a union between the
slim, dark, only daughter of our minor vice-regal
house, and the fat, fair, only son of the dethroned
dynasty. Of course, the suggested bridegroom is
no longer en 'prince, but you can’t ‘ wash the balm
off an anointed king ’ —or vice-king, and though it
was young What’s-his-name’s Pa, not himself, that
got ‘anointed,’ still ‘balm’ is, I guess, hereditary
to a certain extent, and survives office —also to a
certain extent. However, whether the Clarke
and Madden clans are likely to be permanently
allied or not, a tempoiary connecting link already
exists in the person of long, lean Captain Vallange,
private-secretary to Gov. Clarke and son-in-law of
Judge Madden. When buxom Sylvia Madden
married young Yallange, her pa was at the zenith
of his glory. The wedding-feast was eaten at
Govt. House; ‘Victorian girls’ donated diamonds;
and society journals described th'e bridegroom as
lord of ancestral English acres, with a yearly
income of £6OOO. Evidently there’s something in
Australia after all, for, with these strong reasons
for liking Britain, the Vallanges have passed most
of their married life under the gleam of the kanga-
roo—‘himself’ turning his hand to such aide de-
camp and attache jobs as have presented them-
selves.
“The modernest of Yankee millionaires threaten
to rival their women folk in the magnificence of
their jewels. The great jewel theft that set New
York recently.by the ears' opens our eyes as to the
vanity of big brother Jonathan. In this instance
a valet robbed his master —one Mr. Thebaud —of
an ‘ eleven-carat, pear-shaped diamond worth
£4OOO, a rare pearl valued at £3OOO, and 275 scarf
pins.’ As the theft was effected during a visit of
Mr. and Mrs. Thebaud to some friends at White
Plains, it is evident that the whole 275 scarf pins
were on active service. Just imagine—27s scarf
pins ! Why the average man boasts one, and the
sprig of fashion, none. The cameos and pearls of
long ago are mostly now decorating the windows
of the sign of Three Balls.
“The absence of Suffragist Vida Goldstein is
much felt by the hard-working Public Service she-
clerks. These fair agitators are mostly of the
G.P.O. branch, and after 20 years’ service of eight
hours per day are only in receipt of £B4 a year.
Now that the bright and comely Vida is on
her way to Uncle Sam’s domain, there is no one
to captivate bachelor and other politicians and
beguile them to take up the cause of the toil-worn
sisterhood. The way the winsome leader of
Oman’s Rights used to lobby the Nice members
of the House of Reps., and appeal pathetically to
them from her corner in the gallery, with her
note-book in her hand, and a wistful look in her
eye, was a very influential way. Now, the petti-
coated quill-drivers of the G.P.O. are searching
for a like eloquent advocate—one to voice their
claim to a decent increment, and to the status of
the men clerks, whose work is but on a level with
their own.
A section of London is going to the dogs nightly
with great eagerness. These dogs are music-hall
animals, and do their novel “ turn ” like first-class
biped mummers. They are dressed up to repre-
sent a more or less happy family that is broken
up by the efforts of an outsider to establish a
manage & trois. There are the husband and wife
and the gay bachelor. Husband takes a tender
farewell of his wife and starts for the city. Then
the villain dog strolls round and taps at the bed-
room window. A s soon as it is opened he
scrambles in, and the audience sits waiting and
whispering—questioning the morality of teaching
dogs to behave like humans, and saying that their
owner deserves hydrophobia. Then the husband-
dog returns in a great hurry and, with a revolver,
shoots his faithless mate and false friend.
English papers are carefully pointing out that
the rose is to be the coronation flower, and “loyal
subjects must be careful to intermingle red roses
and white, King Edward being an equal representa-
tive of the Houses of York and Lancaster.” This
is another effort at keeping up the pretence that the
royal family of England is an English royal family
and not a made-in-Germany imported article.
Queen Victoria used to revel in talking of her
“martyred ancestor, Charles 1.,” and the best
way of ingratiating oneself with the irritable old
lady tvas to talk of her “Divine right ” to the
throne. But, as a matter of fact, since the days
of Elizabeth there hasn’t been a genuine. English
monarch of England. The Scotch monarchs who
followed her were, however, at least British,
except that they had a very large infusion of
French blood. Then" came Dutch William, and
another temporary reversion to the Scoto-
French line, followed by the German Georges—
Hanoverians who didn’t even talk English. Since
then the royalties of England have been through-
out pure German, and have always married pure
Germans, until Edward VII. took a Danish wife.
The only English royal blood in Great Britain
sports the bar sinister ; the German Kings of the
country, though they never stooped to an English
•wife, occasionally condescended to an English
mistress. j
Yours -affectionately,
AKENEHI.
The f criminal person who lives by exploit-
ing his neighbors in these modern days is a man
of method. He does not blunder around with a
crowbar at times when he may be disturbed, but,
when a likely house is spotted, he first finds out
how many people are living there, also their
names. Then he watches till"'it is Mary Jane’s
afternoon off and only one of the family is at
home—generally a woman. To her he sends a
telegram that the husband or somebody has met
with an accident, and she’s to come to the hospital
at onoe. Then the house is artistically ransacked.

Brough told his audience at Auckland (M.L.) the other
week that he and his wife would never, never see them
again. As the Broughs, up to then, had been enjoying an
enormously successful tour through Maoriland, their talk
or retirement from the stage sounds sincere, but they
have not yet farewelled in Australia. This country
owes it to the Broughs to send them away with a solid re-
tiring allowance.

• '1® n ey Agricultural Ground by the contingent a few
before its embarkation. A fifty-minutes sham-
hght took place. The din of battle was awful, and the ex-

Boers supposed to be wounded ; all greatly frightened ;
9?.P*’ u f, e d-” After the prisoner had sung “ Soldiers of
the Kmg,” a fat officer informed the reserved pavilion
that he was going to be shot, whether for bad singing or
reckless defence of his country didn’t transpire. Then
the whole army carefully went to sleep, and when the
sentry awoke he was just in time to jerk the old escaping
Boer back to the lines. Again the beefy officah sentenced
h irn in a loud voice to be shot. As he fell, apparently
smothered in gore, a Boer commando attacked, and in
the confusion the Boer got up and ran. De Wet had
again got away.
Shaw has managed to get his play, “ Mrs.
Warren’s Profession,” produced at a club theatre, after
the Lord Chamberlain had refused to license it, and all
London managers had declined to lend or let their stages.
The profession ”is what Kipling has called the Oldest
in the World, and is stated as returning 35 per cent,
profit on outlay ! Fanny Brough, in the name-part, has
the already much-quoted remark, that the only way for a
poor girl to get a decent living is to be good to some man
who can afford to be good to her. (“ Decent Living ”is
flood.) Also, she argues with her grown-up daughter,
who has other views: “Do you think your aunt and I
were such fools as to let other people trade on our good
looks by employing us as shop-girls, or barmaids, or
waitresses, when we could trade on them ourselves, and
get all the profits? Not likely.” Julius Knight was in
the cast. What a fate for the noble legs of Marcus
Superbus !
“ Melb.”: Williamson’s operatic concerts were patronised
by the Kirk, Goshen, and Ebenezer. Aminadab Sleek and
the porcine young Miss Aminadabs were in the balcony ;
Chadband was in the stalls, and Stiggins was a bobber. To
others the bouquet was somewhat withered.
While singing a duet with Philip Newbury at Lawrence
(M.L.), the other evening, Madame Emily Spada was
stung on the cheek by a bee. The lady made a wild
clutch at her face, and threw the bee heavily on the
floor, but proceeded with the duet without any noticeable
interruption. The average man under the same circum-
stances would have stopped dead in his warbling, said
thing's about the insect, and fled off the stage, with both
hands to his head, to look for whisky.
“ Melb.”: Last night I went to one of the busking
theatrical shows in a suburb. Overflowing audience,
mostly a clan of howling boys, at threepence. The leading
performers included several persons of long and honor-
able careers. The intellectual standard of the play was
far above that of any Melbourne representation on the
same night. With what conscientious care and skill the
leading lady and her associates went through their parts 1
How thorough was their technique ! Might not a place,
with regular two “ quids,” be found for such people in a
National Theatre of Instruction? They carry all Shake-
speare in their heads.
It is an amazing fact that ‘‘ lolanthe,” the most beauti-
fully humorous of the Gilbert and Sullivan operas, was
never revived in London until December last. First
produced at the Savoy on Nov. 9, ’B2; it ran for some
months, but although there have been revivals of “ Pina-
fore ” and the later operas, “ lolanthe ” was an abandoned
reef for 19 years. W. S. Gilbert superintended the re-
hearsals, and brought the dialogue up-to-date in places.
Strephon, complaining that although his fairy body is
out-and-out Tory, his mortal legs are a pair of confounded
Radicals, goes.on to say that one leg is “ actually inclined
to kick up its heel in the Pro-Boer interest.”
“ J.A.C.” : A correspondent, writing from London
(13/l/’O2), sends the following :—“ Miss Holroyd is in
Milan. She has made very great progress during the
time she has been there. She is studying histrionic art,
and will probably soon make, her debut—in Italy. Her
knowledge of Italian is now quite sufficient. I met
Florence Schmidt yesterday. She has really had great
success, is in the very front rank, and certainly might
have sent many more good reports of her doings if she
were made that way. After the first successful appear-
ance of a concert-singer in London, it is from the pro-
vinces that the engagements come pouring in, and about
these, of course, one hears nothing in Australia, unless
the singer chooses to send anything. Miss Schmidt is
singing twice this week at the Queen’s Hall here.

nowhere else see her name announced, either here or in
the country, and the concert announcements in the

dications of an artist’s pecuniary worth. Her voice, of
good quality, is as so many others that it could hardly
account for the tremendous trumpeting of her fame ;

appear to be no longer there—an A“ sung in ‘Good

They run in all shapes. The latest addition to Sydney’s
street entertainers is a clerical-looking elocutionist, who
recites with appropriate action at street corners and
holds out a clerical-looking hat for the casual copper.
He has a loud, rich voice that would stand to him if he
were to start a pew religion.
A rather superfluous new memoir of Barry Sullivan, by
one Sillard, may be summarised thus : His proper name
was Thomas Sullivan, and he added the Barry at man-
hood. Born 1821. First appearance at the Cork Theatre,
1837. Got to the Edinburgh Theatre Royal in 1841.
During one of his years there, as walking gentleman, the
company acted 132 plays in 150 nights. In 1847, Sullivan
was leading man at Liverpool at £5 a week. Advanced
to Manchester at £6 10s. Engaged for the London Hay-
market, in 1852, at J2lO a week. Irving played small
parts to him in an Edinburgh engagement in 1857.
Eighteen months in America advanced Sullivan’s prestige
before coming to Australia. Opened in Melb. in 1862.
Commenced management in 1863. Got £6O a night,
three nights a week, at Drury Lane in 1866. His pay at
Drury in 1868 was £75 a night. Lost £BOOO managing
the Holborn Theatre. Acted Richard 111. 60 nights
running at Drury Lane in 1876. After his last American
trip he refused £24,000 for 10 months there. In his last
engagements he occasionally cleared £l2OO a week at
first-class English provincial theatres.

The J.P. again: At Rotorua (M.L.) two
of the species fined a man £2 for cruelly flogging
five horses; and for lighting a fire under them
and burning their bodies horribly, which the ac-
cused admitted doing, they didn’t fine him any-
thing—only ordered him to come up for sentence
when called upon. Yet, though cruelty is so
lightly regarded in Maoriland, Premier Seddon
and his kind are shocked beyond words at the
suggestion that any Maoriland soldier could
possibly behave otherwise than as a tender Chris-
tian in S’Africa.


Three Years
Liver Trouble.
CLEMENTS TONIC SOLELY RESPONSIBLE FOR CURE
The Case of
Mrs. KATHERINE GARLAND.
(by our special reporter.)
“ Your visit has come as a pleasant
eurprise to me,” was the remark with which
Mrs. Garland, of No. 4 Denham-street,
Surry Hills, greeted the reporter from this
journal, as it saves me the trouble of
writing to you about my bad attack of liver
complaint, from which I suffered terribly.
I am a very poor correspondent at the best
of times, preferring like most members of
my sex, to receive letters rather than write
them ; but I am so conscious of a deep
obligation to Clements Tonic for restoring
me to health that I made up my mind to
write expressing my gratitude, and as you
have obviated that duty I will gladly give
the fullest details.
“ How long did your liver trouble afflict
you ? ” asked the reporter.
“ For fully three years, and I hope it will
never come on again. Do you know, I
believe I would rather be dead than
continue like I was then. The first sign of
my failing health was an unaccountable
fastidiousness regarding my food. I some-
times felt like eating a little, and as soon as
it was before me I should turn right against
it, and do nothing but mince and turn it
over, then finally leave it alone. There
was always a sickly, sour taste in my mouth,
my tongue being so dry and rough,
particularly in the morning, and I was often
seized with a fit of vomiting, which was
invariably followed by the act of belching
distasteful gases from the stomach. The
operation of ridding myself of the bile and
Bour wind from the stomach caused me no
end of discomfort and pain, and blinding
headaches were frequently experienced
after I had been retching. Heavy pains in
the shoulders, and sometimes in the chest,
often paid me more attention than was
agreeable, as they made me form the
opinion that I was slowly falling into the
clutches of some dreadful disease. Indeed,
this idea was made all the more likely by
the yellowish hue of my skin, and my eyes
became a dirty green colour. ., Liver spots
also came out on my chest. Besides the.
pains in the chest and shoulders, I would
have severe attacks in the side, and they
were so excessively tormenting that I had
to apply hot fomentations, but they only
gave temporary relief, and made the next
attack more acute. It was next door to
impossible to ever get a brief respite from
my many ailments, for I could scarcely ever
get to sleep, and the little repose that did
occasion a ly come to me was marked by
unpleasant dreams and restlessness. On
awakening it required a great stretch of
imagination to believe I had slept at all,
so weary and fatigued would I feel. My
limbs ached with sheer weakness, and I’m
sure I was an object deserving compassion
when crawling about the house in my vain
efforts to do a little housework, only to sit
down quite exhausted and fret for hours.
When I wanted to stand up I had to do so
carefully and steadily, otherwise a fainting
giddiness would take charge of me, and my
head would whirl round like a spinning-
wheel. These fainting sensations left me
dreadfully depressed and low-spirited, and
I sat or lay brooding over my misfortunes
till I got very melancholy. There was not
an ounce of flesh on my bones. So weak and
debilitated had I become that I was almost
afraid to look in the glass.”
“You look remarkably well now, how-
ever, Mrs. Garland.”
“Yes, thank God, 1 am like I used to be
before my three years’ sickness. But you
have not learned all my troubles. The
worst and most terrifying thing has yet to
be described. I have not yet mentioned the
anxiety my heart used to cause me. You
do not think I look like a woman whoso
heart) is diseased, do you ? No; I’m sure
I don’t. But I was almost worried to death
with that thought when I was so ill. My
heart used to start suddenly, beating with
such violence that I dreaded it stopping or
easing off, in case it would stop for ever.
But it would quieten down after nearly
scaring the life out of me, only to start
again with renewed rapidity. I have since
learned that palpitation of the heart occurs
in liver jlisease cases when they are very
serious, bo mine must have been a very bad
case.”
“And what treatment did yon receive
from your doctor ? ”
“ Oh, the usual—medicines and pills, and
I don’t mind telling you that the liquids
they termed medicines did me no end of
injury. Why, I used to loathe the thought
of swallowing it; the smell was offensive
and the taste was disgusting. But I
persevered with them for three years all to
no purpose. Then a friend told me that
Clements Tonic was the best thing for me to
take, which I did, and you can see the
result for yourself. Clements Tonic seemed
a little bitter at first, but I could soon drink
it like water, without any reluctance what-
ever. But even if it had been gall I would
have drank it, because it brought about my
convalescence in no time. I told my doctor
I would rather have Clements Tonic than all
his stuff, as it was doing me good, and his
physic had completely failed. He replied :
‘ Well, I’ll not make much out of you now.’
And he spoke truthfully, for I can be
independent of all doctors now. I rapidly
experienced ease from pains in the stomach,
chest and shoulders, and the easing of pains
in the head brought delightful, soothing
sleep. My appetite came back, and I- did
not notice any discomfort after eating. The
liver spots were eradicated, and my skin
recovered its usual colour. No more fears
of heart disease worried me, as the palpit-
ation disappeared with the other disagree-
able symptoms. To he as brief as possible,
I must candidly admit that C ements Tonic
is solely responsible for my cure. When my
son-in-law was up in the mountains he wrote
saying he wa»*ick, and I sent him a couple
of bottles of Clements Tonic to Katoomba.
He subsequently thanked me, stating that
the medicine I had forwarded had cured
him. One of my daughters has been under
treatment during the past twelve months
for asthmatical bronchitis (another doctor
says it is heart disease she has got). They
have not done her the least good with their
physic, so lately she has been taking
Clements Tonic. She has had three bottles
up to now, and she is making good progress
in the right direction. I believe Clements
Tonic will make a woman of her again. My
faith in it is without limit, and I am so
grateful for its effect upon myself and
relatives that you can publish my testimony
as much as you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I Katiibrinb Garland, of No. 4 Denhatt-street,
Surry Hills, Sydney, in the Colony of New South
Wales, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consisting of
five folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
five, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
aocount of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and'passed in the ninth year of the reign of ner
present Majesty, intituled “ An Act for the more
effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken
and made in the various Departments of the Govern-
ment of New South Wales, and to substitute Declara-
tions in lieu thereof, and for the suppression of
voluntary and Extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”

TOO ILL TO LEAVE HIS BED.
A COMPLETE CURE.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir,—l an< writing to tell you
about the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my ease, About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
my case to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.
Westminster Bridge-road, S.E., London.

A Seven Years’ Case.
EXPECTORATING BLOOD AND MATTER.
COMPLETELY CURED.
“ Mr. W. G. Hearne.
“Dear Sir, —Your medicine has cured me of bronchitis
and asthma, from which I suffered for upwards of seven
years, during which period I was scarcely ever free from
coughs, and frequently the difficulty of breathing was so
distressing that for nights in succession I had to sit up.
I write you this acknowledgment from a sense of duty, as
in my case every other treatment had failed. For a year
previous I had been getting very much worse, and at the
time I obtained your medicine I was confined to bed
suffering from a most violent cough, expectorating blood
and matter, and apparently beyond hope of recovery.
The first dose of the medicine gave me welcome relief,
and I steadily improved as I continued the treatment,
until I became, as I am now, quite well. —Yours sincerely,
H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney."

‘ H.F.” : Isn’t it time that pictured angels
were drawn up-to-date ? Anatomically the present
floating girl laoks muscles to move her wings.
The arm is the natural wing-foundation. Wings
and arms separated are a physiological monstro-
sity. Then the wings should be four times present
size, with a proportionate muscular increase of
the shoulders. Also, the pictured angel has no
tail; even if it could start in flight it would flop
to earth like a capsized kite. This is dreadful to
think of when you want to be devout. Again,
unless it sends its clothes to the wash, a self-
respecting angel needs a beak to preen its feathers,
and claws to hold tight to the fence when the wind
blows, or how can it finish its yarn with the
cherubim ?
London Morning Post critic gathers from
Tlios. Hardy’s lamentable book of pomes that
“ poetry has been with Mr. Hardy rather an
amusement than a passion.” Which reminds one
of the fable of the bad pelting boys and the help-
less suffering frogs. It was sport for Hardy, but
agony for trie public whom he smote with, say,
‘‘ Th.e Song of the Soldiers’ Wives,” an up-to-date
inspiration of S’African interest —
And now you are nearing home again,
Dears, home again ;
No more, may be, to roam again
As in that bygone time,
Which took you far away from us
To stay from us ;
Dawn, hold not long the day from us
But quicken it to prime !
And to make the matter worse the dears are no
nearer home again than they were at the time of
the Mafeking Drunk. The Morning Post man
recognises this melancholy fact when he writes—
The series of poems ends with one on “ The Battle-god,”
which has also a note of hope :
Let men rejoice, let men deplore,
The lurid Deity of heretofore
Succumbs to one of saner nod ;
The Battle-god is god no more.
Note of hope ”is good. “ Hope ” may be oata-
jOgued in this connection as a Hardy annual.

A General Muster.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ SAY you know nothittg about it, and you’ll come
k good deal nearer the truth.”
u Wasn’t I here ? ”
“You Was dead drunk At Hogan’s. Didn’t see
the daylight for a month, and you know it as well
As I do.”
“ All right, then,” concluded Dick.
But old George Brown, the overseer, spoke with
authority before he left the hut. “It would be a
great deal better for yourself, Dick, if you stuck
to what you do know something about.”
Through the silence that followed the remark
came old Dad’s voice : “I raked both their graves
this morning,”
“ And I see ’em filled,” said Dick.
“You heard about it,” the cook retorted.
“ How could any man see, who was at the end of
A month’s spree and a two years’ cheque ? ”
“ The old man only died ten years ago,” said
Hick.” “ I buried him, Dad ! ”
“ What Was it all about ? ” asked the drover’s
man.
Dick began : “It was this way: Old Billy col-
lared young Billy’s gin.”
“She was a lady woman,” corrected Dad.
, “ She was all that, and young Bill was a swell.
The old man sent him to England to make him a
swell, and the young missus, she came out to
be his wife.”
“I raked her grave, too,” said Dad.
“Young Bill went away through America, but
got back time o’ the last general muster, an’ rode
up to the station unsuspected. She was there,
but old Billy was away, W hen Old Bill come back,
what he found didn’t suit him. He and young
Billy went out together, and he come back by his-
self, and, after that, he saw blue devils till he
died.”
“I reckon, then, George Brown Was right,”
said the cook.
“ Right! How ? ”
“You knows nothin’ about it. Tell us the
yarn, Dad.”
Dick cursed. Dad smoked for a while, and at
length spoke. ~
“I see her the first day she come, in the buggy,
alongside the old man. She was some kind of a
relation, and she was to marry young Billy. Her
face was the color of a ripe quondong, and when
she laughed you’d think it was a school o’ bell-
birds. And old Billy hadn’t buried the old missus
then more ’n two year. I rakes her grave reg’lar.
I was in the garden then, and the young missus,
she’d be planting half her time, vi’lets mostly.
I’ve got vi’lets on her now. Says she to me one
day : ‘ Will won’t be out for a couple o’ years,
Dad; his father’s sent him to Texas to learn cow-
boy work.’ And a day or two later: ‘ How many
acres is in the run, an’ how many sheep do it run,
an* is it all belonging to Mr. Johnston ? ’
“ Then thinks I, ‘ Young Billy would have
been better here.’ But he would have been better
away, better away.”
“ It was ter be ! ” observed the cook.
“ Yes, it was ter be, mebbe. A letter that
young Billy wrote missed, for old Billy didn’t box
his business. But, anyhow, the general muster
come on, and there was a parson at the station a
week afore, an’ stayed for a week, an’ he married
old Billy to young Billy’s girl. (You never see
a general muster, none o’ you, ’cept Dick, an’ he
was always drunk). _ Seven stations used to drive
on to Tommy’s Plain, and every squatter had his
drovers, and tailers, an’ two or three fightin’ men.
The station blacks used ter muster too, but that
year they was all scared because two lots of
buckeeners was down from the hills.’’
“ What’s buckeeners ? ” asked the drover’s man.
“ Buckeeners is wild blacks as comes down on
the station blacks. Their dart is their kidney fat.
Some says they eats it, and others that they
’noints theirselves with it. I only knows they
goes for it, an’ they were about the muster. I see
their fire one night, an’ George Brown saw them
the day before. Six of em’ and no gins. George
told King Billy at the station an’ give it out at
the muster, and we reckoned to give ’em a run by-
and-bye. The muster went on right enough.
The clear-skins was cut out and divided, and then
there was the crooked ones, the faked brands and
the doubtfuls. The worst fightin’ was always
about them. There had been a dozen sets-to may-
be, and both Billy’s fightin’ men was dog-tired,
when a fat worker was run out. I knowed him
for one of ours that got away a year before, but
McLintock’s men swore to their brand. I was
pulling off my shirt when somebody sees dust
down the river. Our station was up-river. We
waits, till who rides up but young Billy. An’ he
was a toff. Paddy Neylor was drivin’ him up
head of the mail. When old Billy spotted him I
knowed things was crooked. Stead o’ lighting up
his face turned dark as thunder. But the fight
was off anyhow, and, when young Billy come
down among us shakin’ hands here and there,
they all got good-humored about the cattle, and
Tom Ryan and I, who was to a’ fought, drunk a
bottle together by the fire.
“ Towards morning I got up and put the logs
together, then took a turn out to the edge of the
timber to listen for the horses’ bells. It was over
50 mile to our home-station, and we were goin’ to
make an early start. I was lookin’ out when day
would break, and along comes young Billy.
“‘Martin,’ says he, ‘I come on you sudden
like.’
“ ‘ You did ! ’ says I.
“ ‘The governor didn’t expect me for a year or
two yet,’ he goes on, ‘but I got full of Texas.’
And then he begins to ask about the young
missus. Was she well, an’ was she light-hearted,
and was she lookin’ out for him ? I felt scared
while he talked, for I could feel things crooked ;
but, ‘ fresh as river water,’ says I, ‘an’ singin’ like
a magpie, and not expectin’ a sight of you for a
year to come.’
“ ‘ Gad, she will be surprised, ’ he says, and
then, ‘ Martin, d’ye think you could get hold of a
horse without runnin’ up the lot ? ’ I knew what
he meant. In less than half-an-hour I had two
horses in and was fixin’ one when out of the
tent comes old Billy, cussin’ as he comes.
“Young Billy was riggin’hisself out back o’
the buggy, an’ I kept away, for there was blood
behind the old man’s eye. Soon the son showed
up: ‘What the hell do you want to sneak out
o’the camp this fashion for?’ says the old man,
and young Billy pulls off his hat polite as a prince,
and, says he: '
“ ‘ I wanted to give ’em a surprise at the home-
station.’
“‘Let your surprise wait, then,’ said the old
man ; ‘ you gave us enough o’ that yesterday.’
“ Young Billy said no word, but went across to
the fern, an’ I hitched up the hosses.
“ We got away an hour after daybreak, the boss
drivin’ the buggy, and I bringing along the stock
horse, the best bit o’ stuff on the river. Young Billy
was ridin’ a young thing and showin’ off cowboy
tricks as we went along. It might have been
eleven When We camped a spell, ahd just as the
pots was boilin’ young Billy Whips acrbss a stock-
horse and away. * Sti long, dad, ’ says he; 111
tell ’em you’ll be in by sundown. ’
“He made for the outside track —that was ten
mile shorter than ours, if you kept it.
“Old Billy gets up and lets out a roar, then
walks up and down a spell, and in the end turns
round to us. * That damned young fool will get
bushed and lost. The thing he’s ridin’ was bred
100 miles down the river and knows no more o
the river than he does. Put my saddle on Warri-
gal! I’ll follow him, an’ you can take the buggy
“ Two minutes after he was up, and the look on
his face I’ll never forget. I saw another man
look the same once. That was Morgan at Brown s
Plains when he rode back with the boy he’d shot
across his saddle. I was there.”
“Damn Morgan!” said the drovers man;
“ What happened ? ”
“We got along to the station all right, and the
young missus is walking up and down the veran-
dah. She calls to me as I goes towards the gar-
den, and asks for the boss. Then I had to do my
pitch, and as soon as young Billy comes into it
she flings up her arms with a screech.. ‘An
they’ve gone together? You fools, you idiots!
she says. Maybe I looked fool and idiot enough,
for it knocked me stiff. But, all of a sudden, I
sights the wedding-ring on her finger, and then
remembers old Billy’s look. Nothin’ would do
her but all hands out and search. George Brown,
he was a young fellow then, tried to talk sense,
but she hunted him —hunted us all till we went
out across the house paddock. Then, says George
Brown, ‘ We may as well camp till he comes in,
or she comes out of her tantrums.’
“We watched the track till sundown, and then
George begun to get uneasy. ‘ The young fellow
may have got mixed up with the lignum and that
mallee patch,’ said he. ‘You go back to the
station, Martin ; we’ll spread out and ride on a
bit.’ ,
“ I went back, and she lashed out at me with
her tongue ; and night come, and she. lit all the
lamps and ramped up and down outside. But,
whenever one of the hands come in, she’d rush to
her own room an’ lock the door, and sing out to
the girls to ask who it was.
“ They all come in one by one, George Brown
last. He was goin’ up to her when he heard the
rattle of hoofs along the track, and up rides the
boss without his hat or whip, and the mare spurred
from girth to flank.
“‘Has he come in?’ he sings out, and we all
starts. He goes right inside, and George Brown
follows. And when George comes out there’s a
queer look on him. He tells us the boss never got
up with his son an’ lost the tracks about dark.
Then, as he was headin’ for home, he struck the
buckeening blacks, an’ his horse bolted and carried
him to the devil.
“All hands turned out with guns and whips.
We were goin’ to light fires right across to the
river to scare the blacks and attract young
Billy.
“Young Billy, aye! ’Twas three days before
we found him, head down in the lignum with his
skull smashed.
“ The buckeeners,” said the drover’s man.
“So you says, and so George Brown says, and
so some folk on the river said,” answered old Dad,
solemnly.
“ What should they say, anyhow ? ”
“Just so, what else? What did she say when
she saw old Billy’s face as he came in? For a
week after George Brown and he watched her;
wouldn’t let the < women-folk come near her for
fear they’d hear" her raving. And what did he
say, year after year, half in the horrors and half
out ? And why did they send t’other boy away to
school an’ to college an’ never let him anigh the
station for 10 years ? And why did she take to
drink and run hini neck and neck for the grave in
the garden? And why is it George Brown’s boss
as long as he lives ? I seen her face when I told her
the boy was comin,’ and his’n when he went on to
his tracks, and I huried him and her, and rakes
the graves of all of ’em regular.”
According to Colonel Lyster, of the
“ Federal ” contingent, he can’t understand why
that advance-pay grievance should have such
prominence. “Officers,” he says, “are supposed
to look after the men’s interests, and settle any
differences that may arise.” Is that all Col. Lyster
knows ? Officers are bound to forward any com-
plaints that may be made, even against them-
selves. There are questions that no regimental
officer can settle, and when one of them falls over
a snag of that sort, he is apt to get angry and run
in the man who persists in laying it in his way.
Whatever the officer is “ supposed ” to do, the
contingenter doesn’t trust him, and he is probably
right. These officers have been chosen by the
Barton Government to stave off troublesome
questions till the men are safely landed in S’Africa.
What happens afterwards doesn’t matter.
As for the alleged “mistake” in the matter of
the pay, it’s the sort of mistake that mean and
shuffling Australian Governments generally do
make in dealing with their contingents. The
British regulation applies to this contingent, but
the Barton crowd thought fit to cut down the
advance from 30 days’ to 10 days’ pay. All
very well to vapour at “patriotic” meetings
about the “empire,” but it would be more pat-
riotic to abstain from diddling the empire’s soldiers
out of their wages.
A church with as interesting a history as
that of the Hawkesbury Ebenezer, pilgrimmed to
the other day, is the iron erection for many years
described as old St. Stephen’s, Sydney. It has
now trekked to Rookwood Asylum, where it is the
central figure in the city of 1100 decayed old men.
It was designed early in the last century by the
followers of Knox and co. in Scotland, and
there was keen competition as to its carriage to
N.S.W. The job was finally divided among
several vessels. The church was brought out
as ballast—a bit of a church being considered
in these days rather a lucky thing to have on
board. Macquarie-st. knew it for many years
both as a place of worship and also as the lending
branch of the State Library. The overflow from
St. James’s used to make for it on Sabbath morn-
ings, and squash the sect to which it properly
belonged up against the wall. So a resolution
was passed that “this kirk was brought to the
colony for the sole use of United Presbyterians,
at much expense and considerable inconvenience,
and it is requested that they will assemble before
11 o’clock, when the church will be thrown open
to the public.” This was in the days when one
could throw a stone without hitting a church at
every street-corner. St. Stephen’s now acts as
church and library to the derelict veterans. All
denominations are allowed to use it in turn, and
the echo of “ Ave Maria” chases the echo of
the “ Old Hundredth ” round the walls, and would
doubtless disparage the same severely, if it could
only catch up. And during the week the toothless
veteran returns “ Tom Jones ” and vainly applies
for “ Maria Monk,” and old United Presbyterian
ghosts and memories groan bitterly at the dese-
cration.

Dawnward?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
That reddish veil which o’er the face
Of night-hag East is drawn—
Flames new Disaster for the race,
Or can it be the dawn ?
That rumbling sound horizonward 1
What destinies are there ?
Do organed Hopes triumphant chord,
Or thunders roar ‘ ‘ Despair ” ?
What gifts ate those the clouds release
As far ahead they scud ?
Are they the genial rains of Peace*
Or deluges bf blobd ?
tlur niotley masses struggle slow
’Mid wilderness, through sands;
t)ur flags with fetish watchwords glow
Abdve the gloomy bands.
Three watchwords ! Will they glorify*
Or weave us fates mbre stark ?
Lead dawn Ward from this lowering sky*
Or downward to the dark ?
Will “Freedom ” over Athens’ scrolls
Our greater glory carve ?
Or prove mere choice to sell our souls
To Mammon or—to starve ?
Content with Freedom’s forms, will wa
Real tyranny caress,
Through sybaritic apathy,
Or mad forgetfulness ?
“ Equality ! ” Will each a king
Become, a seer, a sage ?
Or will it ruthless all men fling
In cosmic helotage ?
Will crucibles, wherein, tho’ great
With primal vice, we pour
Equalities, precipitate
Napoleons—as before?
While heedless on our masses move,
Their sad-eyed mystics see
On rushing Cloudland’s stage above
Dark hints of what may be.
Palladium and Shibboleth
Pose on each misty dome :
Red Crisis’ tableaux blotch with death
Smug Order’s monochrome.
Race-ogres here on vulture-cloud,
And there race-fathers hie;
And Then and Now and Will-Bc crowd
The pantomimic sky.
Prophetic ’mid the whirlwind flow
These cryptic figures steal:
Are they to be for further woe,
Or may they be for weal ?
V.
Will turbaned Shem, revived, through sweet
White women filtered long,
With sober scowl triumphant meet
The drunken Western throng ?
Will Ham, acquit of servile strain,
Of art and craft compact,
A loathing Europe’s pallor stain—
Democracy in fact ?
Will Japhet still his brothers lead
Unto the shambled tryst,
With tentacles of trading greed
And drivel of his Christ ?
Will Gog, awake, his Huns outpour
At Empire-breaking time,
To sluice aWay our fariie and lore;
Our features and —but crime ?
Scrolls, written “ Debt,” and “Wanton Wat,’*
And “ Sterile Lbve,” flare high ;
Are these bur “ Mene ! Mene ! ” or
Illusions bf the sky ?
“ Majority ! ” Divorced from wise;
Sad Conscience, will he prowl
Through tender, human heresies
With Torquemadan scowl ?
And “ Comfort! ” Will her siren song
To narcotising shades
Seduce our veterans, while Wrong
Our weaker frontiers raids ?
Will “ Sport ” educe a virile pith,
Our pulses teach to throb ?
Or weary earth re-saddle with
A Nika-riot mob ?
Will centre-seeking “Culture” hold
Tangential Passion’s bolt ?
Yield orbits of an Age of Gold,
Or comets of Revolt ?
Yet, foodless oft and homeless, we,
Not hopeless, loveless, plod—
Whither ? To Failure’s midnight sea?
Or dawnward ? Ay, to God ?
DANTON.
A M.L. paper gently chides its ignorant
contemporary for marrying (by cable) a Chinese
General’s daughter to the Chinese province of
Yangtse, which the hidebound one mistook for a
man, and converting the German Emperor’s steam
yacht Hohenzollern into a prince who was to
accompany his brother to America. The other
rag gets back by saying it never called ChaUncey
Depew a Frenchman, anyway.
Sergeant-Major Heffernan, who has just
returned from the Transvaal, in an interview
published in Sydney Telegraph, explains the
cattle captures in South Africa thus “ They
are taker? me day and probably lost the next
night, an 1 1; m recaptured again, this interesting
proceed, lg continuing indefinitely.” Does the
same thing happen with the prisoners, and with
the dead or alleged dead ?
A “Star” Performance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We had ceased to wonder how Calligan became
possessed of so many “ flyers,” but when Star
beat Concertina for the Stony Crossing Bridle by
about four lengths, our curiosity revived. He was
£\ good sort, though, in a way, was the proprietor
of the Quart Pot Arms, for he used to shout for
all hands after every race he won. Then he would
drink himself hopeless for two or three weeks on
end.
His daughter, Tom M'Dermid’s sweetheart.
Would at these times Clear out, and leave him to
the tender mercies of the big black-gin cook. She
bated racing, and used to remonstrate tearfully
With Calligan every time a badly-written and
much-interlined programme was tacked up in the
bar. Mrs. Calligan had died years before.
After the Bridle was won, and the shouting was
bver, the horse rubbed down and put away, Miss
Calligan went to her father and said : “Dad, I’m
going.” Old Dan stared at her.
“ Goin’ where ? ” he asked, thickly.
“I’m going over to Mrs. M'Dermid’s,” said
Sara; “an’ —an’ I’m going to marry Tom, Whether
you like it or riot! ”
“ You’re what? You're what ?” be yelled,
getting up from the gin-case bn which he had
been sitting. “D’ yer mean t’say yer gdiri’ tb
marry that cow ? ”
“ I do,” said Sara, tossing her head ; “ and he’s
not a cow.” With that she slammed tbe side-door
and galloped across to Mrs. Mac’s with her bundle
under one arm.
m * • »
Galligan hated Tom because Tom’s father had
once been instrumental in getting him (Calligan)
arrested for illegally branding; and although
he was let off with a caution, he never re-
garded the whole M ‘Dermid family with anything
but the deepest hatred. This was a pity, because
Tom was a real good fellow. As a matter of fact,
Star rightly belonged to him. He missed him
one day, and, although he hunted through all the
runs within thirty miles or so, failed to find him.
Calligan had “planted” him, and, being a fair
bush “vet,” had painted out two of the three
white feet; and only a star remained of what had
been a crooked blaze. Tom’s brand was an easy
one to fake, and in less than a month the colt was
broken in.
That was how Calligan came to win the Bridle.
• • • •
It was the day of the annual Stony Crossing
races. Star was favorite for the “big” money
(£lO, and a pair of spurs), though Tom M'Dermid’s
mare was backed considerably. Calligan was
anxious. From time to time he cast his eye
heavenwards, muttering imprecations against
Heaven for banking up black clouds on a race day.
The bell rang, and the horses went to the post. A
rain-drop plashed on Calligan’s hand. He cursed
audibly.
* * * *
“ They’re off ! ” The rain drops quickened.
The distance was a mile and a half. As they
thundered past the “ stand ” for the first time, the
rain pelted down in earnest. “Look at Dan!”
said somebody. Calligan was jumping on his
hat, and cursing like a madman. They thought
it was the rum.
Half a mile from home Star was leading. They
knew it was Star by the way the nigger on him
eat. The pace was slow, for the ground was now
a quagmire. When they wheeled round the
dump a quarter-mile from the tree which did
duty as a winning-post, most of the spectators
were mystified and asked unanimously :
“What bald-faced horse is in the lead, any-
how ? ” Nobody seemed to know, as no bald-faced
horse had started. But two at least knew—Calli-
gan and Tom, and both were missing. The bald-
faced horse won, and he had Star’s jockey on.
That much the crowd could see as the nags were
flogged past the post, covered with slush. Star
went fully 150 yards further than the rest before
he pulled up. When he did, the black boy on him
was surprised to find Calligan alongside, glaring
like a fiend. “ Don’t weigh in, ”he said hurriedly ;
“ cut straight home now, or I’ll kill yer ! Quick ! ”
and he raised his heavy crop.
The boy rode off swiftly through the bushes.
A good many of the young men there had
known Star before he was stolen, and Calligan
knew it. He cantered up to the excited crowd,
and called out—“ It’s orright, boys. I’ve sent the
’orse ’ome. There’s a bit of a mistake. Give the
prize to Mr. M'Dermid’s mare. She won it right
enough.”
“ Where’s Star ? ” asked someone.
A shout of laughter drowned his reply as he
turned his horse’s head for his pub. Then a few
fights resulted over the beta—and various other
things.
Tom Was Walking Star about when Calligan
to'de up, his face very white. “G’day, Tom,” he
said, awkwardly ; then, sidling up to’ the young
man, half-Whispered; “ Are yet gain’ ter say
anythin’, Tom?”
Tom stopped. “No,” he laughed.
Old Dan mopped his brow, cleared his throat,
and gurgled, “Gi’me yer hand, me boy. Wud
yer like Sara straight-away, Tom, with a bit of an
interest in the pub, eh, me boy ? ”
“My oath, Mr. Calligan,” said Tom. They
shook hands heartily. Then there was a short
silence. Mr. Calligan broke it by remarking,
awkwardly, “S’pose yer’ll ride the’orse back to
your place. What’ll yer ’ave t’ drink ? ”
S. C. O. JESSOP.
“Jimpan”: Saw this legend painted on
the back gate of a pretty Sydney suburban home:
No empty bottles for sale here. We gave up drinking
on New Year’s Day.
Underneath some disappointed bottle-collector
had scribbled— “ My oath !—about time you did,
too ! ”
Pretty well every local or other Govern-
ment that gives away any “ rights ” sooner or
later has trouble with the concessionaires—mostly
sooner. Bendigo (Vic.) is the. latest instance.
Bendigo and Eaglehawk, whose two-score coun-
cillors found themselves incompetent to run about
four miles of tramway and electrically light their
city, lately < decided to hand the concession over to
the Victorian Electric Supply Co., apparently a
London syndicate. The work proceeded for about
a week when the syndicate discovered that a single
track was good enough for Bendigo; and ‘ it
didn’t want too large a capitalisation at the
outset”; and if the councils didn’t agree there
would be a deadlock. The councils had to agree.
That’s only the start. The Bulletin’s prophetic
eye sees more requests in the near future.
